Eight Possible Forms of the Muta'allaq
Al-Sawi enumerates eight possible grammatical forms for the related term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight possible grammatical forms of the related term (muta'allaq) in the basmalah and why it can be a verb or certain derived nouns.

Core Idea
The muta'allaq can take eight forms based on three binary choices.

In this passage, al-Sawi builds on the commentator's discussion of the basmalah's grammatical structure. He explains that the term to which the basmalah relates (muta'allaq) is not limited to a verb; it can also be a noun from a specific set. By combining three binary categories—verb/noun, specific/general, and before/after—he arrives at eight possible forms. This lesson unpacks that classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice that al-Sawi uses a very systematic enumeration style.
2
Identify the three binary choices
The passage states that the muta'allaq may be a verb or a noun, specific or general, and placed before or after. Understand each of these three independent categories.
These combine to give eight possibilities (2 x 2 x 2).
3
Understand the five noun types
When the muta'allaq is a noun, it must be one of five types that operate like verbs: active participle, passive participle, resembling adjective, verbal noun (masdar), and noun of verbal noun (ism al-masdar).
The passage lists them after mentioning the first form (verb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإنما قدرنا المتعلق فعلاً ... إلخ) : اعلم : أنَّ المقرر أنَّه يجوز أن يكون المتعلَّق فعلاً أو اسماً ، وعلى كلّ خاصاً أو عاماً، أو على كلِّ مقدَّماً أو مؤخّراً ، فالحاصل ثمانية أوجه : الأولى منها : ما قاله الشَّارح: لأنَّ الأصل في العمل للأفعال أي وما عمل من الأسماء؛ كاسم الفاعل واسم المفعول والصفة المشبَّهة والمصدر، واسم المصدر؛
His saying: 'And we only specified the related term (al-muta'allaq) as a verb... etc.' Know that it is established that the related term may be a verb or a noun, and in each case specific or general, and in each case placed before or after. So the total is eight aspects. The first of them is what the commentator said: 'Because the principle in grammatical operation is for verbs, that is, also what operates from nouns, such as the active participle, the passive participle, the resembling adjective, the verbal noun (masdar), and the noun of verbal noun (ism al-masdar).'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical classification. It analyses the 'muta'allaq' (the term to which the basmalah relates). It states that the muta'allaq can be a verb or a noun, specific or general, and placed before or after, yielding eight combinations. The first combination is that it is a verb, because the principle in grammatical operation ('amal) is for verbs, and also for certain derived nouns that operate like verbs. It lists five such nouns: active participle, passive participle, resembling adjective, masdar, and ism al-masdar.

Key Terms
متعلق
فعل
اسم
اسم الفاعل
اسم المفعول
الصفة المشبهة
مصدر
اسم المصدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the muta'allaq must be a verb.
Correction: The muta'allaq can also be a noun from a specific set of five types that operate like verbs.

Checkpoint
How many possible forms does the related term (muta'allaq) in the basmalah have?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The muta'allaq in the basmalah has eight possible grammatical forms based on three binary choices, and it can be a verb or one of five types of derived nouns.

Grammatical Analysis of the Basmala's Muta'allaq
Al-Sawi enumerates the eight grammatical possibilities for the muta'allaq and defends the choice of a specific verb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight grammatical possibilities for the muta'allaq of the basmala and why the commentator chooses a specific verb, delayed.

Core Idea
The muta'allaq must be a specific verb, delayed, to convey restriction and blessing.

Al-Sawi begins by establishing the core grammatical analysis of the basmala. He enumerates eight possible forms for the muta'allaq (the element the preposition relates to) and then argues for a particular choice based on linguistic and theological grounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the eight possibilities
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the enumeration of eight possibilities for the muta'allaq: verb/noun, specific/general, preceded/delayed.
The passage only explicitly mentions the first possibility (verb, specific, delayed) but sets up the framework.
2
Understand the rationale for choosing a verb
Notice the argument that verbs are the primary grammatical actors, and estimating a noun would require several implied words, making it less concise.
The chosen verb 'u'allif' is concise and avoids implicit omissions.
3
Realize why the verb must be specific and delayed
Understand that specificity ties the blessing to the particular action, while delay indicates that the blessing is restricted to Allah's name, countering polytheist practices.
This sets up the discussion on qasr in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإنما قدرنا المتعلق فعلاً ... إلخ) : اعلم : أنَّ المقرر أنَّه يجوز أن يكون المتعلَّق فعلاً أو اسماً ، وعلى كلّ خاصاً أو عاماً، أو على كلِّ مقدَّماً أو مؤخّراً ، فالحاصل ثمانية أوجه : الأولى منها : ما قاله الشَّارح: لأنَّ الأصل في العمل للأفعال أي وما عمل من الأسماء؛ كاسم الفاعل واسم المفعول والصفة المشبَّهة والمصدر، واسم المصدر؛ = حسبلة زادها بعض العلما والندب يأتي بعد فرض علما وإن قسا القلب من الأغيار قاذكر حتى لا يبقى إلا الباري (۱) الشاطبية (ص١). ومتأخّراً؛ لأن تقديم المعمول يفيد الاختصاص، وخاصّاً؛ لأنَّ كلَّ شارع في شيء ينبغي له أن يقدر ما جعلت البسملة مبدأ له، ولإفادة حصول البركة لجميع أجزاء الفعل. فهو بطريق الحمل على الفعل، ولما في تقدير الاسم من زيادة الإضمار؛ لأَنَّ المحذوف حينئذ عدة كلمات : المضاف والمضاف إليه، ومتعلق الجار والمجرور ؛ بخلاف أؤلف ؛ فإنَّه مع فاعله المستقر فيه كلمتان. قوله: (ومتأخّراً ) : أي : عن البسملة؛ لأنَّ تقديم المعمول يفيد الاختصاص) : أي : يفيد قصر التبرك في التأليف على اسمه تعالى؛ فالباء داخلة على المقصور عليه؛ لأنَّ المشركين كانوا يبدؤون بأسماء آلهتهم، فيقولون : باسم اللات والعزى ؛ تبرُّكاً لا اختصاصاً، لاعترافهم بالتَّبرك باسمه تعالی فردّ عليهم الموحد . وهذا القصر : إمَّا قصر إفراد، وهو يخاطب به معتقد الشركة، أو قصر قلب، وهو يخاطب به معتقد عكس الحكم، أو قصر تعيين، وهو يخاطب المتشكّك . به قوله : (لأنَّ كلَّ شارع في شيء): أي: تأليف أو غيره. قوله: (ولإفادة حصول البركة) علة ثانية لتقديره خاصاً؛ أي: ففي تقدير المتعلق خاصّاً تخصيص التَّبرُّك بالشُّروع فيه وتعميم أجزائه، بخلاف ما لو قدَّره من مادة الابتداء ؛ فإنَّه ليس خاصاً بالمشروع فيه، ولا عاماً أجزاء المشروع فيه، بل قاصر على التبرك في البداية، فتدبَّر .
He said: (And we only estimated the related element as a verb... etc.) Know: The established position is that it is permissible for the related element to be a verb or a noun, and in each case specific or general, and in each case preceded or delayed, so the total is eight aspects. The first of them is what the commentator said: because the basis of action is verbs, and what acts from nouns like the active participle, passive participle, descriptive adjective, verbal noun, and noun of the masdar; = Hasbala added by some scholars, and the recommended comes after the obligatory, knowledge? And if the heart is hardened by others, then remember until only the Creator remains. (1) al-Shatibiyya (p.1). And delayed, because advancing the object of the action indicates restriction; and specific, because everyone who begins something should estimate what the basmala is made a beginning for, and to obtain blessing for all parts of the action. So it [the verb] is by way of analogical to the verb, and because in estimating a noun there is an increase in implicitness, because what is omitted then is several words: the construct and the construct dependent, and the related element of the preposition and the prepositional object; unlike 'I compose' (u'allif), for it with its agent established in it is two words. He said: (And delayed means after the basmala; because advancing the object indicates restriction): i.e., it indicates restricting the blessing in composition to His name, the Exalted; so the ba' enters onto the restricted [item]; because the polytheists used to begin with the names of their gods, saying: 'By the name of al-Lat and al-'Uzza', seeking blessing by it, not restriction, due to their acknowledgment of blessing in His name, the Exalted, so the monotheist refutes them. And this restriction is either restriction of definition (qasr ifrad), which is addressed to the one who believes in association; or restriction of reversal (qasr qalb), which is addressed to the one who believes the opposite of the ruling; or restriction of specification (qasr ta'yin), which is addressed to the one who is in doubt. He said: (Because everyone who begins something): i.e., composition or other. He said: (And to obtain blessing) is a second cause for estimating it as specific; i.e., in estimating the related element as specific, there is specification of blessing for the beginning of it and generalization of its parts, unlike if he estimated it from the root of 'beginning' (ibtida'); then it would not be specific to the commenced action, nor general to the parts of the commenced action, but rather limited to the blessing at the beginning, so ponder.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is a detailed grammatical and theological analysis of the basmala's related element (muta'allaq). The author begins by citing the commentator's statement, then establishes the rule: the muta'allaq can be a verb or a noun, and each can be specific (khas) or general ('amm), and can be placed before (muqaddam) or after (mu'akhkhar), yielding eight possible combinations. The first of these is the commentator's choice: a verb, because the basis of action in Arabic grammar is verbs, and verbs act on their objects without the need for implied omissions that nouns require. The author then inserts a seemingly marginal poetic line (possibly a digression). Returning to the argument, the author explains why the chosen verb must be specific and delayed: specific because every beginner of an action should estimate the muta'allaq as the specific action for which the basmala is the beginning, and delayed because advancing the object (the muta'allaq) before the basmala would indicate restriction (ikhtisas) — meaning that the blessing is restricted to God's name alone. This is important to counter the polytheists who used the names of their gods seeking blessing without restriction. The author then explains the three types of qasr (restriction) in rhetoric: qasr ifrad (restriction of individuation) for those who associate partners, qasr qalb (restriction of reversal) for those who hold the opposite belief, and qasr ta'yin (restriction of specification) for those who are in doubt. Finally, a second reason for choosing 'specific' is given: to obtain blessing (baraka) for the entire action from its start, not just the initial moment. If the muta'allaq were taken from the root 'ibtida'' (beginning), the blessing would be limited to the beginning only. The author concludes with 'ponder' (fatadabbar), encouraging reflection.

Key Terms
متعلق
فعل
اسم
خاص
عام
مقدم
مؤخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the muta'allaq can be any noun or verb without specification.
Correction: The passage shows that to convey the intended meaning of restriction and blessing, the muta'allaq must be a specific verb, delayed, emphasizing Allah's exclusive blessing.

Checkpoint
How many possible combinations does the author list for the muta'allaq of the basmala?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
The muta'allaq of the basmala is best understood as a specific verb ('u'allif') placed after the basmala to indicate restriction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' before an action, do you consciously connect it to a specific verb (like 'I read', 'I eat', 'I work'), or do you recite it as a mere phrase? How does the lesson's insistence on a specific verb challenge your current practice?
?
The author argues that delaying the verb conveys restriction ('ikhtisas'). In your daily life, does Allah's name come after your action in priority, or do you truly put Him first? Where do you need to realign your intentions so that the mention of Allah is not merely a preface but the very basis of your act?

The Three Types of Restriction (Qasr) and the Blessing of Specificity
Al-Sawi explains the three types of qasr and provides a second reason for the muta'allaq's specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of qasr (rhetorical restriction) and the second reason for choosing a specific muta'allaq.

Core Idea
The basmala uses qasr to restrict blessing to Allah, countering polytheist practices.

In this continuation, Al-Sawi explains the rhetorical concept of qasr (restriction) as applied to the basmala. He describes three types of qasr and gives a second reason for the muta'allaq being specific: to extend blessing to the entire action.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three types of qasr
Read the passage and identify the three types: qasr ifrad, qasr qalb, and qasr ta'yin. Note the audience for each type.
Qasr ifrad is for polytheists, qasr qalb for those with opposite belief, qasr ta'yin for doubters.
2
See how qasr refutes polytheists
Notice that the polytheists used their gods' names for blessing but not restriction. The basmala's qasr restricts blessing to Allah alone.
This is a theological refutation embedded in grammar.
3
Appreciate the second reason for specificity
Understand that estimating the muta'allaq as specific (rather than from 'ibtida'') ensures that the blessing covers the entire action, not just the beginning.
The author encourages reflection: 'fatadabbar'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإنما قدرنا المتعلق فعلاً ... إلخ) : اعلم : أنَّ المقرر أنَّه يجوز أن يكون المتعلَّق فعلاً أو اسماً ، وعلى كلّ خاصاً أو عاماً، أو على كلِّ مقدَّماً أو مؤخّراً ، فالحاصل ثمانية أوجه : الأولى منها : ما قاله الشَّارح: لأنَّ الأصل في العمل للأفعال أي وما عمل من الأسماء؛ كاسم الفاعل واسم المفعول والصفة المشبَّهة والمصدر، واسم المصدر؛ = حسبلة زادها بعض العلما والندب يأتي بعد فرض علما وإن قسا القلب من الأغيار قاذكر حتى لا يبقى إلا الباري (۱) الشاطبية (ص١). ومتأخّراً؛ لأن تقديم المعمول يفيد الاختصاص، وخاصّاً؛ لأنَّ كلَّ شارع في شيء ينبغي له أن يقدر ما جعلت البسملة مبدأ له، ولإفادة حصول البركة لجميع أجزاء الفعل. فهو بطريق الحمل على الفعل، ولما في تقدير الاسم من زيادة الإضمار؛ لأَنَّ المحذوف حينئذ عدة كلمات : المضاف والمضاف إليه، ومتعلق الجار والمجرور ؛ بخلاف أؤلف ؛ فإنَّه مع فاعله المستقر فيه كلمتان. قوله: (ومتأخّراً ) : أي : عن البسملة؛ لأنَّ تقديم المعمول يفيد الاختصاص) : أي : يفيد قصر التبرك في التأليف على اسمه تعالى؛ فالباء داخلة على المقصور عليه؛ لأنَّ المشركين كانوا يبدؤون بأسماء آلهتهم، فيقولون : باسم اللات والعزى ؛ تبرُّكاً لا اختصاصاً، لاعترافهم بالتَّبرك باسمه تعالی فردّ عليهم الموحد . وهذا القصر : إمَّا قصر إفراد، وهو يخاطب به معتقد الشركة، أو قصر قلب، وهو يخاطب به معتقد عكس الحكم، أو قصر تعيين، وهو يخاطب المتشكّك . به قوله : (لأنَّ كلَّ شارع في شيء): أي: تأليف أو غيره. قوله: (ولإفادة حصول البركة) علة ثانية لتقديره خاصاً؛ أي: ففي تقدير المتعلق خاصّاً تخصيص التَّبرُّك بالشُّروع فيه وتعميم أجزائه، بخلاف ما لو قدَّره من مادة الابتداء ؛ فإنَّه ليس خاصاً بالمشروع فيه، ولا عاماً أجزاء المشروع فيه، بل قاصر على التبرك في البداية، فتدبَّر .
He said: (And we only estimated the related element as a verb... etc.) Know: The established position is that it is permissible for the related element to be a verb or a noun, and in each case specific or general, and in each case preceded or delayed, so the total is eight aspects. The first of them is what the commentator said: because the basis of action is verbs, and what acts from nouns like the active participle, passive participle, descriptive adjective, verbal noun, and noun of the masdar; = Hasbala added by some scholars, and the recommended comes after the obligatory, knowledge? And if the heart is hardened by others, then remember until only the Creator remains. (1) al-Shatibiyya (p.1). And delayed, because advancing the object of the action indicates restriction; and specific, because everyone who begins something should estimate what the basmala is made a beginning for, and to obtain blessing for all parts of the action. So it [the verb] is by way of analogical to the verb, and because in estimating a noun there is an increase in implicitness, because what is omitted then is several words: the construct and the construct dependent, and the related element of the preposition and the prepositional object; unlike 'I compose' (u'allif), for it with its agent established in it is two words. Commentary
This passage is a detailed grammatical and theological analysis of the basmala's related element (muta'allaq). The author begins by citing the commentator's statement, then establishes the rule: the muta'allaq can be a verb or a noun, and each can be specific (khas) or general ('amm), and can be placed before (muqaddam) or after (mu'akhkhar), yielding eight possible combinations. The author then inserts a seemingly marginal poetic line (possibly a digression). Checkpoint
Which type of qasr is addressed to someone who believes in sharing blessing with other deities?
A) Qasr ifrad
B) Qasr qalb
C) Qasr ta'yin
D) None

Key Takeaway
The basmala's muta'allaq is a specific verb delayed to restrict blessing to Allah, reinforced by three types of qasr and a second reason to include all parts of the action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you catch yourself, even subtly, attributing success in your projects to your own effort or to other 'sources of blessing' like luck or influential people? How does the qasr in the basmala force you to re-examine where your trust truly lies?
?
When you begin an action—whether reading, working, or studying—do you consciously intend to seek Allah's blessing for every part of it, or do you only ask for a good start and then forget? What would change in your day if you truly believed the basmala covers all parts?
?
The polytheists sought blessing from Allah's name while also invoking others. In your life, are there any signs of such dual reliance? What would it look like for you to completely restrict your source of blessing to Allah alone, starting now?

Linguistic and Grammatical Definition of 'Ism' (Name)
Definition of ism linguistically and grammatically, with commentary unpacking the grammatical criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of 'ism' (name) — the linguistic and the grammatical — and how the grammatical definition distinguishes nouns from verbs and particles.

Core Idea
A noun is a word that carries its own meaning and is not tied to time by its very coinage.

Before we delve into the theological arguments in this course, we must establish a common vocabulary. The very first term we need to clarify is 'ism' (اسم), which can mean a name or a noun. This passage from al-Kharida lays out its linguistic and grammatical definitions, highlighting how grammarians distinguish nouns from verbs and particles. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting the foundational texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that the passage presents both a linguistic and a grammatical definition of 'ism'.
2
Distinguish the two definitions
Identify the linguistic definition (what indicates a named thing) and the grammatical definition (what indicates a meaning in itself not associated with time by coinage). Notice how the linguistic definition is broader.
3
Unpack the grammatical definition
Focus on the three conditions: 'what indicates' as a genus, 'in itself' to exclude particles, and 'not associated with time by coinage' to exclude verbs. Understand the reasoning.
The commentator explains each part.
4
Consider the exceptions
Note that some verbs like 'asā and laysa are temporally neutral, but they are still verbs because they are coined for time in principle. Similarly, nouns that indicate time (like 'when') are still nouns because their time reference is not part of their coinage.
5
Reflect on the importance
Think about why this distinction matters for theology. Knowing the precise meaning of 'ism' helps in understanding arguments about God's names and attributes.
This connects to later lessons on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاسم لغة: ما دلَّ على مسمّى، وعند النحاة : ما دلّ على معنى في نفسه غير مقترن بزمان وَضْعاً .
قوله : (ما دلّ على مسمّى) أي كان فعلاً أو اسماً أو حرفاً بالمعنى المصطلح عليه .
قوله : ( وعند النُّحاة) : أي : في اصطلاحهم.
قوله : (ما دلّ :أي: لفظ دلَّ ... إلخ، وهو جنس يشمل الفعل والحرف .
وقوله في نفسه أي لا في غيره، خرج الحرف.
وقوله : (غير مقترن بزمان وضعاً خرج الفعل؛ فإنَّه دال على معنى في نفسه، لكنَّه موضوع للزَّمان، وإن تجرَّد عنه في بعض الأفعال؛ كعسى وليس ونعم وبئس ، ودخلت الأسماء الدالة على الزمان لا بالوضع; كأسماء الشروط والاستفهام، فتدبَّر .
And the name (ism) linguistically: what indicates a named thing (musamma); and with the grammarians: what indicates a meaning in itself not associated with time by coinage (wad'). He said: 'What indicates a named thing' i.e. whether it is a verb, a noun, or a particle according to the terminological meaning. He said: 'And with the grammarians' i.e. in their terminology. He said: 'What indicates' i.e. a word indicates... etc., and it is a genus that includes the verb and the particle. And His saying 'in itself' i.e. not in something else, which excludes the particle. And His saying 'not associated with time by coinage' excludes the verb; for it indicates a meaning in itself, but it is coined for time, even though some verbs are devoid of time, like 'asā, laysa, ni'ma, and bi'sa. And nouns indicating time enter not by coinage, like nouns of condition and interrogation, so reflect.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'ism' (name) from two perspectives. Linguistically, ism is whatever indicates a musamma (a named entity), which in a broad sense includes verbs and particles as well. Grammatically, ism is defined as a word that indicates a meaning in itself (fi nafsihi) and is not associated with time by its coinage (wad'). The commentary then unpacks the grammatical definition: the phrase 'what indicates' is a genus covering verbs and particles. 'In itself' excludes particles, which indicate meaning in other things (like connecting words). 'Not associated with time by coinage' excludes verbs, because verbs are coined to indicate time (past, present, future), even if some exceptional verbs like 'asā, laysa, ni'ma, bi'sa are temporally neutral. Additionally, nouns that indicate time (like 'morning' or interrogative 'when') are considered nouns because their time reference is not part of their coinage but is acquired through usage. This definition sharpens the grammatical classification of parts of speech.

Key Terms

مسمّى
معنى
زمان
وضع
فعل
حرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The linguistic definition of 'ism' (what indicates a named thing) is the same as the grammatical definition.
Correction: The grammatical definition adds two restrictions: the word must indicate a meaning in itself and not be associated with time by coinage, thus excluding verbs and particles.

Checkpoint
According to the grammatical definition, what distinguishes a noun (ism) from a verb (fi'l)?
A) A noun indicates a meaning in itself, while a verb does not
B) A noun is not associated with time by coinage, while a verb is
C) A noun can be derived from a verb
D) A noun is always definite

Key Takeaway
The grammatical definition of ism is a word that indicates a meaning in itself and is not coined for time, which distinguishes it from verbs and particles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you consciously recognise the grammatical categories of each word? How might a lack of such precision lead you to misunderstand a verse's meaning? Commit today to studying at least one grammar rule each time you open the Book.
?
The commentator ends the passage with 'so reflect'. What does this call to reflection tell you about the weight of precise definitions in your faith? Are you content with superficial understanding, or will you pursue depth even in technical matters? Ask Allah for a love of exact knowledge.

Is 'Allah' a Proper Noun? The Objection and Answer
Al-Sawi presents the objection and complete answer regarding 'Allah' as a proper noun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments in the debate over whether the word 'Allah' is a proper noun or a common noun, and how the answer preserves the meaning of tawhid.

Core Idea
The debate over 'Allah' hinges on whether essence-knowledge requires attributes or not.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary presents a central linguistic-theological debate: is the word 'Allah' a proper noun (علم) or a common noun (كلي)? The objection that if it were a common noun, the testimony of faith would not affirm tawhid, and the answer showing how proper noun designation still allows knowledge through attributes, prepares you to engage with the Arabic text.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Al-Sawi presents al-Baydawi's view that 'Allah' is a common noun and the objection that this would undermine tawhid.
The objection states that if 'Allah' were a common noun, the declaration would not specify God's unique essence.
2
Understand al-Safawi's argument
Al-Safawi argues that if 'Allah' is a proper noun, it would require direct knowledge of essence without attributes – impossible for humans.
This sets up the need for an answer.
3
Learn the answer
Al-Shihab and al-Baydawi respond that if God is the namer, He knows His essence; if a human names, it is through attributes sufficient for the namer's purpose.
The answer references the basmala to illustrate knowledge through attributes.
4
Reflect on the benefit
The passage concludes by stating the benefit of a proper noun is knowledge of essence without attribute, but here attributes are used as a bridge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبيضاوي : إنَّه كلّيّ ; إذ معناه : المعبود بحقِّ، فيصح إطلاقه على كل متّصف بتلك الصفة، ولم يتّصف بها إلَّا الخالق، فهو صفة ورد بأنَّه : لو كان كليّاً لم تفد (لا إله إلا الله) توحيداً ; لأنَّها لم تحصر ذاته لنا على وجه التّشخص، مع أنَّ الشارع جعلها توحيداً. فإن قلت : قال السَّيِّد عيسى الصَّفوي : (عرفوا العلم بما وضع لشخص بعينه، والمتبادر منه أن يكون الشَّخص ملاحظاً للواضع؛ أي: معلوماً له، وذات الله بلا ملاحظةٍ صفة غير معقولة للبشر، فلا يكون الله علماً له؛ لأنَّ العلم ما للذات من غير صفة) أجاب الشهاب تبعاً للبيضاوي: بأنَّ واضع العلم إن كان هو الله؛ فهو يعلم ذاته وصفاته، وإن كان غيره ; فالتحقيق أنَّ تصور الموضوع له بوجه معنى البسملة على الذات الواجب الوجود الخالق للعالم. و(الرَّحمن الرّحيم : صفتان مشبَّهتان بُنيتا للمبالغة من رحم - بالكسر – إما بتنزيله منزلة اللازم اللُّغة ما كافٍ في واضع العلم ؛ كعلمنا ذات الله باعتبار صفاته؛ لأنَّ واضع لا يفعل إلا ما فيه فائدة يعتد بها ، بل كل عاقل كذلك، وإنَّما فائدة العلم معرفة الذات من غير صفة؛ إذ لو قصد ما يحصل بوضع الصفة لم يكن في وضع العلم فائدة يعتد بها . انتهى سحيمي على عبد السلام
And al-Baydawi: it is a common noun; because its meaning is the one rightly worshipped, so it is correct to apply it to everything qualified by that attribute, and nothing is qualified by it except the Creator, so it is an attribute. It is objected: if it were a common noun, the statement 'there is no god but God' would not imply tawhid, because it does not delimit His essence for us in a specific manner, whereas the Lawgiver made it tawhid. If you say: al-Sayyid 'Isa al-Safawi said: 'They defined the proper noun as that which is placed for a specific person, and what is understood from it is that the person is observed by the one who places (i.e., known to him). God's essence without observation of an attribute is inconceivable to humans, so God cannot be a proper noun for Him, because the proper noun is what is for the essence without attribute.' Al-Shihab answered following al-Baydawi: that the one who places the proper noun, if he is God, then He knows His essence and attributes; and if he is other than God, then the verification is that the conceptualization of the subject (of the naming) is in some way [sufficient]. The meaning of the basmala: upon the Essence, the Necessary Existence, the Creator of the world. And al-Rahman, al-Rahim (are two adjectives built for exaggeration from rahima—with kasra— either on the basis of treating it as an inherent [property]? The [?] is sufficient in the one who places the proper noun, like our knowledge of God's essence by virtue of His attributes; because the one who places does nothing unless there is a worthwhile benefit in it, and indeed every rational person is such. And the benefit of the proper noun is knowledge of the essence without attribute; for if he intended what is obtained by placing the attribute, then there would be no worthwhile benefit in placing the proper noun. End of al-Suhaymi on 'Abd al-Salam.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument about whether the word 'Allah' is a proper noun (علم) or a common noun (كلي). It begins with al-Baydawi's view that it is a common noun meaning 'the one worshipped rightly,' applicable to any qualified entity, but only the Creator so qualifies. The objection states that if it were a common noun, the declaration of tawhid would not single out God's essence. Al-Safawi then argues that if 'Allah' were a proper noun, it would require direct apprehension of God's essence without attributes—impossible for humans. Al-Shihab replies that if God Himself is the namer, He knows His essence; if someone else names Him, the intended referent (المسمى) can be known through attributes, fulfilling the purpose of the proper noun. The answer continues by referencing the basmala, arguing that knowing the essence through attributes is sufficient for the namer's benefit. The passage ends by stating that the benefit of a proper noun is knowledge of the essence without attributes, echoing earlier points. The argument is embedded within the commentary on the basmala, linking the linguistic debate to the opening phrase of the Quran.

علم
توحيد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if 'Allah' is a common noun, then 'لا إله إلا الله' cannot imply tawhid.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the proper noun designation preserves tawhid through the namer's intent, and knowledge through attributes is sufficient.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection against considering 'Allah' as a common noun?
A) It would require God to be known without attributes.
B) It would make the phrase 'La ilaha illa Allah' ineffective for tawhid.
C) It contradicts the lawgiver's intention.
D) It implies that others could be called gods.

Key Takeaway
The debate shows that whether 'Allah' is a proper or common noun, the key is that it designates the divine essence in a way that preserves tawhid, either through direct knowledge (by God) or through attributes (by humans).

Nature of Hamdalah: Declarative or Performative?
Author analyzes whether الحمد لله is declarative or performative, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over the grammatical nature of 'الحمد لله' and the theological reasoning behind the preferred interpretation.

Core Idea
The hamdalah sentence is declarative in both form and function, not performative in creating God's right.

In this passage, the author examines a key theological-linguistic question: when we say 'الحمد لله,' are we merely stating a fact about God, or are we actually performing an act of praise? The analysis involves distinguishing between the content of the sentence and the act of uttering it. This lesson unpacks the objection and answer that clarify the correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms: خبرية, إنشائية, إنشاء الثناء, etc.
2
Identify the two views
The passage presents two possible grammatical classifications for the hamdalah sentence. Find them and note which one is considered correct.
These views are about declarative (خبرية) vs. performative (إنشائية).
3
Study the objection and answer
An objection is raised against the performative interpretation, followed by an answer. Understand the theological reasoning behind the answer and why the nominal form is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجملة الحمدلة إمَّا خبريَّة لفظاً ومعنى ؛ بناء على أنَّ المخبر بالحمد حامد؛ وهو الصحيح، أو خبريَّة لفظاً، إنشائية معنى. واستشكل : بأنَّه لا يمكن العبد أن ينشئ اختصاصه تعالى بالمحامد أو استحقاقه إيَّاها لقدّم ذلك. وأجيب : بأنَّ المراد بكونها إنشائيَّة أنّها لإنشاء الثَّناء بمضمونها لا أنَّها لإنشاء مضمونها ؛ إذ هو ثابت أزلاً لا يمكن إنشاؤه من العبيد، وآثر الاسميَّة لدلالتها على الثبوت والدوام، واقتداء بالكتاب العزيز.
And the sentence of hamdalah is either declarative in wording and meaning, based on the fact that the one informing of praise is a praiser—and this is correct—or declarative in wording, performative in meaning. And it was objected: that the servant cannot create God's exclusive right to praise or His deserving of it due to its preeternity. And it was answered: that what is meant by its being performative is that it is for creating the praise by its content, not for creating its content; for it is preeternally established, not possible for servants to create. And he preferred the nominal form because it indicates permanence and continuity, and in emulation of the Mighty Book.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage analyzes the grammatical and theological status of the sentence 'الحمد لله' (Praise be to God). The author presents two possibilities: First, it is a declarative sentence (خبرية) both in wording and meaning, meaning the speaker simply states that praise belongs to God. This is considered correct. Second, it could be declarative in wording but performative (إنشائية) in meaning, meaning that saying it actually enacts praise. An objection raises against the second view: since God's right to praise is eternal, a servant cannot create (إنشاء) that right or deserve it. The answer clarifies that 'performative' means the utterance creates the act of praising (الثناء), not the reality of God's deserving praise, which is eternal. The author then prefers the nominal form (الاسمية) because it implies permanence and follows the Quranic style.

إنشائية
إنشاء الثناء
إنشاء المضمون
اسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'الحمد لله' is performative in the sense that it creates God's right to be praised.
Correction: It only creates the act of praising; God's right to praise is eternal and cannot be created.

Checkpoint
Why can't the sentence 'الحمد لله' be performative in the sense of creating God's right to praise?
A) Because that right is eternal and cannot be created.
B) Because the sentence is only declarative.
C) Because the servant is able to create it.
D) Because the Quran forbids it.

Key Takeaway
The hamdalah sentence is declarative in both wording and meaning; its performative aspect only applies to the act of praise, not to creating the eternal reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If the performative aspect of hamdalah only creates the act of praising, not the reality of God's worth, then your praise is a response, not a gift. Are you living as if your worship adds to God's glory? How will you remind yourself of your place today?

Grammar of Maqul al-Qawl: The Accusative Object of Saying
Author explains the grammatical rule for the object of the verb 'to say' and its accusative case.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the normal grammatical rule for the object of the verb 'to say' (مقول القول) and the exceptional case when 'قول' behaves like 'ظن'.

Core Idea
The object of 'say' is normally a sentence, but it can be a word when 'say' acts like 'think'.

This passage from a grammatical commentary explains the accusative case of 'مقول القول' (the object of saying). The author clarifies that the verb 'قال' typically takes a sentence or a word functioning as a sentence, but there is an exception. When 'قول' is used in the manner of the verb 'ظن' (to think), it can take a single word in the accusative. This lesson unpacks that rule with examples from Ibn Malik.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the overall topic. Notice the mention of 'مقول القول' and the explanation that follows.
The passage includes a citation from Ibn Malik's Alfiyya.
2
Identify the normal rule
The author states that 'القول لا ينصب إلا الجمل أو المفرد الذي في معنى الجملة، أو المفرد الذي قصد لفظه'. Understand that normally the object of 'قال' is either a full sentence, a word that is like a sentence, or a quoted word.
3
Understand the exception
The exception is introduced with 'ما لم يُجر مجرى الظن'. When 'قول' is used like 'ظن', it can put single words in the accusative. The author cites Ibn Malik's lines to support this.
The example 'قل ذا مشفقا' shows this usage.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that 'مقول القول' is always a single word in accusative. The passage clarifies that it is usually a sentence or equivalent, except in the 'ظن' construction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقول القول في محلّ نصب. و (أل) فيه جنسية، أو استغراقية، ولام (الله) قوله : (مقول القول ... إلخ) : أي : لأنَّ القول لا ينصب إلَّا الجمل أو المفرد الذي في معنى الجملة، أو المفرد الذي قصد لفظه ما لم يُجر مجرى الظَّنِّ، فينصب المفردات كما هو معلوم من قول ابن مالك (۱): وكتظنُّ اجعل تقول إن وَلي مستفـهـمـاً بـه ولـم يـنـفـصـلِ ... إلى أن قال : وأُجري القولُ كظن مطلقا . سُلَيْم نحو قُلْ ذا مُشفقا
The object of the verb 'to say' (maqul al-qawl) is in the accusative position. And the 'al' in it is either generic or universal, and the lam of 'Allah'. His statement: 'Maqul al-qawl... etc.', meaning: because the verb 'to say' only puts in the accusative either a sentence or a single word that is in the meaning of a sentence, or a single word whose wording is intended, unless it is used in the manner of the verb 'to think', in which case it puts single words in the accusative, as is known from the statement of Ibn Malik: 'And like 'zanna' make 'taqulu' when it is followed by an interrogative and not separated... until he said: 'And 'al-qawlu' is used like 'zannun' absolutely, like 'sulaym'[?] say 'dha mushfiqan'.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical note on the construction 'مقول القول' (the object of saying). The author explains that the object of the verb 'قال' (he said) is normally in the accusative case (محل نصب). However, the verb 'قال' only takes as its object either a full sentence (جملة) or a single word that functions like a sentence, or a single word whose wording is intended (i.e., quoting the word itself). But if the verb 'قال' is used in the manner of the verb 'ظن' (to think), it can take single words as its object in the accusative. The author cites Ibn Malik's lines from the Alfiyya: he says that sometimes 'قول' (saying) can act like 'ظن' (to think), setting the object as accusative, for example: 'قل ذا مشفقا' (say this, being sympathetic). This is a specific grammatical rule for the verb of saying.

Key Terms
مقول القول
محل نصب
جملة
مفرد
القول كظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'مقول القول' is always a single word in the accusative case.
Correction: The passage clarifies that normally the object of 'قال' is a sentence or a word functioning as a sentence, and only exceptionally, when 'قول' behaves like 'ظن', can it be a single word.

Checkpoint

A) When it is used in the manner of the verb 'ظن' (to think).
B) Always, because 'قال' always takes a single word.
C) Only when the word is a proper noun.
D) Only when the word is indefinite.

Key Takeaway
The object of 'to say' is normally a sentence or sentence-equivalent, but when 'say' behaves like 'think', it can take a single word in the accusative.

The Meaning of 'Al' in 'Al-Hamdu'
Explanation of the types of Al in Al-Hamdu and the objection about praising creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible meanings of the definite article 'Al' in the phrase 'Al-Hamdu lillah' and how the objection about praising creatures is resolved.

Core Idea
All praise is ultimately for Allah, even when outwardly directed to others.

The first words of Surah Al-Fatiha, 'Al-Hamdu lillah', invite theological reflection on the nature of praise. The definite article 'Al' in 'Al-Hamdu' can carry different meanings—generic, universal, or covenantal—each with implications for Islamic monotheism. This lesson unpacks those meanings and addresses a natural objection: if all praise belongs to Allah, what about the praise creatures give to one another?

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without translation to get the rhythm. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the technical terms used: جنسية, استغراقية, عهدية.
2
Identify the three meanings of 'Al'
The passage presents three possibilities: generic, universal, and covenantal. For each, understand the definition and how it applies to praise.
The generic is said to be the original usage; the universal is incidental.
3
Understand the objection and answer
A hypothetical objection is raised: what about a creature praising another creature? The answer is that all praise belongs to Allah in reality because He is the true Bestower.
Reflect on how this preserves Tawhid without denying common human interactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأل) فيه جنسيَّة) أي: وهو الأصل في وضعها، وأما كونها استغراقيَّة؛ فهو طارئ عليها والمعنى على الجنسيّة جنس الحمد مستحق الله تعالى وإذا اختصّ جنس الحمد بالله ؛ فلا فرد منه لغيره تعالى فحينئذ ساوت الاستغراقية. إن قلت يرد عليه حمد الحادث للحادث، وحمد القديم للحادث. أجيب : بأن المراد جميع المحامد الله في الواقع ونفس الأمر لا بحسب الظاهر، فهذان الحمدان وإن كانا بحسب الظاهر لغير الله تعالى ففي الواقع ونفس الأمر هما له ؛ لأنَّه المنعم الحقيقي، فتدبَّر. قوله : (أو استغراقيَّة) أي: وعلامتها أن يحل محلها كل، وجوّز أن تكون عهديَّة، والمعهود هو الحمد القديم الأزليُّ الَّذي بعضهم
His statement: 'And the 'al' in it is generic'—that is, and it is the original in its use, and as for its being universal, it is incidental to it, and the meaning according to the generic is: the genus of praise is due to God Most High, and when the genus of praise is exclusively for God, then no individual of it is for other than Him, so then it equals the universal. If you say: there is objected against it the praise of a creature for a creature, and the praise of the Eternal for a creature. It is answered: that what is meant is all praises are for God in reality and in the actual state of affairs, not according to outward appearance; so these two praises, even if outwardly they are for other than God, in reality and in the actual state of affairs they are for Him, because He is the true Bestower of blessings, so ponder. His statement: 'Or universal'—that is, and its sign is that 'kull' (every) can replace it. And it is possible that it is covenantal, and the covenanted is the preeternal eternal praise which some of them...
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage discusses the grammatical function of the definite article ال in the word الحمد (praise) in the phrase الحمد لله. The author explains two possibilities: First, it is generic (جنسية), which is the original usage. This means the genus of praise belongs exclusively to God. If the entire genus is for God, then no individual instance of praise is for anyone else, effectively making it equivalent to universal (استغراقية) in meaning. An objection is raised: what about a creature praising another creature, or the eternal (God) praising a creature? The answer is that all praise, in reality and in the actual state of affairs, belongs to God alone. Outwardly, praise may seem directed to others, but since God is the true bestower of blessings, any praise ultimately goes to Him. The author then adds that the ال could also be universal (استغراقية), which means it can be replaced by 'كل' (every). Finally, it is also possible that the ال is covenantal (عهدية), referring to the specific preeternal eternal praise that is known. The text cuts off at 'which some of them...'

Key Terms
أل جنسية
أل استغراقية
أل عهدية
حمد الحادث
المنعم الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praising a creature directly contradicts 'Al-Hamdu lillah' and implies shirk.
Correction: The author explains that all praise is ultimately for Allah in reality because He is the true source of every blessing; outward praise to creatures is permissible as it acknowledges the means, not the ultimate source.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection about creatures praising creatures?
A) Such praise is forbidden.
B) In reality, all praise is for God because He is the true bestower of blessings.
C) The definite article is covenantal in those cases.
D) Creatures can be praised independently of God.

Key Takeaway
All praise belongs to Allah in reality, even when it appears directed at others, because He is the true source of all blessings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise someone, do you genuinely feel that the praise ultimately belongs to Allah? How would this awareness change the way you express praise?
?
Think of a compliment you recently received. Did you silently acknowledge that the quality being praised is a gift from Allah? What prevents you from doing that consistently?
?
If all praise is really for Allah, then your pride in your own achievements is misplaced. What is one concrete step you can take today to redirect praise to its true Owner?

The Four Meanings of the Lam in 'Al-Hamdu Lillah'
Al-Sawi explains the four possibilities for the lam and defends istiḥqāq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four possible interpretations of the lam in the phrase 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' and why the interpretation of 'deservingness' (istiḥqāq) is the most secure.

Core Idea
The lam in 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' can mean deservingness, ownership, causation, or specification—but only deservingness avoids theological problems.

The Basmala phrase 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' begins with a lam that can be interpreted in multiple ways. Al-Sawi, commenting on an earlier text, breaks down four distinct meanings and then examines a potential problem with the interpretation of 'ownership'. This passage sharpens our understanding of theological precision in language.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rule of the lam
The lam occurs between a meaning and an essence. This sets the stage for interpreting its possible functions.
2
Memorize the four possibilities
List the four possibilities: istiḥqāq, mulk, taʿlīl, ikhtiṣāṣ. Each yields a different sense of 'al-ḥamdu lillāh'.
Write them down to internalize the classification.
3
Explore the problem with mulk
If the lam is for ownership and 'al' is specific, it would mean a specific pre-eternal praise is owned by God, but ownership applies only to temporal things.
Notice the theological difficulty.
4
See the resolution and preference
Al-Dusūqī resolves the problem by interpreting the specific praise as a composite of divine and human praise, which is temporal. Nevertheless, istiḥqāq remains safe from such issues.
Understand why istiḥqāq is the preferred interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله :(للاستحقاق) أي وضابطها ما وقعت بين معنى وذات، وهذا أحد احتمالات أربع : الثَّاني : الملك، الثَّالث: التعليل الرابع: الاختصاص. فعلى الأوّل معناه : جميع المحامد مستحقة الله. وعلى الثَّاني : مملوكاً لله . وعلى الثَّالث : ثابتة لأجله . وعلى الرابع : مختصّة به . يصير لكن على جعل (أل) عهديَّة لا يناسب جعل اللام للملك ؛ لأنَّه المعنى : الحمد المعهود القديم مملوك لله، والمملوك لا يكون إلا حادثاً لا قديماً ؛ لأنَّ المملوك هو المتصرف فيه، والقديم لا يتصرف فيه، إلَّا أن يقال : المراد بالحمد المعهود حمد من يعتد به وهو حمد الله، وحمد أنبيائه، وحمد أوليائه، فيصحُ حينئذ جعلها للملك؛ لأنَّ المعهود حينئذ هو الهيئة المجتمعة من حمد الله وحمد غيره، وهي مركبة من قديم وهو حمد الله وحادث؛ وهو حمد غيره، والمركَّب من القديم والحادث حادث، والحادث يصح تعلُّق الملك به، كذا ذكره شيخنا الدسوقي في حاشية المصنف (۱) ; ولكن لما كانت لام الاستحقاق سالمة من الإشكال
His statement: '(for deservingness)' means: its rule is that it occurs between a meaning and an essence, and this is one of four possibilities: the second: ownership, the third: causation, the fourth: specification. According to the first, its meaning is: all praises are deserving of God. According to the second: owned by God. According to the third: established for His sake. According to the fourth: specific to Him. But it becomes problematic: if (al) is taken as definitive of a previously mentioned entity, then making the lam for ownership is not appropriate, because the meaning would be: the specific pre-eternal praise is owned by God, and what is owned cannot be but temporal, not pre-eternal; because what is owned is that which is disposed of, and the pre-eternal is not disposed of. Unless it is said: by 'the specific praise' is meant the praise of one who is regarded, which is the praise of God, the praise of His prophets, and the praise of His saints; then making it for ownership is valid, because the specific entity at that time is the composite form consisting of God's praise and the praise of others, and it is composed of the pre-eternal, which is God's praise, and the temporal, which is the praise of others; and the composite of the pre-eternal and the temporal is temporal, and the temporal can be attached to ownership. This is what our teacher al-Dusūqī mentioned in his gloss on the author's work (1). But because the lam of deservingness is safe from the problem.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'for deservingness' (للاستحقاق) in the earlier text. The author explains that the lam (the preposition 'li-') in 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' has a rule: it occurs between a meaning and an essence. Then he enumerates four possible meanings of the lam: 1) istiḥqāq (deservingness) – all praises are worthy of God; 2) mulk (ownership) – praises are owned by God; 3) taʿlīl (causation) – praises are established for God's sake; 4) ikhtiṣāṣ (specification) – praises are specific to God. He then points out a problem if we take the lam as mulk and the 'al' in 'al-ḥamdu' as a definite article referring to a specific previously mentioned praise (al-ʿahdiyya). In that case, it would mean a specific pre-eternal praise is owned by God, but ownership applies only to temporal things, not pre-eternal. However, al-Dusūqī resolved this by interpreting the specific praise as the composite of God's praise and the praise of His prophets and saints, which is temporal, thus making ownership applicable. Nevertheless, the lam of istiḥqāq avoids this problem entirely.

Key Terms
لام الاستحقاق
لام الملك
لام التعليل
لام الاختصاص
الحمد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The lam in 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' has only one possible meaning.
Correction: In fact, there are four possible interpretations: deservingness, ownership, causation, and specification. Each yields a different theological nuance.

Checkpoint
What is the fourth possibility for the lam in 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' according to the text?
A) Istihqaq
B) Mulk
C) Ta'lil
D) Ikhtisas

Key Takeaway
The lam in 'al-ḥamdu lillāh' has four interpretations, but the lam of deservingness (istiḥqāq) is the safest because it avoids theological complications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'al-ḥamdu lillāh', which meaning genuinely occupies your heart? Do you praise Him because He deserves it, or because you feel He owns you? How would your relationship with Him change if you truly internalized that He alone is worthy of all praise?
?
How often do you reflect on the precise meanings of the words you recite in prayer? Is your recitation a conscious acknowledgment of God's attributes, or has it become a habit detached from understanding? Set a specific intention this week to meditate on the meanings of your daily adhkār.
?
If you truly believed that all praises are due to God alone (istiḥqāq), how would you react when people praise you for your achievements? What concrete step can you take to redirect that praise to Him immediately?

Linguistic Definition of al-Hamd and Its Components
Al-Sawi defines al-hamd linguistically and explains its components and the virtue/benefit distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise linguistic definition of al-hamd (praise) as explained by al-Sawi, including its key components and conditions.

Core Idea
Al-hamd is praise for voluntary beauty, whether in virtues or benefits.

In this passage, al-Sawi unpacks the linguistic definition of al-hamd, clarifying each element: what praise is, what makes it 'beautiful,' the role of volition, and the requirement of veneration. He also distinguishes between virtues (intrinsic excellences) and benefits (transitive excellences), showing that both are valid objects of praise. This groundwork is essential for understanding the full concept of hamd in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully.
Read the passage once to get the flow, then read the translation to follow al-Sawi's commentary on each phrase of the definition.
2
Identify the five components of the definition.
The definition contains: 1) al-thana' (praise), 2) bi al-jamil (with beauty), 3) 'ala jamil ikhtiyari (upon voluntary beauty), 4) 'ala jihat al-ta'dhim (in a manner of veneration), 5) sawa ta'allaqa bi al-fada'il am bi al-fawadil (whether pertaining to virtues or benefits). Notice how each is explained.
3
Understand the distinction between virtues and benefits.
Virtues (al-fada'il) are intrinsic excellences like knowledge; benefits (al-fawadil) are transitive excellences like generosity. Both are included in al-hamd.
This distinction is crucial for the later discussion of pillars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحمد لغة : هو الثَّناء بالجميل على جميل اختياري على جهة التّعظيم، سواء تعلَّق بالفضائل أم بالفواضل. اقتصر الشارح عليها قوله : (لغة) : منصوبٌ على التّمييز . قوله : (هو الثَّناء): بتقديم المثلثة على النُّون والمد : الذِّكر بخير، النُّون على المثلثة والقصر ضدُّه، وحينئذ فقوله : (بالجميل) وصف وبتقديم كاشف على حدّ نظرت بعيني، وسمعت بأذني، والمراد به الصادر بالكلام قديماً كان أو حادثاً، فيشمل أقسام الحمد الأربعة. قوله : (بالجميل) بيان للمحمود به وللصيغة الصَّادرين من الحامد للمحمود . قوله : على جميل اختياري) بيان للمحمود عليه، والمراد بالاختياري حقيقة كالحمد على صفات الأفعال، أو حكماً : كالحمد على الذات وصفاتها ؛ لأنَّها منشأ أفعال اختياريَّة، وخرج بذلك ما كان جميلاً غير اختياري، فالثناء عليه مدح. قوله : (على جهة التَّعظيم) : أقحم لفظة (جهة) إشعاراً بأنَّه لا يكفي في الحمد التَّعظيمُ الظَّاهري، بل لا بدَّ أن يوافق الكلام الجنان، كذا قيل؛ لكن قال الأشياخ : الرَّاجح عدم اشتراطه . قوله : (سواء تعلق بالفضائل) : سواء خبر مقدم، وما بعده في تأويل مصدر مبتدأ مؤخَّر، والمعنى : تعلّقه بالفضائل أم بالفواضل مستو والمراد بالفضائل : المزايا القاصرة وهي التي لا يتوقف تعلُّقها(۱) على تعدّي أثرها للغير، وإن كانت هي متعديَّة؛ كالعلم والقدرة والحسن، وبالفواضل المزايا المتعديَّة، وهي التي يتوقف تعلُّقها على تعدّي أثرها للغير؛ كالكرم والتَّعليم، وهذه العبارة معنى قول غيره، سواء كان في مقابلة نعمة أم لا، فتحصل أنَّ أركان الحمد خمسة: حامد، ومحمود، ومحمود به، ومحمود عليه وصيغة؛ فإذا حمدت زيداً لكونه أكرمك بقولك : زيد عالم ؛ فأنت حامد وزيد محمود، والإكرام محمود عليه؛ أي: محمود به لأجله، وثبوت العلم الذي هو مدلول قولك: زيد عالم؛ محمود به، وقولك : زيد عالم هو الصيغة. (۱) قوله : (وهي التي لا يتوقف تعلقها . . . . إلخ ) : أي : لا يتوقف الاتصاف بها على تعدي أثرها للغير؛ أي: سواء كانت في نفسها غير متعدية للغير؛ كالحسن، أو كانت في نفسها متعدية؛ كالعلم، وأما الفواضل ؛ فإن الاتصاف بها يتوقف على تعدي أثرها للغير ؛ كالكرم ،والتعليم فكل من الفواضل والفضائل متعلق الحمد عليه، وهو المحمود عليه، فالثناء على من اتصف بها ،حمد، سواء تعدى أثرها للغير حامداً أو غيره، كأن أعطى المحمود للحامد أو غيره . . . أو علمه أو غيره مسألة أو لا، كان حمده لشجاعته ؛ فإنها اختياري لم يتعد أثره للحامد وغيره . اهـ كاتبه. (۲) قوله: زيد) عالم هذا مثال فيه اجتماع الحمد اللغوي والاصطلاحي، وينفرد اللغوي فيما كان لا في مقابلة نعمة هذا المثال، والمحمود عليه الشجاعة، والحمد الاصطلاحي لا بد أن يكون في مقابلة، سواء كانت على الحامد أو غيره، وينفرد الاصطلاحي في القيام لزيد عند قدومه ؛ لكونه أكرم الحامد أو غيره؛ لأن اللغوي لا يكون إلا باللسان وهذا عمل بالأركان اهـ كاتبه
And al-hamd linguistically is: praise (al-thana') with beauty upon a voluntary beauty in a manner of veneration, whether it pertains to virtues (al-fada'il) or benefits (al-fawadil). The commentator confined himself to it. His saying: (linguistically) is accusative as specification. His saying: (it is al-thana'): with the prefixed letter tha and noon and lengthening: remembrance with good; with noon prefixed to tha and shortening, its opposite. Then his saying: (with beauty) is a description and with prefixed explicative like 'I looked with my eye' and 'I heard with my ear'; what is meant by it is that which emanates from speech, whether ancient or originated, so it includes the four divisions of praise. His saying: (with beauty) is an explanation of what is praised with (al-mahmud bihi) and the form (al-sigha) emanating from the praiser to the praised. His saying: (upon a voluntary beauty) is an explanation of the reason for praise (al-mahmud 'alayhi); what is meant by voluntary is either actually, like praise upon attributes of acts, or juridically, like praise upon the essence and its attributes, because they are the source of voluntary acts; thus excluded is that which is beautiful but not voluntary, so praise upon it is commendation (madih). His saying: (in a manner of veneration): the word 'manner' (jiha) is inserted to indicate that mere apparent veneration is not sufficient in praise, rather the speech must correspond to the heart; thus it is said; but the masters (ashyakh) said: the preponderant view is that it is not a condition. His saying: (whether it pertains to virtues) : 'whether' is a predicate introduced, and what follows is in the interpretation of a source as a delayed subject; the meaning is: its connection to virtues or benefits is equal. And what is meant by virtues are the intrinsic excellences (al-mazaya al-qasira) which are those whose attribution does not depend on the transference of their effect to others, even if they are themselves transitive, like knowledge, power, and beauty; and by benefits are the transitive excellences (al-mazaya al-muta'addiya) which are those whose attribution depends on the transference of their effect to others, like generosity and teaching. This phrase is the meaning of another's saying: whether it is in return for a blessing or not. So it results that the pillars (arkan) of praise are five: the praiser (hamid), the praised (mahmud), that with which praise is made (mahmud bihi), the reason for which praise is made (mahmud 'alayhi), and the form (sigha). So if you praise Zayd because he honored you by saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable', then you are the praiser, Zayd is the praised, the honoring is the reason for praise, i.e., that for which he is praised, the affirmation of knowledge which is the implication of your saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is that with which praise is made, and your saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is the form. (1) His saying: (which are those whose attribution does not depend... etc.): i.e., their attribution does not depend on the transference of their effect to others; i.e., whether they are in themselves non-transitive to others, like beauty, or they are in themselves transitive, like knowledge. As for the benefits, their attribution depends on the transference of their effect to others, like generosity and teaching. So both benefits and virtues are related to the reason for praise, which is the reason for praise. So praise upon one who is described with them is praise (hamd), whether the effect transfers to the praiser or to another, such as if the praised gave something to the praiser or to another... or taught him or another a question or not, his praise for his courage; for it is voluntary and its effect does not transfer to the praiser or to another. End of the writer's words. (2) His saying: 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is an example in which linguistic and terminological praise combine. The linguistic praise stands alone in what is not in return for a blessing in this example, and the reason for praise is courage. Terminological praise must be in return for a blessing, whether it was upon the praiser or another. The terminological praise stands alone in standing up for Zayd upon his arrival because he honored the praiser or another; for the linguistic cannot be except with the tongue, and this is an action with the pillars. End of the writer's words.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the linguistic meaning of 'al-hamd' (praise), followed by an enumeration of its five pillars (arkan). The commentator al-Sawi begins by quoting the definition from the original text: 'al-hamd linguistically is praise (al-thana') with beauty upon a voluntary beauty in a manner of veneration, whether it pertains to virtues (al-fada'il) or benefits (al-fawadil).' He then proceeds to explain each term in the definition.

First, he clarifies that 'linguistically' is accusative as a specification (tamyiz). Then he explains 'al-thana': it is remembrance with good, and its opposite is 'al-dhamm' (blame). The phrase 'with beauty' is a description of the praise; the expression is analogous to 'I looked with my eye' where the word is explicative. What is meant by 'beauty' is that which emanates from speech, whether the speech is eternal (God's speech) or temporal (human speech), so it includes the four types of praise (praise of God for His essence, His attributes, His acts, and praise of others).

Next, he explains 'with beauty' as specifying that with which praise is made (al-mahmud bihi) and the form (al-sigha) emanating from the praiser to the praised. Then 'upon a voluntary beauty' specifies the reason for praise (al-mahmud 'alayhi). 'Voluntary' here means either actually, like praise for acts of will, or juridically, like praise for the essence and attributes of God because they are the source of voluntary acts. Thus, praise of something beautiful that is not voluntary (like a natural beauty) is not called 'hamd' but 'madih' (commendation).

The phrase 'in a manner of veneration' includes the word 'manner' to indicate that mere outward veneration is not enough; the speech must correspond to the heart. However, al-Sawi notes that some masters hold that this condition is not required.

Al-Sawi then explains 'whether it pertains to virtues or benefits': 'virtues' refer to intrinsic excellences (al-mazaya al-qasira) whose attribution does not depend on the transference of their effect to others, even if they are themselves transitive, like knowledge, power, and beauty. 'Benefits' are transitive excellences (al-mazaya al-muta'addiya) whose attribution depends on the transference of their effect to others, like generosity and teaching. This distinction is important because both types are valid objects of praise.

Finally, al-Sawi states that the pillars (arkan) of praise are five: the praiser (hamid), the praised (mahmud), that with which praise is made (mahmud bihi), the reason for praise (mahmud 'alayhi), and the form (sigha). He gives an example: if you praise Zayd because he honored you by saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable', then you are the praiser, Zayd is the praised, the honor is the reason, the affirmation of knowledge (the attribute) is that with which praise is made, and the utterance 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is the form.

The footnote adds nuance: the example combines linguistic and terminological praise. Linguistic praise can be without a blessing, while terminological praise requires a blessing. The example of standing up for Zayd on his arrival is terminological praise because it is an action, not speech.

Key Terms
الحمد
الثناء
الفضائل
الفواضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hamd is only for blessings (ni'mah) received from the praised.
Correction: The linguistic definition of hamd includes praise for virtues (intrinsic qualities) even if no benefit is received, as long as the quality is voluntary.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, which of the following is NOT a component of the linguistic definition of al-hamd?
A) Praise (al-thana')
B) With beauty (bi al-jamil)
C) Upon voluntary beauty (ala jamil ikhtiyari)
D) In return for a blessing (fi muqabil ni'mah)

Key Takeaway
The linguistic definition of al-hamd comprises five elements: praise, beauty, voluntary object, veneration, and inclusion of both virtues and benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah, do you praise Him only for the blessings He has given you, or also for His intrinsic virtues like His knowledge and power? Which do you neglect more, and what does that reveal about your heart?
?
The linguistic definition of hamd does not require your heart to be in a state of veneration—but is your praise of Allah ever truly complete without that inner reverence? When was the last time your tongue and heart were aligned in praising Him?
?
If hamd includes praise for virtues (like knowledge) that have no direct benefit to you, can you sincerely praise Allah for His attributes even when you are not experiencing His favors? Make a concrete commitment to praise Him for one specific attribute today, regardless of your circumstances.

Logic and Technical Terms
beginner
5 min
The Five Pillars (Arkan) of al-Hamd
Al-Sawi enumerates the five pillars of al-hamd and gives an example, then distinguishes linguistic and terminological praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the five pillars of al-hamd (praise) as articulated by al-Sawi, including an example and the distinction between linguistic and terminological praise.

Core Idea
The five pillars of hamd are the praiser, the praised, the attribute, the reason, and the form.

After defining al-hamd linguistically, al-Sawi enumerates its five pillars (arkan): the praiser, the praised, that with which praise is made, the reason for praise, and the form. He illustrates these with an example of praising Zayd for his honor, and then distinguishes linguistic praise (hamd lughawi) from terminological praise (hamd istilahi), which is always in response to a blessing. This lesson solidifies the practical structure of any act of praise.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage listing the five pillars.
Read the passage where al-Sawi states the five pillars and gives the example. Notice how each role is assigned.
2
Understand the example.
In the example, each pillar is identified: you (praiser), Zayd (praised), knowledge (attribute), honor (reason), and the statement (form). See how the components work together.
3
Distinguish between linguistic and terminological praise.
Al-Sawi's footnote explains that the example combines both types. Linguistic praise does not require a blessing, while terminological praise always does. Also, terminological praise can be an action (like standing up), not just speech.
4
Apply the framework to another scenario.
Think of a simple praise statement and identify its five pillars.

(۱) قوله : (وهي التي لا يتوقف تعلقها . . . . إلخ ) : أي : لا يتوقف الاتصاف بها على تعدي أثرها للغير؛ أي: سواء كانت في نفسها غير متعدية للغير؛ كالحسن، أو كانت في نفسها متعدية؛ كالعلم، وأما الفواضل ؛ فإن الاتصاف بها يتوقف على تعدي أثرها للغير ؛ كالكرم ،والتعليم فكل من الفواضل والفضائل متعلق الحمد عليه، وهو المحمود عليه، فالثناء على من اتصف بها ،حمد، سواء تعدى أثرها للغير حامداً أو غيره، كأن أعطى المحمود للحامد أو غيره . . . أو علمه أو غيره مسألة أو لا، كان حمده لشجاعته ؛ فإنها اختياري لم يتعد أثره للحامد وغيره . اهـ كاتبه. (۲) قوله: زيد) عالم هذا مثال فيه اجتماع الحمد اللغوي والاصطلاحي، وينفرد اللغوي فيما كان لا في مقابلة نعمة هذا المثال، والمحمود عليه الشجاعة، والحمد الاصطلاحي لا بد أن يكون في مقابلة، سواء كانت على الحامد أو غيره، وينفرد الاصطلاحي في القيام لزيد عند قدومه ؛ لكونه أكرم الحامد أو غيره؛ لأن اللغوي لا يكون إلا باللسان وهذا عمل بالأركان اهـ كاتبه
And al-hamd linguistically is: praise (al-thana') with beauty upon a voluntary beauty in a manner of veneration, whether it pertains to virtues (al-fada'il) or benefits (al-fawadil). The commentator confined himself to it. His saying: (linguistically) is accusative as specification. His saying: (it is al-thana'): with the prefixed letter tha and noon and lengthening: remembrance with good; with noon prefixed to tha and shortening, its opposite. Then his saying: (with beauty) is a description and with prefixed explicative like 'I looked with my eye' and 'I heard with my ear'; what is meant by it is that which emanates from speech, whether ancient or originated, so it includes the four divisions of praise. His saying: (with beauty) is an explanation of what is praised with (al-mahmud bihi) and the form (al-sigha) emanating from the praiser to the praised. His saying: (upon a voluntary beauty) is an explanation of the reason for praise (al-mahmud 'alayhi); what is meant by voluntary is either actually, like praise upon attributes of acts, or juridically, like praise upon the essence and its attributes, because they are the source of voluntary acts; thus excluded is that which is beautiful but not voluntary, so praise upon it is commendation (madih). His saying: (in a manner of veneration): the word 'manner' (jiha) is inserted to indicate that mere apparent veneration is not sufficient in praise, rather the speech must correspond to the heart; thus it is said; but the masters (ashyakh) said: the preponderant view is that it is not a condition. His saying: (whether it pertains to virtues) : 'whether' is a predicate introduced, and what follows is in the interpretation of a source as a delayed subject; the meaning is: its connection to virtues or benefits is equal. And what is meant by virtues are the intrinsic excellences (al-mazaya al-qasira) which are those whose attribution does not depend on the transference of their effect to others, even if they are themselves transitive, like knowledge, power, and beauty; and by benefits are the transitive excellences (al-mazaya al-muta'addiya) which are those whose attribution depends on the transference of their effect to others, like generosity and teaching. This phrase is the meaning of another's saying: whether it is in return for a blessing or not. So it results that the pillars (arkan) of praise are five: the praiser (hamid), the praised (mahmud), that with which praise is made (mahmud bihi), the reason for which praise is made (mahmud 'alayhi), and the form (sigha). So if you praise Zayd because he honored you by saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable', then you are the praiser, Zayd is the praised, the honoring is the reason for praise, i.e., that for which he is praised, the affirmation of knowledge which is the implication of your saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is that with which praise is made, and your saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is the form. (1) His saying: (which are those whose attribution does not depend... etc.): i.e., their attribution does not depend on the transference of their effect to others; i.e., whether they are in themselves non-transitive to others, like beauty, or they are in themselves transitive, like knowledge. As for the benefits, their attribution depends on the transference of their effect to others, like generosity and teaching. So both benefits and virtues are related to the reason for praise, which is the reason for praise. So praise upon one who is described with them is praise (hamd), whether the effect transfers to the praiser or to another, such as if the praised gave something to the praiser or to another... or taught him or another a question or not, his praise for his courage; for it is voluntary and its effect does not transfer to the praiser or to another. End of the writer's words. (2) His saying: 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is an example in which linguistic and terminological praise combine. The linguistic praise stands alone in what is not in return for a blessing in this example, and the reason for praise is courage. Terminological praise must be in return for a blessing, whether it was upon the praiser or another. The terminological praise stands alone in standing up for Zayd upon his arrival because he honored the praiser or another; for the linguistic cannot be except with the tongue, and this is an action with the pillars. End of the writer's words.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the linguistic meaning of 'al-hamd' (praise), followed by an enumeration of its five pillars (arkan). The commentator al-Sawi begins by quoting the definition from the original text: 'al-hamd linguistically is praise (al-thana') with beauty upon a voluntary beauty in a manner of veneration, whether it pertains to virtues (al-fada'il) or benefits (al-fawadil).' He then proceeds to explain each term in the definition.

First, he clarifies that 'linguistically' is accusative as a specification (tamyiz). Then he explains 'al-thana': it is remembrance with good, and its opposite is 'al-dhamm' (blame). The phrase 'with beauty' is a description of the praise; the expression is analogous to 'I looked with my eye' where the word is explicative. What is meant by 'beauty' is that which emanates from speech, whether the speech is eternal (God's speech) or temporal (human speech), so it includes the four types of praise (praise of God for His essence, His attributes, His acts, and praise of others).

Next, he explains 'with beauty' as specifying that with which praise is made (al-mahmud bihi) and the form (al-sigha) emanating from the praiser to the praised. Then 'upon a voluntary beauty' specifies the reason for praise (al-mahmud 'alayhi). 'Voluntary' here means either actually, like praise for acts of will, or juridically, like praise for the essence and attributes of God because they are the source of voluntary acts. Thus, praise of something beautiful that is not voluntary (like a natural beauty) is not called 'hamd' but 'madih' (commendation).

The phrase 'in a manner of veneration' includes the word 'manner' to indicate that mere outward veneration is not enough; the speech must correspond to the heart. However, al-Sawi notes that some masters hold that this condition is not required.

Al-Sawi then explains 'whether it pertains to virtues or benefits': 'virtues' refer to intrinsic excellences (al-mazaya al-qasira) whose attribution does not depend on the transference of their effect to others, even if they are themselves transitive, like knowledge, power, and beauty. 'Benefits' are transitive excellences (al-mazaya al-muta'addiya) whose attribution depends on the transference of their effect to others, like generosity and teaching. This distinction is important because both types are valid objects of praise.

Finally, al-Sawi states that the pillars (arkan) of praise are five: the praiser (hamid), the praised (mahmud), that with which praise is made (mahmud bihi), the reason for praise (mahmud 'alayhi), and the form (sigha). He gives an example: if you praise Zayd because he honored you by saying 'Zayd is knowledgeable', then you are the praiser, Zayd is the praised, the honor is the reason, the affirmation of knowledge (the attribute) is that with which praise is made, and the utterance 'Zayd is knowledgeable' is the form.

The footnote adds nuance: the example combines linguistic and terminological praise. Linguistic praise can be without a blessing, while terminological praise requires a blessing. The example of standing up for Zayd on his arrival is terminological praise because it is an action, not speech.

Key Terms

حامد
محمود
محمود به
محمود عليه
صيغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All praise is hamd, and hamd is only gratitude.
Correction: Linguistic hamd does not require gratitude; it can be for any voluntary beauty. Terminological hamd does require a blessing. Also, praise for non-voluntary beauty is madih, not hamd.

Checkpoint
In the example of praising Zayd for his honor, what is the 'mahmud bihi' (that with which praise is made)?
A) The honor
B) The knowledge
C) The statement 'Zayd is knowledgeable'
D) Zayd himself

Key Takeaway
The five pillars of hamd provide a complete framework for analyzing any act of praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah, which of the five pillars are you most aware of? Which do you tend to neglect? How can you make your praise more complete?
?
Think of a time you praised someone for a worldly reason. Were you focusing on the reason (mahmud 'alayhi) or the attribute (mahmud bihi)? How does this distinction affect the sincerity of your praise?
?
Al-Sawi says terminological praise can be through actions. When was the last time your actions praised Allah? What specific action can you do today to praise Him in return for His blessings?
