Definition of Hamd and Its Three Modes
Definition of hamd and its three modes with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of hamd (praise) according to the commentator, including its three modes: speech, belief, and bodily service.

Core Idea
Hamd is magnifying the Benefactor in three ways: tongue, heart, and limbs.

Sanusi begins his commentary by unpacking the rich definition of hamd. He explains that hamd is not just verbal praise but can be performed with belief and even physical actions. This lesson will walk through the precise conditions laid out by the commentator, including a story that beautifully illustrates the three modes of thanks.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of hamd
Start by reading the opening sentence that defines hamd as 'informing about magnifying the Benefactor because He is a benefactor.' Notice the three modes mentioned: saying, belief, and service.
The definition includes a condition: it applies even if the praise is not from the one being praised.
2
Understand the explanation of 'yumbi'
The commentator clarifies that 'yumbi' means informing others, not just a private heart feeling. This refines the definition.
3
Examine the belief mode
An objection is raised that belief is a quality, not an act. The answer is that 'act' here includes qualities because it contrasts with passivity.
This shows the flexibility of the term 'act' in this context.
4
Study the story of the Bedouin
Read the story of the Bedouin and Imam Ali, which demonstrates the three modes of thanks (hand, tongue, hidden conscience). This serves as a concrete example.
Though the story is about shukr, it illustrates the three modes mentioned for hamd.
5
Reflect on the three modes
After the story, take a moment to see that hamd can be expressed through multiple channels, not just words.

حاشية الصاوي على شرح الخريدة البهية يُنبئ عن تعظيم المُنعِم بسبب كونه مُنعماً، ولو على غير الحامد، وسواء كان الفعل قولاً باللسان أو اعتقاداً بالجنان أو خدمة بالأركان . فبينهما العموم والخصوص الوجهي؛ ولساني والضمير المحجبا أفادتـكـم الـنَّـعـمـاء مـنـي قوله : (فبينهما العموم والخصوص (الوجهي): ضابطه أنَّهما يجتمعان في مادة، وينفرد كل منهما عن الآخر بجهة . معنى الحمد لأنَّ مورد اللغوي خاص وهو اللسان، ومتعلقه عام، ومورد العرفي عام، ومتعلقه خاص وهو الإنعام. وأَمَّا الشُّكر لغةٌ : فهو الحمد عرفاً ، وأَمَّا الشكر عرفاً : فهو صَرْف العبد جميعَ ما أنعم الله به عليه من عقل وسمع وغيرهما إلى ما خُلق لأجله، وهو أخص مطلقاً الحمد والشكر اللُّغوي لاختصاصه بالله تعالى وبكونه في مقابلة النّعَم التي على الشاكر فقط . قوله : (لأنَّ مورد اللُّغوي خاص إلخ) : تعليل لما قبله، والمراد بالمورد المبدأ، وبالمتعلق المنتهي . قوله : (فهو الحمد عرفاً : أي : فبينهما التّرادف، وإِنَّما يختلفان في التسمية. قوله: (وهو أخصُّ مطلقاً :أي: فبينـه وبـيـن مـا عـداه الـعـمـوم والخصوص المطلق، فيلزم من الشكر Hashiyat al-Sawi on the explanation of al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya: It informs about magnifying the Benefactor because He is a benefactor, even if [the praise] is not from the praiser, and whether the act is a saying with the tongue, a belief with the heart, or a service with the limbs. So between them is general and specific in one respect. His statement: 'yumbi' (informs): meaning it informs the one who does not praise if he were to become aware of it, so it is not objected that this indication might be with the heart. His statement: 'even if not from the praiser': meaning that it is not required that the blessing be for the praiser himself; rather the criterion is that it be in return for a blessing. His statement: 'or a belief with the heart': if you say: belief is not an act of the heart but rather a quality of it. The answer: that what is meant by 'act' here is what is opposite to passivity, so it includes the quality. It is narrated that... His statement: 'with the limbs': what is meant by them are the external organs other than the tongue. A bedouin came to Ali, may Allah honor his face, and he gave him a dirham, and when he considered it too little and he had nothing but his armor, he handed it over to him, so he praised him saying: 'And my thanks were not sufficient for your beauty, but I sought a method in thanks: three things: my hand, my tongue, and the hidden conscience. The blessings from you have benefited me.' His statement: 'so between them is general and specific in one respect': its rule is that they meet in one matter, and each is separate from the other in one aspect. The meaning of hamd: because the subject of the linguistic [hamd] is specific, namely the tongue, and its object is general; and the subject of the conventional [hamd] is general, and its object is specific, namely bestowal of blessings. As for shukr linguistically: it is hamd conventionally. And as for shukr conventionally: it is the servant's directing all that Allah has bestowed upon him – of intellect, hearing, and other things – to that for which they were created; and it is absolutely more specific than hamd and linguistic shukr because it is exclusive to Allah, may He be exalted, and because it is in return for blessings that are upon the one thanking only. His statement: 'because the subject of the linguistic is specific, etc.': an explanation of what preceded; what is meant by 'subject' is the starting point, and by 'object' is the endpoint. His statement: 'so it is hamd conventionally': meaning that between them is synonymy, they only differ in nomenclature. His statement: 'and it is absolutely more specific': meaning that between it and what is other than it is absolute generality and specificity; thus from conventional shukr follows linguistic and conventional hamd and linguistic shukr, but not the reverse; rather the three are individually more general than it in one aspect. His statement: 'because it is exclusive to Allah, etc.': an explanation of its being more specific; its meaning: that directing the limbs to their Creator is impossible to be for other than Allah. His statement: 'and because it is, etc.': a second reason for its being more specific. The conclusion: that the commentary mentioned linguistic and conventional hamd, and linguistic and conventional shukr, and did not mention madh with its two divisions; we mention it to complete the benefit. So madh linguistically: it is praise with the tongue for a non-volitional attribute; and conventionally: it is an action.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the definitions and relationships between the terms 'hamd' (praise), 'shukr' (thanks), and 'madh' (praise for a non-volitional attribute). The author begins by clarifying that 'yumbi' (informs) conveys information to a non-praiser if he were to become aware of it, so it is not just a heart feeling. He then notes that the blessing need not be for the praiser himself; the praise is simply in response to a blessing. On the phrase 'belief with the heart', an objection is raised that belief is a quality, not an act. The answer is that 'act' here includes qualities because it contrasts with passivity. A story about a bedouin and Imam Ali illustrates practical gratitude with hands, tongue, and conscience. The commentator then explains the relationship between hamd and shukr: linguistic hamd has a specific source (tongue) and general object; conventional hamd has a general source and specific object (bestowal of blessings). Linguistic shukr is identical to conventional hamd (synonymous in terminology). Conventional shukr is absolutely more specific than both hamd and linguistic shukr because it is exclusively for Allah and in return for blessings on the thankful person. The commentary then elaborates: the subject (mawrid) and object (muta‘allaq) of each term are analyzed, showing that conventional shukr implies the others but not vice versa. Finally, the commentator adds a definition of 'madh' (praise for a non-volitional attribute) since the original text omitted it. The passage is a detailed lexical and technical analysis, typical of kalam works in defining terms precisely, and it guards against conflating different types of praise and thanks.

Correction: Hamd includes belief with the heart and physical service, as the passage states: 'whether the act is a saying with the tongue, a belief with the heart, or a service with the limbs.'

Key Takeaway
Hamd is magnifying the Benefactor through tongue, heart, or limbs, and it is not limited to verbal praise or restricted to the recipient of the blessing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you thank Allah, do you engage all three modes—tongue, heart, and limbs? Identify one blessing today for which you will express hamd with all three, and do it.
?
Think of a recent blessing someone else received. Did you genuinely magnify the Benefactor for it, or did you feel a twinge of envy? Repent and commit to praising Allah for others' gifts as if they were your own.
?
Your limbs serve your desires often. Will you dedicate one physical action today—like a prostration of gratitude or helping someone—as an act of hamd, making your body praise Allah alongside your tongue?

Relationship Between Hamd and Shukr
Explanation of the relationship between hamd and shukr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relationship between hamd and shukr as 'general and specific in one respect', and the definitions of shukr linguistically and conventionally.

Core Idea
Hamd and shukr share a 'general and specific in one respect' relationship.

Having defined hamd, the commentator now clarifies how it relates to shukr. He introduces the logical relationship of 'general and specific in one respect' (عموم وخصوص وجهي) and then defines shukr both linguistically (as synonymous with conventional hamd) and conventionally (as directing all blessings to their intended purpose). This distinction is crucial for understanding later theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement on the relationship
Read the commentator's statement that between hamd and shukr is 'general and specific in one respect'. Then read the rule that they meet in one matter and are separate in another.
This is a logical relation often used in kalam.
2
Analyze the rule for hamd
The commentator explains that the subject (source) of linguistic hamd is specific (tongue), while its object is general. The subject of conventional hamd is general, and its object is specific (bestowal of blessings). This shows the 'one respect' of overlap and difference.
3
Define shukr linguistically
Linguistic shukr is defined as 'conventional hamd', meaning they are synonymous in terminology.
This establishes a synonymy in one sense.
4
Define shukr conventionally
Conventional shukr is the servant's directing all of Allah's blessings (intellect, hearing, etc.) to their purpose. It is absolutely more specific than both hamd and linguistic shukr because it is exclusively for Allah and in return for blessings on the thankful person.
Note the condition of exclusivity to Allah.
5
Summarize the relationships
Reflect on how the four terms (linguistic hamd, conventional hamd, linguistic shukr, conventional shukr) are related. Use the key: conventional shukr is the most specific.

قوله : (ينبئ) : أي : يخبر غير الحامد لو اطلع عليه، فلا يرد أنَّ هذا الإشعار قد يكون بالقلب . قوله : (ولو على غير الحامد) أي فلا يشترط أن تكون النعمة لنفس الحامد، وإنما المدار على كونه في مقابلة نعمة. قوله : (أو اعتقاداً بالجنان): إن قلت : الاعتقاد ليس فعلاً للقلب وإنَّما هو كيفية له . أجيب بأنَّ المراد بالفعل هنا ما قابل الانفعال فيشمل الكيف روي أنَّ قوله : (بالأركان) : المراد بها الأعضاء الظاهرة غير اللسان، أعرابيّاً أتى عليّاً كرَّم الله وجهه فأعطاه درهماً ، فلما استقله ولم يكن عنده غير درع له ناوله إيَّاه، فمدحه بقوله : وما كان شكري وافياً بجمالكم ولكنني حاولت في الشكر مذهبا ثلاثةً يدي ولساني والضمير المحجبا أفادتـكـم الـنَّـعـمـاء مـنـي قوله : (فبينهما العموم والخصوص (الوجهي): ضابطه أنَّهما يجتمعان في مادة، وينفرد كل منهما عن الآخر بجهة . معنى الحمد لأنَّ مورد اللغوي خاص وهو اللسان، ومتعلقه عام، ومورد العرفي عام، ومتعلقه خاص وهو الإنعام. وأَمَّا الشُّكر لغةٌ : فهو الحمد عرفاً ، وأَمَّا الشكر عرفاً : فهو صَرْف العبد جميعَ ما أنعم الله به عليه من عقل وسمع وغيرهما إلى ما خُلق لأجله، وهو أخص مطلقاً الحمد والشكر اللُّغوي لاختصاصه بالله تعالى وبكونه في مقابلة النّعَم التي على الشاكر فقط . قوله : (لأنَّ مورد اللُّغوي خاص إلخ) : تعليل لما قبله، والمراد بالمورد المبدأ، وبالمتعلق المنتهي . قوله : (فهو الحمد عرفاً : أي : فبينهما التّرادف، وإِنَّما يختلفان في التسمية. قوله: (وهو أخصُّ مطلقاً :أي: فبينـه وبـيـن مـا عـداه الـعـمـوم والخصوص المطلق، فيلزم من الشكر الاصطلاحي الحمد اللغوي والعرفي والشكر اللُّغوي ولا عكس، بل تنفرد الثلاثة عنه بجهة عمومها . قوله: (لاختصاصه بالله ... إلخ): تعليل لأخصيَّته، ومعناه: أنَّ صرف الأعضاء لخالقها يستحيل أن يكون لغير الله . قوله : (وبكونه ... إلخ): علة ثانية لأخصيَّته، والحاصل : أنَّ الشَّرح ذكر الحمد اللغوي والعرفي، والشكر اللُّغوي والعرفي، ولم يذكر المدح بقسميه، ونذكره تتميماً للفائدة. فالمدح لغة : هو الثَّناء باللسان على وصف غير اختياري، وعرفاً : فعل
Hashiyat al-Sawi on the explanation of al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya: It informs about magnifying the Benefactor because He is a benefactor, even if [the praise] is not from the praiser, and whether the act is a saying with the tongue, a belief with the heart, or a service with the limbs. So between them is general and specific in one respect. His statement: 'yumbi' (informs): meaning it informs the one who does not praise if he were to become aware of it, so it is not objected that this indication might be with the heart. His statement: 'even if not from the praiser': meaning that it is not required that the blessing be for the praiser himself; rather the criterion is that it be in return for a blessing. His statement: 'or a belief with the heart': if you say: belief is not an act of the heart but rather a quality of it. The answer: that what is meant by 'act' here is what is opposite to passivity, so it includes the quality. It is narrated that... His statement: 'with the limbs': what is meant by them are the external organs other than the tongue. A bedouin came to Ali, may Allah honor his face, and he gave him a dirham, and when he considered it too little and he had nothing but his armor, he handed it over to him, so he praised him saying: 'And my thanks were not sufficient for your beauty, but I sought a method in thanks: three things: my hand, my tongue, and the hidden conscience. The blessings from you have benefited me.' His statement: 'so between them is general and specific in one respect': its rule is that they meet in one matter, and each is separate from the other in one aspect. The meaning of hamd: because the subject of the linguistic [hamd] is specific, namely the tongue, and its object is general; and the subject of the conventional [hamd] is general, and its object is specific, namely bestowal of blessings. As for shukr linguistically: it is hamd conventionally. And as for shukr conventionally: it is the servant's directing all that Allah has bestowed upon him – of intellect, hearing, and other things – to that for which they were created; and it is absolutely more specific than hamd and linguistic shukr because it is exclusive to Allah, may He be exalted, and because it is in return for blessings that are upon the one thanking only. His statement: 'because the subject of the linguistic is specific, etc.': an explanation of what preceded; what is meant by 'subject' is the starting point, and by 'object' is the endpoint. His statement: 'so it is hamd conventionally': meaning that between them is synonymy, they only differ in nomenclature. His statement: 'and it is absolutely more specific': meaning that between it and what is other than it is absolute generality and specificity; thus from conventional shukr follows linguistic and conventional hamd and linguistic shukr, but not the reverse; rather the three are individually more general than it in one aspect. His statement: 'because it is exclusive to Allah, etc.': an explanation of its being more specific; its meaning: that directing the limbs to their Creator is impossible to be for other than Allah. His statement: 'and because it is, etc.': a second reason for its being more specific. The conclusion: that the commentary mentioned linguistic and conventional hamd, and linguistic and conventional shukr, and did not mention madh with its two divisions; we mention it to complete the benefit. So madh linguistically: it is praise with the tongue for a non-volitional attribute; and conventionally: it is an action.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining the definitions and relationships between the terms 'hamd' (praise), 'shukr' (thanks), and 'madh' (praise for a non-volitional attribute). The author begins by clarifying that 'yumbi' (informs) conveys information to a non-praiser if he were to become aware of it, so it is not just a heart feeling. He then notes that the blessing need not be for the praiser himself; the praise is simply in response to a blessing. On the phrase 'belief with the heart', an objection is raised that belief is a quality, not an act. The answer is that 'act' here includes qualities because it contrasts with passivity. A story about a bedouin and Imam Ali illustrates practical gratitude with hands, tongue, and conscience. The commentator then explains the relationship between hamd and shukr: linguistic hamd has a specific source (tongue) and general object; conventional hamd has a general source and specific object (bestowal of blessings). Linguistic shukr is identical to conventional hamd (synonymous in terminology). Conventional shukr is absolutely more specific than both hamd and linguistic shukr because it is exclusively for Allah and in return for blessings on the thankful person. The commentary then elaborates: the subject (mawrid) and object (muta‘allaq) of each term are analyzed, showing that conventional shukr implies the others but not vice versa. Finally, the commentator adds a definition of 'madh' (praise for a non-volitional attribute) since the original text omitted it. The passage is a detailed lexical and technical analysis, typical of kalam works in defining terms precisely, and it guards against conflating different types of praise and thanks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hamd and shukr are completely synonymous.
Correction: They overlap but have distinct scopes: conventional shukr is more specific, being exclusive to Allah and requiring gratitude for blessings.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between conventional shukr and linguistic hamd according to the passage?
A) They are identical.
B) Conventional shukr is absolutely more specific.
C) Linguistic hamd is more specific.
D) They are opposites.

Key Takeaway
Hamd and shukr are related as 'general and specific in one respect', and conventional shukr is the most specific term, reserved for Allah and tied to personal blessings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find that your own 'hamd' (praise) is often just on your tongue (linguistic) but not accompanied by action? What blessings has Allah given you that you are not directing to their intended purpose? Write down one specific blessing and commit to using it for what it was created for this week.
?
The passage says conventional shukr is 'absolutely more specific' and exclusive to Allah. When you say 'alhamdulillah', are you truly returning gratitude for that specific blessing, or is it a habit? If Allah took away that blessing tomorrow, would you still praise Him with the same heart? Examine your attachment.
?
Conventional shukr requires using intellect, hearing, and other gifts for their created purpose. How have you used your intellect today? Have you used your hearing for anything that displeases Allah? If you are honest, what change must you make tonight before you sleep?

Further Specificity of Shukr and Definition of Madh
Explanation of reasons for specificity and definition of madh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasons why conventional shukr is absolutely more specific, and the definition of madh (praise for a non-volitional attribute) as an additional term.

Core Idea
Conventional shukr is absolutely specific because it is only for Allah and only for blessings on the shakir; madh is praise for an involuntary trait.

The commentator now delves deeper into the reasons behind the absolute specificity of conventional shukr. He explains that it is exclusive to Allah because directing limbs to the Creator cannot be for other than Him, and it only responds to blessings upon the thankful person. Finally, he supplements the discussion by defining madh, a term not covered earlier, which refers to praise for a non-volitional attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary on the reasons
Begin by reading the commentator's explanation of the two reasons for the absolute specificity of conventional shukr: it is exclusively for Allah and it is in return for blessings on the shakir.
2
Understand the first reason
Directing limbs to their Creator is impossible for other than Allah, so conventional shukr is specific to Allah.
3
Understand the second reason
Conventional shukr is only in return for blessings that the shakir himself received, so it is narrower in scope.
4
Read the addition of madh
The commentator notes that the original text only covered hamd and shukr, so he adds madh. Read its linguistic and conventional definitions.
5
Compare madh with hamd
Notice that madh is distinct because it is for non-volitional attributes, whereas hamd is for volitional actions of beneficence.
This completes the set of terms.

?

?

?

Definitions of Hamd, Shukr, and Madh
Defines hamd, shukr, and madh with their linguistic and customary meanings and relationships.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct meanings and relationships between the terms hamd, shukr, and madh in both their linguistic and customary senses.

Core Idea
Hamd, shukr, and madh are not synonyms; each has a specific scope and application.

In this lesson, we explore three key terms of praise in Islamic theology: hamd, shukr, and madh. The author carefully distinguishes each term in its linguistic and customary usages, revealing a precise logical relationship between them. Understanding these distinctions is essential for grasping later theological discussions about divine attributes and human responses to blessings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a very compressed style.
2
Identify the three terms
Find the definitions of hamd, shukr, and madh in both their linguistic and customary senses. Note how each term is introduced.
3
Understand the relationships
Pay attention to how the author compares the terms, especially the statement that customary shukr is 'absolutely more specific' than the others. Grasp the logical hierarchy.
This is the core insight of the passage.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself why these precise distinctions matter for theology. Consider how misusing these terms could lead to conceptual confusion about praise and gratitude to Allah.
This connects to the learning objective.

معنى الحمد لأنَّ مورد اللغوي خاص وهو اللسان، ومتعلقه عام، ومورد العرفي عام، ومتعلقه خاص وهو الإنعام. وأمَّا الشُّكر لغةٌ : فهو الحمد عرفاً ، وأَمَّا الشكر عرفاً : فهو صَرْف العبد جميعَ ما أنعم الله به عليه من عقل وسمع وغيرهما إلى ما خُلق لأجله، وهو أخص مطلقاً الحمد والشكر اللُّغوي لاختصاصه بالله تعالى وبكونه في مقابلة النّعَم التي على الشاكر فقط . قوله : (لأنَّ مورد اللُّغوي خاص إلخ) : تعليل لما قبله، والمراد بالمورد المبدأ، وبالمتعلق المنتهي . قوله : (فهو الحمد عرفاً : أي : فبينهما التّرادف، وإِنَّما يختلفان في التسمية. قوله: (وهو أخصُّ مطلقاً :أي: فبينـه وبـيـن مـا عـداه الـعـمـوم والخصوص المطلق، فيلزم من الشكر الاصطلاحي الحمد اللغوي والعرفي والشكر اللُّغوي ولا عكس، بل تنفرد الثلاثة عنه بجهة عمومها . قوله: (لاختصاصه بالله ... إلخ): تعليل لأخصيَّته، ومعناه: أنَّ صرف الأعضاء لخالقها يستحيل أن يكون لغير الله . قوله : (وبكونه ... إلخ): علة ثانية لأخصيَّته، والحاصل : أنَّ الشَّرح ذكر الحمد اللغوي والعرفي، والشكر اللُّغوي والعرفي، ولم يذكر المدح بقسميه، ونذكره تتميماً للفائدة. فالمدح لغة : هو الثناء باللسان على وصف غير اختياري، وعرفاً : فعل (العليّ) من العُلُوّ، وهو الرّفعة، فأصله: عليو، اجتمعت الياء والواو، وسبقت إحداهما بالسكون ينبئ عن تعظيم الشَّخص بسبب اتصافه بصفة كمال
The meaning of al-hamd: Because the source of the linguistic [meaning] is specific, namely the tongue, and its object is general; and the source of the customary [meaning] is general, and its object is specific, namely beneficence. As for al-shukr linguistically: it is al-hamd customarily. And as for al-shukr customarily: it is the servant's directing all that Allah has bestowed upon him of intellect, hearing, and others to that for which they were created, and it is absolutely more specific than linguistic al-hamd and al-shukr due to its being exclusively for Allah Most High and because it is in return for the blessings that are only upon the one giving thanks. He said: (Because the source of the linguistic is specific etc.): explanation of what preceded, and by 'source' is meant the starting point, and by 'object' the end point. He said: (So it is al-hamd customarily): meaning they are synonymous, they only differ in nomenclature. He said: (And it is absolutely more specific): meaning between it and what is other than it is absolute generality and particularity; so from technical al-shukr necessarily follow linguistic and customary al-hamd and linguistic al-shukr, and not vice versa; rather, the three are unique in their breadth. He said: (Due to its being exclusively for Allah etc.): explanation of its being more specific, and its meaning: that directing the organs to their Creator cannot be for other than Allah. He said: (And because it is etc.): second reason for its being more specific. The conclusion: that the Sharh mentioned linguistic and customary al-hamd, and linguistic and customary al-shukr, and did not mention al-madh in its two divisions, and we mention it to complete the benefit. So al-madh linguistically: it is praise with the tongue of a non-volitional attribute, and customarily: an action (al-'Aliyy) from al-'uluww, which is elevation; its original form is 'aliyu, the ya' and waw met, and one of them is preceded by a sukun, indicating veneration of the person due to his being characterized by a quality of perfection.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes the terms hamd, shukr, and madh in their linguistic and customary senses. The author begins by explaining the difference between the linguistic and customary meanings of hamd: the linguistic meaning has a specific source (the tongue) but a general object, while the customary meaning has a general source (any act or saying indicating praise) but a specific object (beneficence). Then shukr is defined: linguistically it is synonymous with customary hamd, but customarily it is the servant's use of all God's gifts for their intended purpose, making it absolutely more specific than both linguistic hamd and linguistic shukr. The reasons for its specificity are that it is exclusively for God and in return for blessings. The passage then clarifies that technical shukr entails the other three terms, but not vice versa. Finally, madh is introduced: linguistically it is praise of a non-volitional attribute, and customarily it is an action indicating veneration of a person based on perfection. The passage also includes a brief morphological note on the word al-'Aliyy, explaining its root and derivation.

?

Six Terms and Fifteen Logical Relationships
Enumerates the six terms and explains how to derive fifteen logical relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the six terms (shukr, hamd, madh in their linguistic and customary senses) give rise to fifteen logical relationships, and how to derive these relationships through systematic comparison.

Core Idea
Six terms produce fifteen relationships through pairwise comparison.

In this passage, the author presents a systematic count of the logical relationships among six key terms. By carefully stepping through each term and comparing it with the others, we can see how the total of fifteen relationships emerges. This exercise sharpens our ability to see the structure of theological concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Focus on understanding the overall counting process.
The passage uses terms like 'absolute generality' and 'generality from one aspect'—just note them for now.
2
Identify the six terms
Notice that the terms are al-shukr, al-hamd, and al-madh, each in two forms: linguistic and customary. This gives six distinct terms.
Technical al-hamd is mentioned as a separate category within linguistic shukr's comparisons.
3
Follow the counting procedure
The author takes each term in turn and compares it with the remaining terms, counting the relationships. Start with customary shukr, then linguistic shukr, then linguistic hamd, then customary hamd, then linguistic madh.
The relationships are either synonymy, absolute generality, or generality from one aspect.
4
Sum the relationships
Add up the relationships from each step: 5 (customary shukr) + 4 (linguistic shukr) + 3 (linguistic hamd) + 2 (customary hamd) + 1 (linguistic madh) = 15.
This confirms the total of fifteen.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider why the author ends with 'Reflect.' The method ensures no relationship is counted twice. This systematic approach is a hallmark of logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمجموعها ستّة من ضرب ثلاثة؛ وهي الشكر والحمد والمدح في اثنين، وهما اللُّغوي والعرفي ؛ والنَّسب بينها خمسة عشر، وذلك لأنَّك تأخذ الشكر العرفي مع كل واحد يحصل خمس نسب هي العموم والخصوص المطلق. وتأخذ الشُّكر اللُّغوي مع غير الشُّكر العرفي يحصل أربع نسب، فإن الحمد الاصطلاحي؛ فالتّرادف، وإن كان الحمد اللُّغوي أو المدح اللُّغويّ؛ فالعموم والخصوص من وجه، وإن كان مع المدح العرفيّ ؛ فالعموم والخصوص المطلق. كان مع وتأخذ الحمد اللُّغوي مع غير الشُّكر بقسميه يحصل ثلاث نسب، فإن كان مع الحمد العرفي؛ فالعموم والخصوص الوجهي، وإن كان مع المدح بقسميه ؛ فالعموم والخصوص المطلق . وتأخذ الحمد العرفي مع غير الشُّكر بقسميه، والحمد اللُّغوي يحصل نسبتان هما العموم والخصوص المطلق . وتأخذ المدح اللغوي مع العرفي، وبينهما العموم والخصوص المطلق، تأمل .
So their total is six, from the product of three [terms] in two: namely al-shukr, al-hamd, and al-madh in [their] two [forms], i.e. linguistic and customary; and the relationships between them are fifteen. That is because if you take customary al-shukr with each one, there result five relationships: absolute generality and particularity. And if you take linguistic al-shukr with other than customary al-shukr, there result four relationships: if it is with technical al-hamd, then synonymy; if it is with linguistic al-hamd or linguistic al-madh, then generality and particularity from one aspect; if it is with customary al-madh, then absolute generality and particularity. ... And if you take linguistic al-hamd with other than al-shukr in its two divisions, there result three relationships: if it is with customary al-hamd, then generality and particularity from one aspect; if it is with al-madh in its two divisions, then absolute generality and particularity. And if you take customary al-hamd with other than al-shukr in its two divisions and linguistic al-hamd, there result two relationships: absolute generality and particularity. And if you take linguistic al-madh with customary, between them is absolute generality and particularity. Reflect.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage classifies the six terms resulting from the combination of the three root concepts (shukr, hamd, madh) with two categories (linguistic and customary). It then enumerates the logical relationships between them, which total fifteen. The author explains how to count these relationships by taking each term and comparing it with others. Starting with customary shukr, it yields five relationships of absolute generality/particularity with each of the other five terms. Then linguistic shukr yields four relationships when compared to terms other than customary shukr, depending on the type of relationship (synonymy, one-aspect generality, or absolute generality). Similarly, linguistic hamd yields three relationships, customary hamd yields two, and linguistic madh with customary madh yields one. The passage ends with an exhortation to reflect.

The Fifteen Logical Relations Between Shukr, Hamd, and Madh
The author enumerates the fifteen relative relationships between linguistic and conventional variants of shukr, hamd, and madh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three terms shukr, hamd, and madh, each in their linguistic and conventional senses, yield fifteen distinct logical relations including synonymy, absolute generality-particularity, and generality-particularity in some respect.

Core Idea
Three terms, two senses each, fifteen logical relations: precision in theological language.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin presents a meticulous logical taxonomy of three key terms: shukr (gratitude), hamd (praise), and madh (commendation). Each term appears in a linguistic and a conventional sense, creating six concepts. The author then systematically counts the relations between them, revealing fifteen distinct logical relationships. This exercise demonstrates the precision required in theological discourse and the importance of understanding terms in their proper contexts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the six concepts
Recognize that shukr, hamd, and madh each have a linguistic and a conventional meaning, giving six distinct concepts.
Linguistic meaning is literal; conventional meaning is technical in Islamic theology.
2
Grasp the logical relations
Learn the three types of relations: absolute generality-particularity (al-umum wa al-khusus al-mutlaq), generality-particularity in some respect (al-wajhi), and synonymy (al-taraduf).
These are standard relations in logic.
3
Follow the enumeration
Walk through the author's counting method step by step: starting with customary shukr, then linguistic shukr, then linguistic hamd, then customary hamd, then linguistic madh.
Pay attention to which pairs yield which relation.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why such precise classification matters for theology — it shows that terms have specific meanings and relations, avoiding confusion in discourse about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينبئ عن تعظيم الشَّخص بسبب اتصافه بصفة كمال، فمجموعها ستّة من ضرب ثلاثة؛ وهي الشكر والحمد والمدح في اثنين، وهما اللُّغوي ، والعرفي ؛ والنَّسب بينها خمسة عشر، وذلك لأنَّك تأخذ الشكر العرفي مع كل واحد يحصل خمس نسب هي العموم والخصوص المطلق. وتأخذ الشُّكر اللُّغوي مع غير الشُّكر العرفي يحصل أربع نسب، فإن الحمد الاصطلاحي؛ فالتّرادف، وإن كان الحمد اللُّغوي أو المدح اللُّغويّ؛ فالعموم والخصوص من وجه، وإن كان مع المدح العرفيّ ؛ فالعموم والخصوص المطلق. كان مع وتأخذ الحمد اللُّغوي مع غير الشُّكر بقسميه يحصل ثلاث نسب، فإن كان مع الحمد العرفي؛ فالعموم والخصوص الوجهي، وإن كان مع المدح بقسميه ؛ فالعموم والخصوص المطلق . وتأخذ الحمد العرفي مع غير الشُّكر بقسميه، والحمد اللُّغوي يحصل نسبتان هما العموم والخصوص المطلق . وتأخذ المدح اللغوي مع العرفي، وبينهما العموم والخصوص المطلق، تأمل .
It indicates the magnification of a person due to his being characterized by a perfection attribute. So their total is six from the product of three, namely shukr, hamd, and madh in two types, the linguistic and the customary. And the relations between them are fifteen. That is because you take customary shukr with each one and there are five relations: general and particular in an absolute sense. And you take linguistic shukr with other than customary shukr and there are four relations: if it is conventional hamd, then synonymy; if it is linguistic hamd or linguistic madh, then general and particular in a certain respect; and if it is customary madh, then general and particular in an absolute sense. And you take linguistic hamd with other than both types of shukr and there are three relations: if it is customary hamd, then general and particular in a certain respect; and if it is both types of madh, then general and particular in an absolute sense. And you take customary hamd with other than both types of shukr and linguistic hamd, and there are two relations: general and particular in an absolute sense. And you take linguistic madh with customary madh, and between them is general and particular in an absolute sense. Reflect.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a logical classification of the relationships between three key terms: shukr (thankfulness), hamd (praise), and madh (commendation), each having a linguistic (lughawi) and a customary ('urfi) sense, yielding six concepts. The author states that the total number of relations among these six is fifteen. He then systematically enumerates how many relations each term yields when paired with others. He uses the logical relations of general and particular absolute ('umum wa khusus mutlaq) and general and particular in some respect ('umum wa khusus wajhi), as well as synonymy (taraduf). This is a formal logical exercise demonstrating the precision of Arabic terminology in theology. The passage ends with 'tammal' (reflect), encouraging the reader to appreciate the depth of this analysis.

The Meaning of Ṣalāh from God, Angels, and Humans
Commentator explains the meaning of ṣalāh per agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the meaning of 'ṣalāh' varies depending on who performs it: from God it is mercy with magnification, from angels it is seeking forgiveness, and from humans it is supplication.

Core Idea
Ṣalāh means different things from God, angels, and humans.

In this passage, the commentator explains the phrase 'wa afḍal al-ṣalāh' from the matn. He clarifies that the sentence is a supplication, not a report, and then details how the meaning of 'ṣalāh' changes when attributed to God, angels, or humans. This lesson focuses on these distinct meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
2
Notice the grammatical analysis
Observe that the sentence is declarative in form but performative (supplicatory) in meaning. Understand why it cannot be purely declarative.
3
Identify the three categories of agents
Identify the meanings of ṣalāh when attributed to God, angels, and humans. The passage gives: for God, mercy with magnification; for angels, seeking forgiveness; for humans, supplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأفضل الصَّلاة ... إلخ) : لمَّا حمد الله تعالى شكراً للنعمة؛ صلى على حبيبه ، لأنَّه الواسطة لنا في جميع النعم، أداء لبعض ما يجب له ، وعملاً بقوله عليه الصَّلاة والسلام : «كلُّ كلام لا يُذكرُ اللهُ فيه فيبدأ به وبالصَّلاةِ عليَّ ؛ فهوَ أقطع ممحوقٌ من كل بركة» (١) ، والجملة خبريَّة لفظاً إنشائية معنى، فالمقصود بها إنشاء الدُّعاء بأنَّ الله يعظم سيّدنا محمَّداً ويشرفه ويحيّيه بتحيَّة لائقة به كما يحيي بعضنا بعضاً، ولا يجوز أن تكون خبريَّة لفظاً ومعنى ؛ لأنَّ المخبر بأنَّ الله صلى عليه – أي : الدُّعاء بخير، فإذا أضيفت إلى الله كان معناها زيادة الإنعام المقرونة بالتَّعظيم والتبجيل (والتسليم) أنعم عليه - لم يكن مصلياً ؛ أي: داعياً بأنَّ الله يعظمه إلَّا على قول من يقول : إنَّ المراد من الصَّلاة التّعظيم، أو إنَّها موضوعة للقدر المشترك وهو الاعتناء بالمصلى عليه، فيجوز أن تكون خبريَّة لفظاً ومعنى ؛ لأنَّ من أخبر بأنَّ الله صلَّى عليه فقد عظمه واعتنى به، وهو خلاف التحقيق. قوله : (الدُّعاء بخير) : أي : بأي لفظ كان . قوله : (فإذا أضيفت إلى الله) : أي : نسبت له . وقوله : (المقرونة بالتَّعظيم ... إلخ) أي: بالنسبة لصلاة الله على الأنبياء، وأمَّا صلاة الله على غيرهم ؛ فمعناها أصل الرّحمة والإنعام، وأمَّا إن أضيفت لغير الله من سائر المخلوقات؛ فهي على معناها الأصلي، وهو الدُّعاء بخير. وقد اختلف في الصَّلاة هل هي مشترك لفظي تعدد وضعه، وهو قول : الجمهور، واختار ابن هشام في «مغنيه» أنَّها من المشترك المعنوي قائلاً : أنَّ الصَّلاة لغة بمعنى واحد وهو العطف، ثمَّ العطف الصواب عندي : بالنّسبة إلى الله تعالى الرَّحمة، وإلى الملائكة الاستغفار، وإلى الآدميين دعاء بعضهم لبعض ) (١) ، وفي المقام كلام طويل، انظره في «حاشية شيخنا الأمير على عبد السَّلام) (٢).
His saying: ('And the best of prayer...etc.') meaning: When he praised God, thanks for the blessing, he prayed upon His beloved, because he is the intermediary for us in all blessings, performing some of what is due to him, and acting upon his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every speech in which God is not mentioned, and begins with it and with prayer upon me, it is cut off, devoid of all blessing.' (1) And the sentence is declarative in form but performative in meaning. So the intended meaning is the initiation of supplication that God magnifies our master Muhammad, honors him, and greets him with a greeting suitable to him, just as we greet one another. And it is not permissible that it be declarative in form and meaning, because the one who reports that God has prayed upon him – i.e., supplication with good – so when it is attributed to God, its meaning is an increase of favor accompanied by magnification and veneration. (And the salutation) If He bestows favor upon him, he would not be praying, i.e., supplicating that God magnifies him, except according to the opinion of those who say that the intended meaning of prayer is magnification, or that it is used for the common meaning which is care for the one prayed upon. Then it is permissible that it be declarative in form and meaning, because whoever reports that God has prayed upon him has magnified and cared for him. But this is contrary to what is verified. His saying: ('Supplication with good') meaning: with any wording. His saying: ('So when it is attributed to God') meaning: attributed to Him. And his saying: ('accompanied by magnification...etc.') meaning: with respect to God's prayer upon the prophets. And as for God's prayer upon others, its meaning is the essence of mercy and favor. And as for when it is attributed to other than God from the rest of creatures, then it is upon its original meaning, which is supplication with good. And there has been disagreement concerning prayer: is it a homonym with multiple coinages? This is the saying of the majority. And Ibn Hisham in his 'Mughnī' chose that it is a shared meaning, saying: prayer in language has one meaning, which is turning. Then the correct view in my opinion: with respect to God, it is mercy; with respect to angels, it is seeking forgiveness; and with respect to humans, it is supplication of some for others. (1) And on this matter there is a lengthy discussion; see it in 'Ḥāshiyat Shaykhunā al-Amīr ʿalā ʿAbd al-Salām' (2).
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the phrase 'وأفضل الصلاة' from the matn. The commentator explains that the author, after praising God, prays upon the Prophet because he is the means of all blessings. He cites a hadith about beginning speech with God and prayer upon the Prophet. Then he discusses the grammatical nature of the sentence: it is declarative in form but performative in meaning (i.e., it is a supplication). He argues that it cannot be declarative in both form and meaning because if it were simply reporting that God blesses the Prophet, then it would not be a supplication. Then he discusses the meaning of 'صلاة' when attributed to God: it means an increase of favor with magnification, not mere supplication. He explains that if one reports that God blesses someone, that report itself is a magnification, but this is not the correct view. He then clarifies the meanings: 'صلاة' from God is mercy with magnification, from angels is seeking forgiveness, from humans is supplication. Finally, he mentions the linguistic debate: is 'صلاة' a homonym (multiple meanings) or a single core meaning? He presents his own view (refining Ibn Hisham's opinion) that it has a single core meaning (turning/care) but specific applications vary depending on the agent.

Key Terms
صلاة
الدُّعاء
الإنعام
التَّعظيم
الاعتناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'ṣalāh' always means supplication, even when attributed to God.
Correction: When attributed to God, it means mercy and magnification, not supplication.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what does 'ṣalāh' mean when attributed to God?
A) Supplication
B) Mercy with magnification
C) Care
D) Seeking forgiveness

Key Takeaway
Ṣalāh from God is mercy and magnification; from humans it is supplication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the ṣalāh upon the Prophet in your prayers, do you feel the weight of asking God to magnify and honor him, or has it become a hollow phrase? How can you restore that sense of urgency and love?
?
The commentator says God's ṣalāh on non-prophets is 'the essence of mercy and favor.' Are you truly seeking that special mercy from God, or are you content with a faith that doesn't actively yearn for His favor? What concrete step will you take today to invite that mercy?
?
If every time you say 'Allāhumma ṣalli ʿalā Muḥammad' you are making a fresh supplication, not just repeating words, how does that change the way you approach duʿā'? Will you pause after every ṣalāh to feel the plea rising from your heart?

The Linguistic Nature of Ṣalāh: Homonym or Shared Meaning?
Commentator discusses the linguistic classification of ṣalāh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate whether 'ṣalāh' is a homonym with multiple meanings or a single core meaning that varies by context.

Core Idea
Ṣalāh has one core meaning that manifests differently.

After explaining the contextual meanings, the commentator turns to a linguistic question: Is 'ṣalāh' a homonym (with separate meanings for God, angels, and humans) or does it have a single core meaning? He presents the majority view and Ibn Hisham's opinion, then gives his own refined position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on the dispute
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
2
Understand the two positions
Identify the majority view (homonym) and Ibn Hisham's view (shared meaning). Note the difference.
3
Note the commentator's own view
Observe that the commentator refines Ibn Hisham's view: the core meaning is al-ʿaṭf (turning/care), with specific applications for God, angels, and humans.

The Meaning of Salat from Allah: Mercy with Glorification
Al-Sawi explains the meaning of salat from Allah as mercy with glorification, refuting the view that it means only glorification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that when Allah is described as sending salat upon someone, it means He bestows increased favor accompanied by glorification, contrary to the view that it is merely a declarative report.

Core Idea
Allah's salat is not just a statement—it is a real bestowal of mercy and veneration.

In Islamic theology, the term 'salat' takes different meanings depending on its subject. When attributed to Allah, it carries a specific connotation that goes beyond mere supplication or declaration. This lesson clarifies the precise meaning, as explained by al-Sawi in his commentary on al-Kharida.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with the text. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage begins mid-sentence; 'بخير' is a continuation from the previous page.
2
Identify the two competing interpretations
Observe the author's explanation of the correct meaning and the alternative view he refutes.
The alternative view is that salat means only glorification or a general 'attention'.
3
Understand the key terms
Focus on 'in'am' (favor), 'ta'zim' (glorification), and 'khabariyya' (declarative). These are crucial for grasping the distinction.
4
Reflect on the author's conclusion
Why does al-Sawi say the alternative is 'contrary to the verified reality'? Think about the implications for how we understand Allah's actions.
The verified reality (tahqiq) is that Allah's salat is a real act, not a mere report.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to consolidate what you've learned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بخير، فإذا أضيفت إلى الله كان معناها زيادة الإنعام المقرونة بالتَّعظيم والتبجيل (والتسليم) أنعم عليه - لم يكن مصلياً ؛ أي: داعياً بأنَّ الله يعظمه إلَّا على قول من يقول : إنَّ المراد من الصَّلاة التّعظيم، أو إنَّها موضوعة للقدر المشترك وهو الاعتناء بالمصلى عليه، فيجوز أن تكون خبريَّة لفظاً ومعنى ؛ لأنَّ من أخبر بأنَّ الله صلَّى عليه فقد عظمه واعتنى به، وهو خلاف التحقيق.
with good, so if it is added to Allah, its meaning is increase of favor accompanied by glorification and veneration (and greeting). [If one says] 'He bestowed favor upon him' - he is not considered one who prays (musalliyan), i.e., one who supplicates that Allah glorifies him, except according to the one who says: the intended meaning of prayer (al-ṣalāh) is glorification, or that it is coined for the common meaning, which is attention to the one prayed upon, so it is permissible for it to be declarative (khabariyya) in wording and meaning, because whoever informs that Allah has prayed upon him has glorified him and attended to him, and that is contrary to the verified reality.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the meaning of 'salat' (prayer/ blessing) when attributed to Allah. The author explains that when the word 'salat' is added to Allah, it means an increase of favor (in'am) accompanied by glorification (ta'zim) and veneration (tabjil). He then refutes a contrary opinion: some claim that if one merely says 'Allah bestowed favor upon him', that is not considered a prayer (i.e., supplication for glorification) unless one holds that 'salat' means glorification or a general concept of 'attention'. On that view, the sentence could be declarative (khabariyya), i.e., reporting that Allah has glorified and attended to the person. However, the author states that this is contrary to the verified truth (al-tahqiq). The passage preserves the distinction that the primary meaning of salat from Allah is mercy and glorification, not a mere report.

التعظيم (al-taʿẓīm)
الإنعام (al-inʿām)
خبريَّة (khabariyya)
التحقيق (al-taḥqīq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Salat from Allah is just a declarative report of glorification.
Correction: It is an actual increase of favor accompanied by glorification and veneration.

Checkpoint

A) Mere supplication for good
B) Increase of favor accompanied by glorification and veneration
C) Only glorification
D) A declarative report

Key Takeaway

The Meanings of 'Salat' and Its Linguistic Nature
Al-Sawi explains the contextual meanings of 'salat' and the linguistic dispute over its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the word 'salat' changes meaning based on attribution and the key linguistic debate over whether it is a homonym or polyseme.

Core Idea
The word 'salat' has a core meaning of 'inclination' that manifests as mercy, forgiveness, or supplication.

This passage unpacks the word 'salat' (prayer) by showing that its meaning shifts depending on whether it is attributed to Allah, angels, or humans. It also presents a scholarly dispute: is 'salat' a homonym with multiple unrelated meanings, or a polyseme with a single underlying sense? Al-Sawi explains the majority view and Ibn Hisham's alternative, then directs readers to further commentary.

Guided Path
1
Understand contextual meanings
Read the passage to see how 'salat' changes meaning when attributed to Allah vs. others. Note the specific definitions for each case.
The meaning for Allah includes mercy and glorification; for angels it is seeking forgiveness; for humans it is supplication.
2
Identify the majority view
The majority of scholars consider 'salat' a homonym (mushtarak lafẓī) with multiple independent meanings.
3
Learn Ibn Hisham's argument
Ibn Hisham argues for a polyseme (mushtarak maʿnawī) with a single core meaning: inclination (al-ʿaṭf).
He claims the specific meanings derive from this core in different contexts.
4
Reflect on the core meaning
Consider how 'inclination' applies to Allah (mercy), angels (seeking forgiveness), and humans (supplication).
This shows the unity behind the diverse uses of the word.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الدُّعاء بخير) : أي : بأي لفظ كان . قوله : (فإذا أضيفت إلى الله) : أي : نسبت له . وقوله : (المقرونة بالتَّعظيم ... إلخ) أي: بالنسبة لصلاة الله على الأنبياء، وأمَّا صلاة الله على غيرهم ؛ فمعناها أصل الرّحمة والإنعام، وأمَّا إن أضيفت لغير الله من سائر المخلوقات؛ فهي على معناها الأصلي، وهو الدُّعاء بخير. وقد اختلف في الصَّلاة هل هي مشترك لفظي تعدد وضعه، وهو قول : الجمهور، واختار ابن هشام في «مغنيه» أنَّها من المشترك المعنوي قائلاً : أنَّ الصَّلاة لغة بمعنى واحد وهو العطف، ثمَّ العطف الصواب عندي : بالنّسبة إلى الله تعالى الرَّحمة، وإلى الملائكة الاستغفار، وإلى الآدميين دعاء بعضهم لبعض ) (۱) ، وفي المقام كلام طويل، انظره في «حاشية شيخنا الأمير على عبد السَّلام) (۲).
His saying: (Supplication for good): i.e., with any wording. His saying: (So if it is added to Allah): i.e., attributed to Him. And his saying: (accompanied by glorification... etc.): i.e., with respect to the prayer of Allah upon the prophets; as for the prayer of Allah upon others, its meaning is the essence of mercy and favor. And as for if it is added to other than Allah from among the creatures, then it is upon its original meaning, which is supplication for good. And there is disagreement concerning prayer (al-ṣalāh): is it a homonym (mushtarak lafẓī) with multiple coinages? That is the majority view. And Ibn Hisham in his 'Mughni' chose that it is a polyseme (mushtarak maʿnawī), saying: 'Prayer linguistically has one meaning, which is inclination (al-ʿaṭf). Then the inclination – correct in my view – is: with respect to Allah Almighty: mercy; with respect to the angels: seeking forgiveness; and with respect to humans: supplication of some for others.' (1) And on this topic there is lengthy discussion; see it in the gloss of our teacher al-Amir on 'Abd al-Salam (2).
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the different usages of the word 'salat'. The author comments on a line from the matn: 'supplication for good' can be with any wording. When 'salat' is attributed to Allah, it means mercy and favor; when attributed to others (like humans), it retains its original linguistic meaning of supplication for good. The passage then presents a linguistic dispute: the majority hold that 'salat' is a homonym (mushtarak lafẓī) – a single word coined for multiple distinct meanings. But Ibn Hisham in his 'Mughni al-Labib' argues it is a polyseme (mushtarak maʿnawī) with a single core meaning: 'inclination' (al-ʿaṭf). This inclination manifests differently: from Allah it is mercy, from angels it is seeking forgiveness (istighfar), and from humans it is mutual supplication. The author refers the reader to further discussion in the gloss of his teacher al-Amir.

Key Terms
مشترك لفظي (mushtarak lafẓī)
مشترك معنوي (mushtarak maʿnawī)
العطف (al-ʿaṭf)
الرحمة (al-raḥma)
الاستغفار (al-istighfār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'salat' has completely separate unrelated meanings with no connection.
Correction: Ibn Hisham argues that all meanings share a core sense of 'inclination', which is adapted to different contexts: mercy from Allah, forgiveness from angels, and supplication from humans.

Checkpoint
What is the core meaning of 'salat' according to Ibn Hisham's view cited in the passage?
A) Supplication for good
B) Glorification
C) Inclination (al-ʿaṭf)
D) Mercy

Key Takeaway
The word 'salat' is a polyseme with a single core meaning of 'inclination', which takes specific forms depending on the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad', do you consciously ask Allah to incline toward the Prophet with mercy and honor? How does knowing that 'salat' means 'inclination' change the intention behind your prayer? Will you pause and internalize this meaning the next time you send blessings?
?
If 'salat' from one human to another is simply supplication, how often do you genuinely pray for the well-being of others—especially those you struggle with—with the same care you want for yourself? Name one person you will sincerely supplicate for today, and do it now.
?
The majority view treats 'salat' as a homonym with separate definitions. Ibn Hisham's view unifies them under 'inclination'. Which view makes your relationship with Allah's mercy more coherent? Are you willing to adopt a unified understanding that deepens your awe, or do you prefer fragmented knowledge? What will you change today?

The Use of 'Alā in Prayers upon the Prophet
Sawi explains the grammatical rule for using 'alā in blessings on the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical rule for using the preposition 'alā in blessings upon the Prophet and why it is used instead of 'li'.

Core Idea
The preposition 'alā in blessings on the Prophet is necessary to avoid heretical meanings.

This passage from Sawi's commentary addresses a subtle grammatical point about the formula of blessings on the Prophet. An objector might wonder why 'alā (upon) is used instead of the usual 'li' (for) for good supplications. Sawi explains the reasoning, emphasizing that using 'li' would lead to serious theological misunderstandings.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial answer
Read the passage carefully and identify the objection raised: why use 'alā for blessings when 'li' is used for good supplications? Note the initial answer that suggests 'ṣalāt' includes the meaning of 'atf.
2
Understand the correct answer
Focus on the correct answer: when the phrase is specifically for prayer and peace, 'alā is obligatory. Understand the reasoning that distinguishes between 'ṣallaytu lahu' and 'ṣallaytu ʿalayhi'.
3
See the theological implications
Reflect on why using 'li' would be corrupt: it would imply worshipping the Prophet or delegating authority to him. Appreciate that grammar protects theology.
This connects to the broader principle that language in worship must be precise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي : التَّحية أي من الله ومن العباد، فتحيَّة الله تعظيمه لنبيه بكلامه القديم، كما أحدنا ضيفه ومن المخلوقات طلب ذلك من الله تعالى يحيي قوله (على النَّبيِّ : إن قلت: إنَّ الدُّعاء إن كان بخير؛ تعدَّى باللام، وإن كان بشر؛ تعدَّى بـ (على). أجيب : بأنَّه ضمَّن الصَّلاة معنى العطف وهو يتعدَّى بـ(على)، والحقُّ في الجواب أن يقال : محلُّ ذلك ما لم يكن بعنوان الصَّلاة والسَّلام، فإن كان به؛ تعيَّن تعديته بـ (على)؛ للفرق بين صليت له، وصليت عليه، وسلَّمت له، وسلَّمت عليه، فلو تعدَّى باللام؛ لأوهم معنى فاسداً؛ لأنَّ صلَّيت (له معناه : عبدته وسلَّمت (له معناه : فوَّضت له الأمر، ولأنَّه خلاف الوارد في القرآن والأحاديث.
His statement: (i.e., the greeting from God and from the servants, so God's greeting is His glorification of His Prophet with His eternal speech, just as one of us honors his guest, and from the creatures it is seeking that from God, He gives life. His statement: (upon the Prophet): If you say: Supplication, if it is for good, is transitive with the preposition 'li', and if it is for evil, it is transitive with 'alā. The answer: He included in the prayer the meaning of inclination ('atf), which is transitive with 'alā. The correct answer is to say: The judgment applies when it is not under the title of prayer and peace; but if it is under that title, its transitivity with 'alā is determined because of the difference between 'I prayed for him' (ṣallaytu lahu) and 'I prayed upon him' (ṣallaytu ʿalayhi), and 'I greeted for him' (sallamtu lahu) and 'I greeted upon him' (sallamtu ʿalayhi). If it were transitive with 'li', it would imply a corrupt meaning, because 'I prayed for him' means I worshipped him, and 'I greeted for him' means I delegated the matter to him, and because it contradicts what is transmitted in the Qur'an and hadiths.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical-linguistic argument about the correct preposition to use in prayers (ṣalāt) and greetings (salām) upon the Prophet. The commentator first explains that the greeting from God is His honoring His Prophet, and from creatures it is asking for that. Then an objection is raised: normally in Arabic, supplication for good uses 'li' (for) and for evil uses 'alā (upon). So why is 'alā used in blessings? The initial answer is that the word 'ṣalāt' incorporates the meaning of 'atf (inclination or mercy), which takes 'alā. But the correct answer is more precise: when the phrase is specifically for the purpose of prayer and peace (as in the formula), 'alā is obligatory to avoid the false meanings that would arise if 'li' were used. 'Ṣallaytu lahu' would mean 'I worshipped him', and 'sallamtu lahu' would mean 'I delegated the matter to him'. These are theologically unacceptable. Moreover, using 'alā is in accordance with the Qur'an and hadith.

Key Terms
الصلاة
السلام
على
اللام
تعدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the preposition 'alā should only be used for evil things, so using it for blessings is incorrect.
Correction: In the specific context of prayer and peace upon the Prophet, 'alā is mandatory to avoid heretical meanings like worship or delegation.

Checkpoint
Why is the preposition 'alā used in prayers upon the Prophet instead of 'li'?
A) Because 'alā is the only preposition that can be used with supplication.
B) To avoid the meaning of worshipping or delegating authority to the Prophet.
C) Because 'alā is grammatically required for all forms of salutation.
D) Because the word 'ṣalāt' is derived from 'ʻaṭf' which takes 'alā.

Key Takeaway
The correct preposition for blessings on the Prophet is 'alā because using 'li' would imply false meanings such as worship or delegation.

?

If using the wrong preposition could imply worshipping the Prophet, what other common phrases in your speech might unknowingly contradict tawhid? How often do you examine your own words for hidden shirk?

The Meaning of al-Mustafa (the Chosen)
Sanusi defines al-Mustafa and explains its active/passive interpretation with hadith support.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-Mustafa means 'the chosen one' and can carry both passive and active senses, supported by a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
Al-Mustafa means the chosen one, both passively and actively.

This lesson explores the linguistic meaning of one of the Prophet's honored titles: al-Mustafa. The commentator explains its grammatical form, its root meaning of selection, and provides a famous hadith that illustrates why the Prophet is called the chosen one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's definitions sink in.

3
Reflect on the hadith evidence
Read the hadith carefully. How does it support the meaning of 'chosen one'?
The hadith shows Allah's successive selections culminating in the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهو بمعنى المفعول أو الفاعل أيضاً. (المصطفى): اسم مفعول من الاصطفاء، وهو الاختيار، فمعناه: المختار . (الكريم) من الكرم، وهو صفة تقتضي الإعطاء لا في نظير شيء، أو هو نفس الإعطاء المذكور ، وقد يراد بالكريم الطَّيِّب، وهو الأنسب هنا ؛ أي: فهو طيب الأصل غيره : فهو مرفوع الرتبة، أو رافع لرتبة منِ اتَّبعه، فهو بمعنى المفعول أو الفاعل، لفّ ونشر مرتب. قوله : (المصطفى) : أصله المصتفى ؛ بتاء مثناة فوقية بعد الصَّاد، قلبت طاء للقاعدة المشهورة. قوله: (فمعناه المختار) : أي : لما في الحديث الصحيح : «إنَّ الله اصطفى كنانة من ولد إسماعيل، واصطفى قريشاً من كنانة، واصطفى من قريش بني هاشم، واصطفاني من بني هاشم، فأنا خيار من خيار من خيار قوله : (وهو صفة تقتضي الإعطاء) : أي : فيكون صفة ذات . وقوله : (أو هو نفس الإعطاء) : أي فيكون صفة فعل. قوله : (وهو الأنسب) :هنا :أي لكونه من الصفات الجامعة. قوله : (طيب الأصل) : أي : النَّسب . (1) أخرجه مسلم (٢٢٧٦) ، وابن حبان في صحيحه» (٦٢٤٢) عن واثلة بن الأسقع نه ، وينظر سبل الهدى والرشاد (۲۹۹/۱)
Thus it is in the sense of the passive or also the active. (al-Mustafa): a passive participle from al-istifa, which is choice. So its meaning: the chosen one. (al-Karim) from al-karam, and it is an attribute that necessitates giving not in exchange for anything, or it is the very giving mentioned. And sometimes by al-Karim is meant the good/pure one, and that is more appropriate here; i.e., he is of good origin. Other than that: he is elevated in rank, or he elevates the rank of whoever follows him. So it is in the sense of the passive or the active, a [laf wa nashr] ordered. His saying: (al-Mustafa): its origin is al-mustafa with a tā' marbūṭa after the ṣād, it was changed to ṭā' by the well-known rule. His saying: (so its meaning is the chosen one): i.e., according to what is in the authentic hadith: 'Indeed Allah chose Kinānah from the children of Ismāʿīl, and chose Quraysh from Kinānah, and chose from Quraysh Banī Hāshim, and chose me from Banī Hāshim, so I am the best of the best of the best.' His saying: (and it is an attribute that necessitates giving): i.e., then it is an attribute of essence. And his saying: (or it is the very giving): i.e., then it is an attribute of action. His saying: (and it is more appropriate): here, i.e., because it is among the comprehensive attributes. His saying: (good origin): i.e., the lineage. (1) Reported by Muslim (2276) and Ibn Ḥibbān in his Ṣaḥīḥ (6242) from Wāthilah ibn al-Asqaʿ, and see Subul al-Hudā wa al-Rashād (1/299).
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic commentary defining two key descriptors of the Prophet: 'al-Mustafa' and 'al-Karim'. The author explains that 'al-Mustafa' is a passive participle from 'al-istifa' (selection), meaning 'the chosen one'. He notes that it can carry either passive or active sense syntactically. For 'al-Karim', he gives two interpretations: as an attribute of essence (inherent generosity) or as an attribute of action (the act of giving). He then notes that in this context 'al-Karim' is better understood as 'good/pure', referring to the Prophet's good lineage (origin). The commentary also provides a morphological note on 'al-Mustafa' (original form with tā' changed to ṭā') and supports the meaning of being chosen with a famous hadith where the Prophet states Allah's successive selections culminating in his own person. The passage ends by confirming that 'good origin' means noble lineage.

الاصطفاء

Correction: The commentator states it can be understood in either passive or active sense.

The Meaning of al-Karim (the Generous)
Sanusi interprets al-Karim with two interpretations and selects the meaning of good origin.

The Linguistic Derivation of 'آل' in Arabic
Sibawayh and Al-Kisa'i's theories on the origin of 'آل' with phonetic and logical discussions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main theories on the origin of the Arabic word 'آل' and the phonetic and logical arguments used to support them.

Core Idea
The word 'آل' originates either from 'أهل' or 'أول', with specific phonetic changes.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Kharida delves into the linguistic roots of the word 'آل', commonly used to refer to the family of the Prophet. The commentator presents the views of two leading grammarians, Sibawayh and Al-Kisa'i, and examines the phonetic transformations involved. Additionally, he addresses a potential logical problem with one of the arguments, highlighting the rigor of Arabic linguistic analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two theories
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation. Note that Sibawayh derives 'آل' from 'أهل', while Al-Kisa'i derives it from 'أول'.
The passage also includes explanations of additional phrases, but focus on the derivation first.
2
Understand the phonetic changes
Sibawayh's theory involves turning 'ha' into hamza and then into alif. Al-Kisa'i's theory involves turning waw into alif. Note the conditions: sukun and openness of preceding letter.
Similar to the derivation of 'Adam'.
3
Examine the objection and response
Why not change 'ha' directly to alif? The response is that such a change is not attested due to distant articulation points, whereas ha-to-hamza is common.
Example: 'ma'' from 'mawh'.
4
Consider the logical issue of circularity
The use of diminutive forms as evidence is questioned because the diminutive is derived from the augmented form, leading to circular reasoning.
This shows the attention to logical consistency in linguistic arguments.
5
Reflect on the significance
This discussion illustrates how Arabic grammar and phonetics interact, and how scholars defended their views with both phonetic and logical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأصله عند سيبويه أهل قلبت هاؤه همزة، ثمَّ الهمزة ألفاً لسكونها وانفتاح ما قبلها كما في آدم، وعند الكسائي : أول كجمل من : آل يؤول إذا رجع، فقلبت الواو ألفاً لتحرُّكها وانفتاح ما قبلها وقوله : (مطلقاً) : أي : ولو عصاة. قوله : (عند سيبويه) : أي : والبصريين. قوله : قلبت هاؤه همزة : أي : لقرب المخرجين . قوله : (ثمَّ الهمزة ألفاً : إن قلت : لِمَ لم تقلب الهاء من أول الأمر ألفاً ؟ أجيب بأنَّه لم يُعْهَد قلب الهاء ألفاً لبعد مخرجيهما، بخلاف قلب الهاء همزةً، فهو معهود كماء أصله موه تحرّكت الواو وانفتح ما قبلها، قلبت ألفاً وقلبت الهاء همزة وكذلك عهد قلب الهمزة ألفاً كما في (آدم). قوله : (وعند الكسائي ... إلخ) : أي : واستدل الأول : بتصغيره على أهيل، والثاني: على أويل. إن قلت : إنَّ المصغَر فرع المكبر، فيلزم عليه الدور .
Its origin according to Sibawayh is 'ahl'; its ha was turned into a hamza, then the hamza into an alif due to its sukun and the openness of what precedes it, as in 'Adam'. According to Al-Kisa'i: 'awal' like 'jamal' from 'aala ya'ulu' meaning 'to return', so the waw was turned into an alif due to its movement and the openness of what precedes it. And his saying: (absolutely): i.e., even if they are disobedient. His saying: (according to Sibawayh): i.e., and the Basrans. His saying: 'its ha was turned into a hamza': i.e., due to the proximity of the points of articulation. His saying: (then the hamza into an alif): If you say: why was the ha not turned into an alif from the beginning? The answer is that turning ha into an alif is not known due to the distance of their points of articulation, unlike turning ha into a hamza, which is known, like 'ma'' whose origin is 'mawh'; the waw moved and what preceded it opened, so it turned into an alif and the ha turned into a hamza. And similarly, turning the hamza into an alif is known, as in 'Adam'. His saying: (and according to Al-Kisa'i... etc.): i.e., the first (Sibawayh) deduces from its diminutive 'uhayl', and the second (Al-Kisa'i) from 'uwayl'. If you say: the diminutive is derived from the augmented form, so it entails circularity.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage examines the linguistic derivation of the word 'آل' (family/clan). The commentator presents two theories: Sibawayh holds that 'آل' originates from 'أهل' (people), where the 'ha' changes to a hamza and then to an alif, similar to the derivation of 'آدم'. Al-Kisa'i holds that 'آل' comes from 'أول' (root 'أول' meaning to return), with the waw changing to an alif. The commentator then addresses an objection: why not change 'ha' directly to an alif? The answer is that such a change is unprecedented due to the difference in articulation points, while changing 'ha' to hamza is common. Finally, a potential circularity is noted: using the diminutive forms 'أهيل' and 'أويل' as evidence is problematic because the diminutive is derived from the augmented form, leading to a logical circle (دور).

Key Terms
آل
سيبويه
الكسائي
أهل
أول
قلب
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the derivation of 'آل' is simple and straightforward.
Correction: The commentator shows that there are two valid theories backed by phonetic and logical arguments, and even raises a logical problem with one of the evidence forms.

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against using the diminutive form as evidence for the origin of 'آl'?
A) The diminutive is not attested
B) The diminutive is derived from the augmented form, causing circularity
C) The diminutive is not used in Arabic
D) The diminutive has a different meaning

Key Takeaway
The word 'آل' has disputed origins, with Sibawayh tracing it to 'أهل' and Al-Kisa'i to 'أول', each supported by phonetic shifts and addressing logical consistency.
