Basic Construction of 'لا سيما' and Case Endings
Al-Sawi explains the basic construction and case endings of 'لا سيما'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the basic construction of the phrase 'لا سيما' and the rules governing the case endings of its constituent parts.

Core Idea
'لا سيما' has a unique construction with specific case endings depending on the role of 'ما'.

In this passage, Al-Sawi breaks down the phrase 'لا سيما' into its components: 'سي' and 'ما'. He explains how the case endings of 'سي' change based on whether it is the noun of negation or part of a fixed expression. Understanding this will help you correctly parse and use this common Arabic phrase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components
Look at the phrase 'لا سيما' and identify 'سي' as a noun in the construct state and 'ما' as its genitive. Notice the example given.
2
Understand the case endings
Note that 'سي' can be read with kasra or damma, but its fatha is a case-ending fatha when it is the noun of 'لا النافية للجنس' in the construct state. This indicates accusative.
If 'ما' is preventive (kāffa), the fatha becomes a built-in fatha (like in 'لا رجل').
3
Learn about the accusative after it
If an indefinite noun follows 'لا سيما' and is accusative, it is a tamīz (specification). This is a key rule for parsing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مثل الذي هو رفيقه ولا مثل شيء هو رفيقه، و(سي) مضاف، و(ما) مضاف إليه، فعلى كل من الوجهين الجر والرفع تكون فتحة (سي) فتحة إعراب ؛ لأنّ اسم لا النافية للجنس إذا كان مضافاً يكون منصوباً، وأمَّا نصب النكرة بعدها فعلى التّمييز، و(ما) كافة على الإضافة، والفتحة فتحة بناء مثلها في لا رجل) . قوله : (هو رفيقه ... إلخ) : أي : وهذا الضمير مبتدأ عائد على الصلة، ورابط الصّلة وحذفه هنا ليس بشاذٌ بل واجب، سواء طالت الصّلة - كما هنا - أو لم تطل كما في قولهم : لا سيّما زيد؛ لأنَّ هذا الكلام جرى في كثرة الاستعمال مجرى الأمثال، فلا يغيّر عما سمع فيه من الحذف . قوله : (إذا كان مضافاً ... إلخ) : إن قلت : يلزم على إضافة اسم لا لما الموصولة عمل لا في معرفة مع أنَّها لا تعمل إلَّا في النكرات. أجيب : بأن (سي) كمثل متوغلة في الإبهام، فلا تفيد إضافتها للمعرفة التّعريف . قوله : (وأمَّا نصب النكرة بعدها :أي وأمَّا المعرفة : فلا يجوز نصبها عند الجمهور، وجوّز بعضهم نصبها بجعل (ما ) كافَّة، ولا سيّما بمنزلة (إلَّا) الاستثنائيَّة، فما بعدها منصوب على الاستثناء، كما نقله «حواشي الأشموني» (1) . قوله : ( والفتحة فتحة بناء) : بحث فيه شيخنا الأمير بقوله : (أقول : قد (1) ينظر حاشية الصبان على شرح الأشموني (٢٤٩/٢).
Discussion on the Companions, may God be pleased with them. Like that which is his companion and not like anything that is his companion, and (sayy) is in the construct state, and (ma) is the genitive to it. So according to both aspects, the genitive and the nominative, the fatha of (sayy) is a case-ending fatha, because the noun of the negative of genus (lā an-nāfiya li-l-jins) when it is in the construct state is accusative. As for the accusative of the indefinite after it, it is on the basis of tamīz (specification), and (ma) is al-kāffa (preventive) from annexation, and the fatha is a built-in fatha like that in 'lā rajula' (no man). His saying: 'huwa rafīquhu... etc.' means: this pronoun is a subject (mubtada') referring back to the relative clause (ṣila), and the linker of the relative clause (rābiṭ aṣ-ṣila) is omitted, and its omission here is not anomalous but obligatory, whether the relative clause is long—as here—or short, as in their saying 'lā siyyama Zaydun', because this expression, due to extensive use, has become like proverbs, so it is not changed from what is heard in it of omission. His saying: 'when it is in the construct state... etc.' If you say: adding the noun of lā (ism lā) to the relative pronoun (mā al-mawṣūla) necessitates that lā operates on a definite [noun], whereas it only operates on indefinite [nouns]. The answer: because (sayy) is like an example deeply rooted in vagueness, so its annexation to a definite does not confer definiteness. His saying: 'and as for the accusative of the indefinite after it' meaning: and as for the definite: its accusative is not permissible according to the majority, but some allowed its accusative by making (ma) a preventive (kāffa), and 'lā siyyama' is like the exceptive (illā), so what follows it is accusative on the basis of exception (istiṯnā'), as transmitted in 'Ḥawāshī al-Ashmūnī'. His saying: 'and the fatha is a built-in fatha': our teacher al-Amīr discussed it by saying: 'I say: indeed ...' (1) See Ḥāshiyat aṣ-Ṣabbān ʿalā Sharḥ al-Ashmūnī (2/249).
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical analysis of the expression 'لا سيما' (lā siyyama). It begins by presenting a sample construction: 'مثل الذي هو رفيقه ولا مثل شيء هو رفيقه' (like that which is his companion, and not like anything that is his companion). The author explains that the word 'سي' (sayy) is in the construct state (مضاف) and 'ما' (ma) is its genitive. The vowel on 'سي' is a fatha, which is a case-ending fatha because the noun of 'لا النافية للجنس' (the negative of genus) when used in the construct state is always accusative (منصوب). However, if the word after 'لا سيما' is indefinite and appears in the accusative, that is a tamīz (specification). The 'ما' can also be 'كافة' (preventive), meaning it blocks the annexation, and then the fatha is a built-in fatha (like in 'لا رجل'). The passage then explains a point about the pronoun 'هو' in the example: it is a subject (مبتدأ) that refers back to the relative clause (الصلة), and the linker (رابط الصلة) is omitted—this omission is obligatory because the expression is so common it is treated like a proverb. The author then addresses an objection: if 'سي' is added to 'ما' which is a relative pronoun (definite), then 'لا' would act on a definite noun, but 'لا النافية للجنس' only operates on indefinite nouns. The answer is that 'سي' is deeply vague (متوغلة في الإبهام) so its annexation to a definite does not make it definite. Finally, the author distinguishes between the accusative of the indefinite noun after 'لا سيما' (which is tamīz) and the accusative of the definite noun, which is not allowed by the majority. However, some grammarians permit it by making 'ما' a preventive (كافة), treating 'لا سيما' as an exceptive particle (إلا), making the following noun accusative on the basis of exception (استثناء). The passage concludes with a reference to discussions in Al-Ashmūnī's glosses.

Key Terms
لا سيما
لا النافية للجنس
مضاف
مضاف إليه
تمييز
كافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'لا سيما' always requires the following noun to be in the genitive case.
Correction: While it is often genitive, the following noun can also be accusative as tamīz (specification) or in exceptional constructions.

Checkpoint
When 'ما' in 'لا سيما' is considered 'كافة' (preventive), what happens to the fatha on 'سي'?
A) It becomes a case-ending fatha
B) It becomes a built-in fatha
C) It changes to kasra
D) It is dropped

Key Takeaway
The fatha on 'سي' in 'لا سيما' can be either a case-ending fatha (when it is the noun of negation in construct state) or a built-in fatha (when 'ما' is preventive).

Obligatory Omission of the Linker and Definiteness in 'لا سيما'
Al-Sawi discusses the obligatory omission of the linker and the definiteness issue in 'لا سيما'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the linker (rābiṭ aṣ-ṣila) is obligatorily omitted in 'لا سيما' and how the rule of definiteness is handled when 'سي' is added to a relative pronoun.

Core Idea
The omission of the linker is obligatory, and the vagueness of 'سي' allows it to govern a definite relative pronoun.

The passage continues by examining a specific comment about the pronoun 'هو' in the example. Al-Sawi explains that the omission of the linker is not anomalous but obligatory due to the proverbial nature of the phrase. He then addresses the objection that 'لا' only works on indefinites, resolving it through the inherent vagueness of 'سي'. This lesson unpacks these nuanced points.

Guided Path
1
Examine the pronoun omission
Notice that in 'هو رفيقه', the pronoun 'هو' is a subject referring to the relative clause. The linker (rābiṭ) is omitted, and Al-Sawi says this omission is obligatory.
This is because the phrase has become proverbial.
2
Understand the objection about definiteness
Consider the objection: if 'سي' is added to 'ما' (a relative pronoun, definite), then 'لا' would act on a definite, but it only acts on indefinites.
3
Learn the answer
The answer is that 'سي' is deeply vague (متوغلة في الإبهام), so its annexation to a definite does not confer definiteness, preserving the rule.
4
Distinguish between indefinite and definite after it
Note that the accusative of an indefinite noun after 'لا سيما' is tamīz, but for a definite noun, the majority do not allow accusative. Some allow it by treating 'ما' as kāffa and 'لا سيما' as an exceptive particle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الصحابة رضي الله عنهم مثل الذي هو رفيقه ولا مثل شيء هو رفيقه، و(سي) مضاف، و(ما) مضاف إليه، فعلى كل من الوجهين الجر والرفع تكون فتحة (سي) فتحة إعراب ؛ لأنّ اسم لا النافية للجنس إذا كان مضافاً يكون منصوباً، وأمَّا نصب النكرة بعدها فعلى التّمييز، و(ما) كافة على الإضافة، والفتحة فتحة بناء مثلها في لا رجل) . قوله : (هو رفيقه ... إلخ) : أي : وهذا الضمير مبتدأ عائد على الصلة، ورابط الصّلة وحذفه هنا ليس بشاذٌ بل واجب، سواء طالت الصّلة - كما هنا - أو لم تطل كما في قولهم : لا سيّما زيد؛ لأنَّ هذا الكلام جرى في كثرة الاستعمال مجرى الأمثال، فلا يغيّر عما سمع فيه من الحذف . قوله : (إذا كان مضافاً ... إلخ) : إن قلت : يلزم على إضافة اسم لا لما الموصولة عمل لا في معرفة مع أنَّها لا تعمل إلَّا في النكرات. أجيب : بأن (سي) كمثل متوغلة في الإبهام، فلا تفيد إضافتها للمعرفة التّعريف . قوله : (وأمَّا نصب النكرة بعدها :أي وأمَّا المعرفة : فلا يجوز نصبها عند الجمهور، وجوّز بعضهم نصبها بجعل (ما ) كافَّة، ولا سيّما بمنزلة (إلَّا) الاستثنائيَّة، فما بعدها منصوب على الاستثناء، كما نقله «حواشي الأشموني» (1) . قوله : ( والفتحة فتحة بناء) : بحث فيه شيخنا الأمير بقوله : (أقول : قد (1) ينظر حاشية الصبان على شرح الأشموني (٢٤٩/٢).

Checkpoint
Why does the annexation of 'سي' to the relative pronoun 'ما' not violate the rule that 'لا النافية للجنس' only operates on indefinite nouns?
A) Because 'ما' is indefinite
B) Because 'سي' is inherently indefinite despite annexation

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that the omission of the linker in 'لا سيما' is obligatory because the expression is fixed from frequent use. Consider your own speech: are there phrases or acts of worship that you perform carelessly because they have become routine? How can you restore the intentionality that the obligation demands? Write one specific action you will take this week to treat a routine act of 'ibadah with the fixedness it deserves.
?
The passage resolves an apparent contradiction by appealing to the deep-rooted vagueness of the word 'سي', which allows a definite to be treated as indefinite for grammatical purposes. Are there situations in your understanding of Islam where you see a contradiction between a rule and a reality, but you haven't considered the 'built-in vagueness' that resolves it? How can you humble yourself to accept that your limited understanding may not capture the full wisdom? Commit to studying one issue you currently find contradictory with an open heart this week.

The Meaning of 'اللطيف' for Allah: Linguistic Roots and Metaphorical Interpretation
Explains the linguistic meanings of 'لطيفة' and their metaphorical application to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal meanings of 'لطيف' (small, thin, transparent) cannot be directly attributed to Allah, and how the term is interpreted metaphorically as 'Knower of hidden things' or 'Beneficent'.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are beyond physical meanings; 'اللطيف' points to His perfect knowledge and kindness.

This lesson explores the word 'لطيفة' that appears in the poem 'al-Kharidah al-Bahiyyah'. While its basic meanings are physical (small, thin, transparent), such descriptions are impossible for Allah. We will see how scholars use the principle of 'necessary consequence' to interpret the term in a way befitting Allah's majesty.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic root
Learn that 'لطيفة' comes from 'لطف', meaning small, thin, or transparent. These are the literal meanings.
Note that they are physical attributes.
2
Apply the principle of impossibility
Realize that these physical meanings cannot apply to Allah because He is transcendent. The commentary explains that we then take the 'necessary consequence' (لازم) of these meanings.
3
Discover the two valid interpretations
The passage gives two interpretations: Allah is the Knower of hidden things (because hidden things are often small/thin/transparent) and the Beneficent Bestower of favors. Both are valid.
Another root 'لطف' (to be kind) gives the second interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية الصاوي على شرح الخريدة البهية
هي (لطيفة) من اللُّطف، وهو ضد الكثافة من (لطف) كـ (كرم) ، دقّ أو رقّ، فاللطيف الصَّغير الحجم أو الرَّقيقُ القوام، أو الشَّفَّافُ الذي لا يحجب ما وراءه كالزُّجاج ، فإذا أطلق بهذا المعنى على الله تعالى فمعناه : العالم بخفيَّات الأمور، لما مرّ من أنَّ اللفظ إذا أوهم خلاف المراد في حقه تعالى يراد منه لازمه .
قوله : (هي لطيفة : قدَّر الضمير ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّ (لطيفة) خبر مبتدأ محذوف، فهو نعت مقطوع ؛ لئلا يتوهم أنَّ تلك الأوصاف المذكورة تعدُّ من جملة الاسم.
قوله : (دقّ) : أي : صغر حجمه .
وقوله : (أو رقّ) : ضدُّ غلظ
قوله : (الصَّغير الحجم) راجع لـ(دق).
وقوله : (أو الرقيق القوام): راجع لـ(رق).
وقوله : (أو الشَّفاف) : لم يبين ما يرجع له، فحقه أن يقول بعد قوله: (أو رق) أو شفّ، فيكون في الكلام لَفّ ونشر مرتب، والمعاني متغايرة؛ : فإنَّه لا يلزم من الصغر الرّقة، ولا من الرّقة الشَّفافية، ولا من الشَّفافية الصغر.
قوله : (إذا أوهم خلاف المراد أي وهذه المعاني مستحيلة على الله تعالى، فوصفه باللطف من حيث تعلق علمه بهذه المعاني؛ فإنَّ خفيَّات الأمور : إما صغيرة الحجم، أو رقيقة القوام، أو شفّافة.
قوله : (وأما لطف): جملة مستأنفة مقابلة لقوله : (من لطف)، وفعل الأوَّل لازم، والثَّاني متعد.
قوله : (وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر ... إلخ ) : الوجه : المأخذ والدليل .
معنى الخريدة ۱۰۰ وأمَّا (لَطَف) كـ(نصر) فمعناه أحسنَ وأنعم، ومعناه في حقه تعالى ظاهر ؛ أي: المحسن المنعم على عباده .
وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر اللطيف بالعالم بخفيَّات الأمور، ووجه من فسَّره بالبَرِّ المحسن لعباده .
والمراد هنا أنَّها قليلة الألفاظ أو سَلِسَةُ الألفاظ أو واضحتها، والكلُّ صحيح، وعلى الأوّل فقوله: (صغيرة في الحَجم) أي : القدر، وصف ،كاشف أبياتها أحد وسبعون بيتاً ولمَّا كان هذا الوصف يوهم أنَّها قليلة العلم استدرك عليه بأن رفع هذا التوهم بقوله : (لكنَّها كبيرة) أي : عظيمة (في العلم) أي: المعاني تعالى، فوصفه باللطف من حيث تعلق علمه بهذه المعاني؛ فإنَّ خفيَّات الأمور : إما صغيرة الحجم، أو رقيقة القوام، أو شفّافة.
قوله : (وأما لطف): جملة مستأنفة مقابلة لقوله : (من لطف)، وفعل الأوَّل لازم، والثَّاني متعد.
قوله : (وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر ... إلخ ) : الوجه : المأخذ والدليل .
قوله: (أنّها قليلة الألفاظ) راجع لصغر الحجم.
وقوله : (أو سلسة الألفاظ ) : راجع لرقة القوام.
وقوله : (أو واضحتها): راجع للشَّفافية.
قوله : (بأن رفع هذا التَّوهُم) تصوير لمعنى الاستدراك؛ لأنَّ الاستدراك عبارة يؤتى بها لرفع ما يتوهم ثبوته أو نفيه .
Hashiyah al-Sawi on the Explanation of al-Kharidah al-Bahiyyah.
It (لطيفة) is from al-lutf, which is the opposite of density, from (لطف) like (كرم), meaning small in size or thin, so al-latif is the small in volume or the thin in build or the transparent which does not veil what is behind it like glass. When it is used in this meaning with respect to Allah the Exalted, its meaning is: The Knower of hidden matters, because it has passed that the word, if it suggests something contrary to what is intended with respect to Him the Exalted, its necessary consequence is intended from it.
His statement: (هي لطيفة): he estimated the pronoun; indicating that (لطيفة) is the predicate of a deleted subject, so it is a disjunctive adjective; lest it be thought that those mentioned attributes are part of the name.
His statement: (دقّ): i.e. its size became small.
And his statement: (أو رقّ): opposite of thickness/harshness.
His statement: (الصَّغير الحجم) refers back to (دقّ).
And his statement: (أو الرقيق القوام) refers back to (رقّ).
And his statement: (أو الشَّفاف): he did not clarify to what it refers back, so it would be proper for him to say after his statement: (أو رقّ) or شفّ, so in the statement there would be an arranged laff wa nashr (wrapping and spreading), and the meanings are different; for smallness does not necessarily imply thinness, nor does thinness imply transparency, nor does transparency imply smallness.
His statement: (إذا أوهم خلاف المراد) i.e. these meanings are impossible with respect to Allah the Exalted, so His description with lutf is in terms of His knowledge being attached to these meanings; for hidden matters are either small in size, or thin in build, or transparent.
His statement: (وأما لطف): a new sentence corresponding to his statement: (من لطف), and the first verb is intransitive, and the second is transitive.
His statement: (وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر ... إلخ): the 'وجه' is the basis and evidence.
The meaning of al-Kharidah 100. And as for (لَطَف) like (نصر), its meaning is to do good and be gracious, and its meaning with respect to Him the Exalted is apparent; i.e. The Beneficent, The Bestower of favors upon His servants.
And by this you know the basis of the one who interpreted al-latif as the Knower of hidden matters, and the basis of the one who interpreted it as the Righteous Benefactor to His servants.
And what is meant here is that it (the poem) is few in words, or smooth in words, or clear in them, and all are correct. On the first interpretation, his statement: (صغيرة في الحَجم) i.e. in quantity, a description. The number of its verses is seventy-one. When this description suggested that it is little in knowledge, he corrected for it by removing this suggestion with his statement: (but it is great) i.e. magnificent (in knowledge) i.e. meanings. Exalted is He, so He is described with lutf in terms of His knowledge being attached to these meanings; for hidden matters are either small in size, or thin in build, or transparent.
His statement: (وأما لطف): a new sentence corresponding to his statement: (من لطف), and the first verb is intransitive, and the second is transitive.
His statement: (وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر ... إلخ): the 'وجه' is the basis and evidence.
His statement: (أنّها قليلة الألفاظ) refers back to small size. And his statement: (أو سلسة الألفاظ) refers back to thinness of build. And his statement: (أو واضحتها) refers back to transparency.
His statement: (بأن رفع هذا التَّوهُم) is a depiction of the meaning of istidrak; because istidrak is an expression brought to remove what is suspected to be established or negated.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary on the word 'لطيفة' as it appears in the poem 'al-Kharidah al-Bahiyyah'. The commentator begins by giving the linguistic root: 'لطيفة' comes from 'اللطف', which is the opposite of density (الكثافة). The verb 'لطف' (like 'كرم') means to be small or thin. From this, 'اللطيف' can mean small in volume, thin in build, or transparent like glass. When applied to Allah, these literal meanings are impossible, so the term is used metaphorically: it means 'the Knower of hidden things' (العالم بخفيَّات الأمور), because hidden things are often small, thin, or transparent. The commentator explains that whenever a word, if taken literally, would imply something contrary to what is befitting for Allah, we intend its necessary consequence (لازم) rather than the literal sense.

Then the commentator addresses a grammatical point: the phrase 'هي لطيفة' is a predicate of a deleted subject, so it is a disjunctive adjective (نعت مقطوع) to avoid the misunderstanding that the attributes mentioned (small, thin, transparent) are part of the divine name itself. He then critiques the arrangement of the three meanings: the author of the original explanation should have explicitly matched 'دق' with صغير الحجم, 'رق' with رقيق القوام, and 'شف' with شفاف, in a proper 'laff wa nashr' (wrapping and spreading) order. He notes that these three meanings are distinct and not implied by each other.

Next, the commentator continues the theological point: since the literal meanings (small size, thinness, transparency) are impossible for Allah, His being described as 'لطيف' must refer to His knowledge of hidden matters, which can be small, thin, or transparent. Then he introduces a second verb form: 'لطف' like 'نصر' means 'to be kind/good' (أحسن وأنعم), and this meaning is direct for Allah: He is the Beneficent, the Bestower of favors. This leads to two valid interpretations of 'اللطيف' for Allah: the Knower of hidden things, and the Righteous Benefactor.

The commentary then shifts to describing the poem itself: here 'لطيفة' means few in words, smooth, or clear. All these are correct. On the first meaning (few words), the poet describes the poem as 'صغيرة في الحجم' (small in size), meaning in quantity—the poem has seventy-one verses. However, this might suggest that the poem is small in knowledge, so the poet corrects this by saying 'لكنها كبيرة في العلم' (but it is great in knowledge). The commentator explains that this correction is called 'istidrak', which is a rhetorical device used to remove a false impression. He then connects this back to the theological point: Allah is described as 'لطيف' because His knowledge encompasses hidden things that are small, thin, or transparent. Finally, he breaks down the three possible meanings of the poem's description: 'قليلة الألفاظ' refers to small size, 'سلسة الألفاظ' to smoothness (thinness), and 'واضحتها' to clarity (transparency). The whole passage thus weaves together linguistic, grammatical, theological, and poetic commentary.

استعارة تصريحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah is physically small, thin, or transparent because He is described as 'اللطيف'.
Correction: These physical meanings are impossible for Allah. The term is interpreted metaphorically as 'Knower of hidden things' or 'Beneficent', relying on the necessary consequence or a different root.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is it impermissible to interpret 'اللطيف' for Allah as physically small, thin, or transparent?
A) Because these are impossible attributes for Allah.
B) Because the word has a different root.
C) Because the poem uses it differently.
D) Because it would lead to anthropomorphism.

Key Takeaway
The word 'اللطيف' cannot be taken literally for Allah; it must be interpreted as 'Knower of hidden things' or 'Beneficent' based on the principle of necessary consequence or a second root.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as 'اللطيف', do you truly feel that He knows even the smallest, most hidden concerns of your heart? How does this awareness change the way you share your deepest struggles with Him?
?
The second meaning of 'اللطيف' is 'The Beneficent, The Bestower of favors.' Have you truly considered that every good thing in your life, no matter how small, is a direct favor from Him? Take a moment to name three such favors you previously took for granted, and thank Him specifically for them.
?
If someone were to ask you who Allah is based on this attribute alone, would you be ready to explain that He is both the Knower of your innermost secrets and the One who constantly shows you kindness? What would you say to someone who insists that Allah is distant and indifferent?

Poetic Description and Rhetorical Correction: The Meaning of 'لطيفة' for the Poem
Explains the application of 'لطيفة' to the poem and the use of استدراك to correct misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'لطيفة' is used to describe the poem 'al-Kharidah al-Bahiyyah' and the rhetorical device of استدراك (correction) to remove a false impression.

Core Idea
A poem can be 'لطيفة' in its words, but its greatness lies in its knowledge, not its size.

After exploring the theological meanings, we now see how the same word 'لطيفة' is applied to the poem itself. The commentator explains three possible meanings (few words, smooth, clear) and how the poet corrects a potential misunderstanding about the poem's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three descriptions of the poem
The passage says 'لطيفة' here means either few in words, smooth in words, or clear in words. Each corresponds to one of the literal meanings (small, thin, transparent).
These are different from the theological interpretation.
2
Understand the 'size' reference
The poem is described as 'small in size', meaning it has only seventy-one verses. This might give the impression that it contains little knowledge.
3
Learn about استدراك
The correction is a rhetorical device called استدراك, which removes a false expectation. Here, it is used to assert that the poem is actually great in knowledge.
This shows the importance of precise language in theological poetry.

Source Text

حاشية الصاوي على شرح الخريدة البهية
هي (لطيفة) من اللُّطف، وهو ضد الكثافة من (لطف) كـ (كرم) ، دقّ أو رقّ، فاللطيف الصَّغير الحجم أو الرَّقيقُ القوام، أو الشَّفَّافُ الذي لا يحجب ما وراءه كالزُّجاج ، فإذا أطلق بهذا المعنى على الله تعالى فمعناه : العالم بخفيَّات الأمور، لما مرّ من أنَّ اللفظ إذا أوهم خلاف المراد في حقه تعالى يراد منه لازمه .
قوله : (هي لطيفة : قدَّر الضمير ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّ (لطيفة) خبر مبتدأ محذوف، فهو نعت مقطوع ؛ لئلا يتوهم أنَّ تلك الأوصاف المذكورة تعدُّ من جملة الاسم.
قوله : (دقّ) : أي : صغر حجمه .
وقوله : (أو رقّ) : ضدُّ غلظ
قوله : (الصَّغير الحجم) راجع لـ(دق).
وقوله : (أو الرقيق القوام): راجع لـ(رق).
وقوله : (أو الشَّفاف) : لم يبين ما يرجع له، فحقه أن يقول بعد قوله: (أو رق) أو شفّ، فيكون في الكلام لَفّ ونشر مرتب، والمعاني متغايرة؛ : فإنَّه لا يلزم من الصغر الرّقة، ولا من الرّقة الشَّفافية، ولا من الشَّفافية الصغر.
قوله : (إذا أوهم خلاف المراد أي وهذه المعاني مستحيلة على الله تعالى، فوصفه باللطف من حيث تعلق علمه بهذه المعاني؛ فإنَّ خفيَّات الأمور : إما صغيرة الحجم، أو رقيقة القوام، أو شفّافة.
قوله : (وأما لطف): جملة مستأنفة مقابلة لقوله : (من لطف)، وفعل الأوَّل لازم، والثَّاني متعد.
قوله : (وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر ... إلخ ) : الوجه : المأخذ والدليل .
معنى الخريدة ۱۰۰ وأمَّا (لَطَف) كـ(نصر) فمعناه أحسنَ وأنعم، ومعناه في حقه تعالى ظاهر ؛ أي: المحسن المنعم على عباده .
وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر اللطيف بالعالم بخفيَّات الأمور، ووجه من فسَّره بالبَرِّ المحسن لعباده .
والمراد هنا أنَّها قليلة الألفاظ أو سَلِسَةُ الألفاظ أو واضحتها، والكلُّ صحيح، وعلى الأوّل فقوله: (صغيرة في الحَجم) أي : القدر، وصف ،كاشف أبياتها أحد وسبعون بيتاً ولمَّا كان هذا الوصف يوهم أنَّها قليلة العلم استدرك عليه بأن رفع هذا التوهم بقوله : (لكنَّها كبيرة) أي : عظيمة (في العلم) أي: المعاني تعالى، فوصفه باللطف من حيث تعلق علمه بهذه المعاني؛ فإنَّ خفيَّات الأمور : إما صغيرة الحجم، أو رقيقة القوام، أو شفّافة.
قوله : (وأما لطف): جملة مستأنفة مقابلة لقوله : (من لطف)، وفعل الأوَّل لازم، والثَّاني متعد.
قوله : (وبهذا علمت وجه من فسَّر ... إلخ ) : الوجه : المأخذ والدليل .
قوله: (أنّها قليلة الألفاظ) راجع لصغر الحجم.
وقوله : (أو سلسة الألفاظ ) : راجع لرقة القوام.
وقوله : (أو واضحتها): راجع للشَّفافية.
قوله : (بأن رفع هذا التَّوهُم) تصوير لمعنى الاستدراك؛ لأنَّ الاستدراك عبارة يؤتى بها لرفع ما يتوهم ثبوته أو نفيه .

It (لطيفة) is from al-lutf, which is the opposite of density, from (لطف) like (كرم), meaning small in size or thin, so al-latif is the small in volume or the thin in build or the transparent which does not veil what is behind it like glass. Commentary
This passage is a continuous commentary on the word 'لطيفة' as it appears in the poem 'al-Kharidah al-Bahiyyah'. The commentator begins by giving the linguistic root: 'لطيفة' comes from 'اللطف', which is the opposite of density (الكثافة). The verb 'لطف' (like 'كرم') means to be small or thin. From this, 'اللطيف' can mean small in volume, thin in build, or transparent like glass. When applied to Allah, these literal meanings are impossible, so the term is used metaphorically: it means 'the Knower of hidden things' (العالم بخفيَّات الأمور), because hidden things are often small, thin, or transparent. The commentator explains that whenever a word, if taken literally, would imply something contrary to what is befitting for Allah, we intend its necessary consequence (لازم) rather than the literal sense.

Then the commentator addresses a grammatical point: the phrase 'هي لطيفة' is a predicate of a deleted subject, so it is a disjunctive adjective (نعت مقطوع) to avoid the misunderstanding that the attributes mentioned (small, thin, transparent) are part of the divine name itself. He then critiques the arrangement of the three meanings: the author of the original explanation should have explicitly matched 'دق' with صغير الحجم, 'رق' with رقيق القوام, and 'شف' with شفاف, in a proper 'laff wa nashr' (wrapping and spreading) order. He notes that these three meanings are distinct and not implied by each other.

Next, the commentator continues the theological point: since the literal meanings (small size, thinness, transparency) are impossible for Allah, His being described as 'لطيف' must refer to His knowledge of hidden matters, which can be small, thin, or transparent. Then he introduces a second verb form: 'لطف' like 'نصر' means 'to be kind/good' (أحسن وأنعم), and this meaning is direct for Allah: He is the Beneficent, the Bestower of favors. This leads to two valid interpretations of 'اللطيف' for Allah: the Knower of hidden things, and the Righteous Benefactor.

The commentary then shifts to describing the poem itself: here 'لطيفة' means few in words, smooth, or clear. All these are correct. On the first meaning (few words), the poet describes the poem as 'صغيرة في الحجم' (small in size), meaning in quantity—the poem has seventy-one verses. However, this might suggest that the poem is small in knowledge, so the poet corrects this by saying 'لكنها كبيرة في العلم' (but it is great in knowledge). The commentator explains that this correction is called 'istidrak', which is a rhetorical device used to remove a false impression. He then connects this back to the theological point: Allah is described as 'لطيف' because His knowledge encompasses hidden things that are small, thin, or transparent. Finally, he breaks down the three possible meanings of the poem's description: 'قليلة الألفاظ' refers to small size, 'سلسة الألفاظ' to smoothness (thinness), and 'واضحتها' to clarity (transparency). The whole passage thus weaves together linguistic, grammatical, theological, and poetic commentary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A short poem (few verses) contains little knowledge.
Correction: The poem's small size in quantity does not imply lack of knowledge; the poet corrects this by emphasizing its greatness in knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the poet say 'لكنها كبيرة في العلم' (but it is great in knowledge)?
A) To show that the poem contains many verses.
B) To correct the impression that the poem is small in knowledge.
C) To describe Allah's knowledge.
D) To explain the meaning of لطيفة.

Key Takeaway
The term 'لطيفة' applied to the poem means it is concise, smooth, and clear, and its small size in verses does not diminish its great knowledge.

?

Logic and Technical Terms

Defining Mabahith and Bahth in Kalam
Definition of mabahith and bahth as technical terms in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical meanings of two key kalam terms: mabahith (topics) and bahth (investigation), and how they relate to the study of the science.

Core Idea
In kalam, 'topics' are the propositions we examine, and 'investigation' is proving predicates for subjects.

Every science has its own vocabulary, and kalam is no exception. Before we dive into the arguments, we need to know precisely what the author means when he says 'the investigations of this art.' This brief passage defines two foundational terms: mabahith (the topics themselves) and bahth (the act of investigating). Understanding these definitions will help you follow the logical structure of the entire discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Notice the parallel structure: first defining 'mabahith' then 'bahth'.
Pay attention to the words 'جمع' and 'وهو' which introduce each definition.
2
Identify the definition of mabahith
What is the 'locus of investigation'? The author says it is 'the propositions' that we examine to attain the intended knowledge.
Mabahith are the topics or subject matter, not the act of searching.
3
Identify the definition of bahth
Bahth is defined both linguistically ('searching') and terminologically ('establishing predicates for subjects'). Note the shift from general to technical meaning.
This is a standard logical definition used in Islamic scholarship.
4
Reflect on the relationship
How do these two terms connect? Mabahith are the propositions (the 'where'), and bahth is the process (the 'how') of proving something about those propositions.
Together they describe the structure of inquiry in kalam.
5
Test your understanding
Try the checkpoint question to see if you can recall the technical meaning of 'bahth'.
If you get it wrong, re-read the definitions in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مباحث هذا الفنّ): جمع مبحث، وهو محلُّ البحث، وذلك المحل هو القضايا التي يبحث فيها عن تحصيل العلم المقصود بالذات. وأمَّا البحث فهو لغة التَّفتيش واصطلاحاً: إثبات المحمولات للموضوعات .
His saying: 'The investigations (mabahith) of this art': Plural of mabahith (investigation), and it is the locus of investigation, and that locus is the propositions in which one investigates the attainment of the knowledge intended essentially. As for investigation (bahth), it is linguistically searching, and terminologically: establishing predicates (mahmulat) for subjects (mawdu'at).
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a terminological definition. The author comments on the phrase 'the investigations of this art' from the sharh. He defines 'mabahith' (plural of mabhat) as 'the locus of research', specifying that this locus consists of the propositions (qadaya) in which one investigates the attainment of the knowledge that is the primary intended goal of the science. Then he defines 'bahth' (research/investigation) itself: linguistically it means 'searching', but as a technical term in logic and kalam, it means 'establishing predicates for subjects.' This is a standard Aristotelian/logical definition of investigation, which is the process of proving that a predicate belongs to a subject.

Key Terms
مباحث
مبحث
البحث
القضايا
المحمولات
الموضوعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may confuse mabahith with the actual inquiries or the act of investigation.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'mabahith' refers to the propositions themselves, not the act of inquiry. The act is 'bahth'.

Checkpoint
What is the technical meaning of 'bahth' according to this passage?
A) Searching for evidence
B) Establishing predicates for subjects
C) Classifying propositions
D) Defining the art of kalam

Key Takeaway
In kalam, 'topics' (mabahith) are the propositions under study, and 'investigation' (bahth) is the logical process of proving predications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Defining Qism and Its Plural Aqsam
Al-Sawi defines 'qism' and its plural, with linguistic and logical distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'qism' and its plural 'aqsam', the distinction between kull and kulli, and the different vowellings of the root q-s-m.

Core Idea
Arabic voweling distinguishes division into part, act, and oath.

This lesson unpacks the foundational term 'qism' (division) and its plural 'aqsam', which are central to logical classification in kalam. Al-Sawi carefully defines the word, links it to key concepts like whole (kull) and universal (kulli), and clarifies its different vowellings to avoid confusion with unrelated meanings. Understanding these distinctions is essential for following later discussions on the divisions of judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once through. Notice the technical terms and their definitions. Don't worry about full comprehension yet.
2
Understand the basic definitions
Focus on the definitions of 'qism', 'kull', 'kulli', 'juzʼ', 'juzʼi', and 'taqsim'. See how they relate to each other.
3
Identify the three vowellings
The commentary distinguishes three vowel patterns: qism (kasra+sukun), qasm (fatha+sukun), and qasam (fathatayn). Note their different meanings.
4
Link to the commentary
Read the provided commentary to deepen your understanding of why Al-Sawi emphasizes the linguistic distinctions.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the multiple-choice question to check if you grasped the key definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحكم

والأقسام جمع قِسم ؛ بكسر فسكون وهو ما اندرج مع غيره تحت كلّ أو كليّ، والكلُّ ما تركيب من جوهرين فأكثر، والكلي ما صدق على كثير، ويسمى المندرج تحت الكلِّ جزءاً أو بعضاً، والمندرج تحت الكلي جزئياً، ويسمى مورد القسمة وهو الكل أو الكلي مقسماً، بفتح فسكون فكسر، والتقسيم : التمييز والتفصيل ؛ أي: جعل الشيء أقساماً .

قوله : (جمع قسم ; بكسر فسكون احترز به عن الفتح مع السكون؛ فإنَّه مصدر قسم، والتَّقسيم أبلغ منه، إذ الأوّل صادق بجعل الشيء والثَّاني نصّ في الكثرة، وأمَّا القسم بفتحتين؛ فهو الحلف

قسمين

واليمين .
The divisions of judgment

And 'al-aqsam' is the plural of 'qism' with a kasra and sukun, and it is what falls together with others under a whole (kull) or a universal (kulli). The whole (kull) is what is composed of two or more substances, and the universal (kulli) is what applies to many. What falls under the whole is called a part (juzʼ) or a portion (baʻḍ), and what falls under the universal is called a particular (juzʼi). The locus of division is called the divided (muqassam) with a fatha, sukun, and kasra. And division (taqsim) is discrimination and detail, i.e., making a thing into divisions.

His saying: 'the plural of qism with a kasra and sukun—he avoids [the form] with a fatha and sukun, for it is the infinitive of qasama (to divide), and taqsim is more intense than it, since the first is true of making a thing [into divisions] and the second is explicit in multiplicity. As for 'qasam' with two fathas, it is the oath and the swearing.'
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage defines the key term 'qism' (division) and its plural 'aqsam', and distinguishes different vowellings. Al-Sawi begins by stating that 'al-aqsam' is the plural of 'qism' (with a kasra on the qaf and sukun on the sin). He defines 'qism' as something that falls together with other items under a whole (kull) or a universal (kulli). 'Kull' is defined as what is composed of two or more substances (essences), while 'kulli' is what applies to many individuals. Items under a 'kull' are called parts (juzʼ) or portions (baʻḍ); items under a 'kulli' are called particulars (juzʼi). The term 'muqassam' (divided) refers to the object undergoing division, whether it is a whole or a universal. Finally, 'taqsim' (division) means discrimination and detail, i.e., making something into divisions.

Then he comments on the author's phrase 'jamiʻ qism' (plural of qism). He explains that using the voweling 'qism' (kasra+sukun) avoids the pattern 'qasm' (fatha+sukun), which is the infinitive (masdar) of the verb 'qasama' (to divide). 'Taqsim' is more intensive than 'qasm' because 'qasm' can simply mean making something into divisions, while 'taqsim' explicitly indicates multiplicity. The voweling 'qasam' (two fathas) means 'oath' or 'swearing', a completely different meaning.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'qism' simply means 'division' in any sense, without distinguishing the act, the part, or the oath.
Correction: The passage shows that 'qism' specifically means a part or category under a whole or universal, while 'qasm' is the act of dividing, and 'qasam' is an oath.

Checkpoint
What does 'qism' (with kasra and sukun) mean according to this passage?
A) The act of dividing something.
B) An oath or swearing.
C) Something that falls together with others under a whole or universal.
D) The process of discrimination.

Key Takeaway
The root q-s-m yields three distinct words: qism (part), qasm (act), and qasam (oath); precise voweling is crucial in theological terminology.

Clarifying Kull, Kulli, and Logical Division Terms
Al-Sawi clarifies the terms 'kull', 'kulli', their examples, and the logical terms for division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between kull (whole) and kulli (universal), their examples, and related logical terminology.

Core Idea
Kull is a composite whole; kulli is a universal instantiated by individuals.

In this passage, al-Sawi provides concrete examples to distinguish between a whole (kull) and a universal (kulli). He also defines key terms used in logical division, such as the locus of division (mawrid al-qisma) and detail (tafsil). Understanding these distinctions is essential for grasping how classical logicians categorize and analyze concepts.

Guided Path
1
Study the examples of kull and kulli
Read the examples of 'whole' (mat composed of thread and rope) and 'universal' (human subsuming Zayd, Amr, Bakr). Note how they differ in nature: one is a composite object, the other is a concept with instances.
2
Understand 'composed of two or more substances'
Notice that this definition applies to both physical composites (mat) and hylomorphic composites (person as body and soul). This sets the scope of what qualifies as a 'whole'.
3
Grasp the scope of 'applies to many'
Realize that this covers both things that agree in essence (like human) and those that differ (like animal). It thereby includes genus, species, and other logical universals.
4
Learn the logical terms for division
Focus on 'mawrid al-qisma' (locus of division) as the origin of the divisions, and 'tafsil' (detail) as an explanatory term for the process of division itself.
These terms are specific to the terminology of the logicians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (تحت كل :أي كالحصير اندرج تحته الخيط والسمر. وقوله : (أو كلي :أي كالإنسان اندرج تحته زيد وعمرو وبكر. قوله : (ما تركَّب من جوهرين فأكثر) : أي : مثل الحصير وذات الشَّخص.

قوله : (ما) صدق على كثير ) أي متفق الحقيقة أو مختلفها، فيشمل الجنس والنوع وغيرهما ؛ نحو حيوان وإنسان وناطق، وضاحك وماش. قوله : (ويسمَّى المندرج . . . إلخ) : أي : في اصطلاح المناطقة.

قوله: (ويسمَّى مورد (القسمة أي محل ورودها، وهو : منشأ الأقسام.

قوله : (والتفصيل) : عطف تفسير .
His saying: 'under a whole (kull)' i.e., like a mat, under which thread and rope are subsumed. And his saying: 'or a universal (kulli)' i.e., like a human, under which Zayd, Amr, and Bakr are subsumed. His saying: 'what is composed of two or more substances' i.e., like a mat and the essence of a person.

His saying: 'what applies to many' i.e., whether agreeing in essence or differing, so it includes genus, species, and others; like animal, human, speaking, laughing, and walking. His saying: 'and what is subsumed... etc.' i.e., in the terminology of the logicians (mantiqiyyun).

His saying: 'and the locus of division (mawrid al-qisma) i.e., the place of its occurrence, and it is the origin of the divisions.'

His saying: 'and detail (al-tafsil)' is an explanatory conjunction.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples and further clarification of the terms 'kull' (whole) and 'kulli' (universal) mentioned earlier. Al-Sawi explains that 'under a whole' means something like a mat, which contains sub-parts like thread and rope. 'Under a universal' means like a human, which has individuals under it such as Zayd, Amr, and Bakr. 'Composed of two or more substances' applies to a mat (a composite of threads) and the essence of a person (body and soul).

Then he explains 'what applies to many' can be either things that agree in essence (like human—all individuals share the same humanity) or differ (like an animal—includes different species). This definition covers genus (jins), species (nawʻ), and other logical categories. He notes that the terms for what is subsumed (part, particular, etc.) are used in logic (by logicians).

Finally, he defines 'mawrid al-qisma' (locus of division) as the place where division occurs, which is the origin of the divisions. And 'tafsil' (detail) is an explanatory conjunction to 'taqsim' (division), meaning that division involves detailed separation.

Key Takeaway

Dividing Wholes vs. Universals
Al-Sawi defines 'aqsam' and distinguishes two types of division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between dividing a whole into parts and dividing a universal into particulars, and the logical signs that distinguish them.

Core Idea
Dividing a whole and a universal follow different rules: parts cannot be predicated, but particulars can.

In this passage, Al-Sawi lays out the basic terminology of logical division. He clarifies what a 'whole' and a 'universal' mean, and gives simple tests to tell them apart. Understanding these signs is essential for accurate reasoning in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the definitions of 'qism', 'kull', and 'kulli'.
Notice that 'kull' is a composite of substances, while 'kulli' applies to many.
2
Identify the two signs
Al-Sawi gives two distinct signs: one for dividing a whole into parts, and another for dividing a universal into particulars. Find these in the text.
The signs involve whether the divided term (muqsam) can be predicated of the division.
3
Apply the signs to examples
Consider the examples provided: mat (whole) and human (universal). Test whether the signs hold. For the mat, the whole is not predicated of its parts. For human, the universal is predicated of each particular.
This reinforces the logical distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الحكم. والأقسام جمع قِسم ؛ بكسر فسكون وهو ما اندرج مع غيره تحت كلّ أو كليّ، والكلُّ ما تركب من جوهرين فأكثر، والكلي ما صدق على كثير، ويسمى المندرج تحت الكلِّ جزءاً أو بعضاً، والمندرج تحت الكلي جزئياً، ويسمى مورد القسمة وهو الكل أو الكلي مقسماً، بفتح فسكون فكسر، والتقسيم : التمييز والتفصيل ؛ أي: جعل الشيء أقساماً . قوله : (جمع قسم ؛ بكسر فسكون احترز به عن الفتح مع السكون؛ فإنَّه مصدر قسم، والتَّقسيم أبلغ منه، إذ الأوّل صادق بجعل الشيء والثَّاني نصّ في الكثرة، وأمَّا القسم بفتحتين؛ فهو الحلف واليمين . قوله : (تحت كل :أي كالحصير اندرج تحته الخيط والسمر. وقوله : (أو كلي :أي كالإنسان اندرج تحته زيد وعمرو وبكر. قوله: (ما تركَّب من جوهرين فأكثر) : أي : مثل الحصير وذات الشَّخص. قوله : (ما) صدق على كثير ) أي متفق الحقيقة أو مختلفها، فيشمل الجنس والنوع وغيرهما ؛ نحو حيوان وإنسان وناطق، وضاحك وماش. قوله : (ويسمَّى المندرج . . . إلخ) : أي : في اصطلاح المناطقة. قوله: (ويسمَّى مورد (القسمة أي محل ورودها، وهو : : منشأ الأقسام. قوله : (والتفصيل) : عطف تفسير . وعلامة تقسيم الكلِّ إلى أجزائه صِحَّةُ انحلاله إلى الأجزاء التي تركبت منها، وعدم صحة حمل المقسم على الأقسام. وعلامة تقسيم الكلي إلى جزئياته صحة حمل المقسم على كلِّ من الأقسام :نحو زيد إنسان وعمرو إنسان. قوله : (صحة انحلاله :أي تفصيله بأن تحلَّ الحصير إلى خيط وسمر، بحيث يكون كل منهما على حدته. قوله : (وعدم صحة ... إلخ) : معطوف على (صحَّة) أي : لا يصحُ الإخبار بالمقسم عن أحد الأقسام، فلا تقول : الخيط حصير، ولا اليد أو الرجل إنسان مثلاً . قوله : (نحو زيد إنسان أي فزيد مثلاً جزئي من جزئيات الإنسان لا جزء.
The divisions of judgment. And 'al-aqsam' is the plural of 'qism' (with a kasra and sukun), and it is that which is subsumed with others under a whole (kull) or a universal (kulli). And 'al-kull' is that which is composed of two or more substances. And 'al-kulli' is that which applies to many. And what is subsumed under the whole is called a part (juz') or a portion (ba'd), and what is subsumed under the universal is called a particular (juz'i). And the locus of division (mawrid al-qisma) — which is the whole or the universal — is called 'muqsam' (with fath, sukun, kasr). And 'al-taqsim' is distinction and detail; i.e., making a thing into divisions. He said: (plural of qism; with kasra and sukun) is a precaution against (the word) with fatha and sukun, for that is the masdar of qasama, and al-taqsim is more emphatic than it, since the first applies to making a thing and the second is explicit in multiplicity. As for 'al-qasm' with two fatha, it is the oath and the swearing. He said: (under a whole) like a mat, under which are subsumed the thread and the wire. And He said: (or a universal) like 'human', under which are subsumed Zayd, 'Amr, and Bakr. He said: (that which is composed of two or more substances) i.e., like the mat and the person's self. He said: (that which applies to many) i.e., whether agreeing in essence or differing, so it includes the genus, species, and others; like 'animal', 'human', 'speaker', 'laugher', and 'walker'. He said: (and what is subsumed ... etc.) i.e., in the terminology of the logicians. He said: (and the locus of division is called) i.e., the place of its occurrence, and it is the source of the divisions. He said: (and 'al-tafsil') is an explicative conjunction. And the sign of dividing a whole into its parts is the validity of its dissolution into the parts from which it was composed, and the invalidity of predicating the divided term (muqsam) upon the divisions. And the sign of dividing a universal into its particulars is the validity of predicating the divided term upon each of the divisions, like 'Zayd is a human' and ''Amr is a human'. He said: (validity of its dissolution) i.e., its detail by dissolving the mat into thread and wire, such that each of them is separate. He said: (and the invalidity ... etc.) is conjoined with 'validity', i.e., it is not correct to inform with the divided term about any one of the divisions, so you do not say 'the thread is a mat', nor 'the hand or the leg is a human', for example. He said: (like 'Zayd is a human') i.e., Zayd, for example, is a particular from the particulars of human, not a part.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage explains the terminology of division (qisma) as used in logic and kalam. It begins by defining 'al-aqsam' (the divisions) as the plural of 'qism', which is a subclass placed alongside others under a whole (kull) or a universal (kulli). The whole is a composite of two or more substances, such as a mat composed of thread and wire. The universal is a concept that applies to many individuals, such as 'human'. The subclass under a whole is called a part (juz') or portion (ba'd), while the subclass under a universal is called a particular (juz'i). The locus of division is called 'muqsam' (the thing divided). Then the text distinguishes between 'qasm' (source division) and 'taqsim' (the act of dividing), noting that 'taqsim' is more emphatic. The sign of dividing a whole into its parts is the ability to dissolve the whole into its constituent parts, but the term for the whole cannot be predicated of any one part (e.g., the thread is not a mat). The sign of dividing a universal into its particulars is the ability to predicate the universal term of each particular (e.g., Zayd is human). The commentary provides illustrative examples and clarifies that these distinctions are from the terminology of logicians. This passage is crucial for understanding how kalam arguments handle classification and division.

Key Terms
أقسام
قسم
كل
كلي
جزء
جزئي
تقسيم
مقسم
مورد القسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that dividing a whole and dividing a universal follow the same rule, or that parts can be called by the name of the whole.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that for a whole, the divided term cannot be applied to the parts, while for a universal, it can be applied to each particular. For example, a thread is not a mat, but Zayd is human.

Checkpoint
What is the sign of dividing a whole (كُلّ) into its parts?
A) Validity of predicating the divided term upon each part.
B) Validity of dissolving the whole into its parts and invalidity of predicating the divided term upon the parts.
C) Validity of predicating the divided term upon the whole.
D) Inability to dissolve the whole into parts.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction in logical division is that parts of a whole do not bear the name of the whole, while particulars of a universal do bear the name of the universal.

The lesson shows that a part (like a thread) cannot bear the name of the whole (mat). Think of a community you belong to—have you ever assumed that a single action of one member defines the whole? How does the rule of dividing a whole challenge you to correct that assumption starting today?

Components of Hukm and the Pronoun Problem
Al-Sawi lists components of hukm and resolves a pronoun ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three components of a judgment (hukm) and how the definition applies even to initial negations through proper pronoun reference.

Core Idea
A judgment always involves a subject, a predicate, and affirmation or negation.

Al-Sawi begins by breaking down the definition of hukm into its essential parts: the subject, the predicate, and the act of affirming or negating. He then addresses a potential confusion: does the definition exclude negations that are stated from the start, without prior affirmation? Through careful pronoun analysis, he shows that all negations are included.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three components
Read the opening sentences and note the three elements Al-Sawi lists: the subject (mahkum 'alayh), the predicate (mahkum bihi), and affirmation (ithbat).
The third component is the core of the definition.
2
Understand the definition
Focus on the phrase 'affirmation of something for something or negation of it from it'. See how it applies to examples like 'Zayd is standing'.
3
Examine the pronoun issue
Al-Sawi raises a problem: if the pronoun in 'negation of it' refers back to the thing affirmed, then initial negations are excluded. Consider the example 'Zayd is not standing' said without prior affirmation.
This is a common point of confusion.
4
Learn the resolution
Al-Sawi answers that the pronoun refers to 'the thing' without the restriction of prior affirmation. He distinguishes this from the 'dirham and its half' construction where the pronoun must refer to a different thing.
The distinction lies in whether the pronoun can refer back to the same unspecified thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها : المحكوم عليه؛ كزيد في المثال، ومنها : المحكوم به؛ كالقيام في المثال، ومنها إثبات أمر وهو المراد هنا، فقوله: (إثبات أمر لأمر) كإثبات القيام لزيد في : زيد قائم . قوله : (أو نفيه عنه :أي: عن أمر والمتبادر أنَّ الضمير في (نفيه) : عائد على الأمر المقيَّد بالإثبات، وحينئذ فلا يشمل التعريف ما إذا نفي من أول وهلة من غير تقدُّم إثبات، كأن تقول ابتداء: زيد ليس بقائم. والجواب : أنَّ الضَّمير عائد على الأمر لا بالقيد المتقدم، وليس من قبيل عندي درهم ونصفه؛ لأنَّ قوله : ( ونصفه لا يصح عوده على الدرهم السابق ولا على مطلق الدّرهم الصَّادق بالأوَّل كما هنا، وإنَّما تعيَّن فيه عود الضمير لدرهم آخر غير السابق. و(أو) في التّعريف ليست للشَّكٍّ؛ لأنَّها لا تدخل في التعريف رسماً كان أو حداً؛ لأنَّ الشَّكَ لا يجامع التّصور جزماً الذي هو المقصود من التّعريف، وإنَّما هي للتنويع ، و (أو) التي للتنويع تدخل في الرسم دون الحد؛ لأنَّه يلزم على دخولها في الحدّ كون الفصل مساوياً لماهيته وأخص منها؛ لأنَّ الفصل الواقع في الحدّ مساو للماهية قطعاً، فحيث ذكر فصل آخر يقوم مقامه توجد معه الماهيَّة؛ لزم أن تكون الماهية أعم . والفرض مساواته لها . منه، قوله: (والحاكم به أي بالحكم لا بالمعنى المذكور، كما هو ظاهر، بل بمعنى المحكوم به ففيه استخدام ويصح أن يكون الضَّمير على الأمر؛ أي: والحاكم بالأمر المثبت لغيره وهو المحكوم به . عائداً
And among them: the subject of judgment (al-mahkum 'alayh), like Zayd in the example, and among them: the predicate of judgment (al-mahkum bihi), like standing in the example, and among them: affirmation of something (ithbat amr) which is what is intended here. So his saying: (Affirmation of something for something) like the affirmation of standing for Zayd in: 'Zayd is standing'. His saying: (or negation of it from it) i.e., from something. And what is immediately apparent is that the pronoun in 'negation of it' returns to the thing qualified by affirmation. In that case, the definition does not include when something is negated from the very beginning without prior affirmation, like when you say initially: 'Zayd is not standing'. And the answer is that the pronoun returns to the thing without the prior qualification, and it is not of the type of 'I have a dirham and its half', because his saying 'and its half' is not validly returnable to the previous dirham nor to an absolute dirham that applies to the first, as here. Rather, it necessarily returns to another dirham different from the previous. And (or) in the definition is not for doubt, because it does not enter into a definition, whether it be a descriptive definition (rasm) or a true definition (hadd), because doubt does not coexist with certain conceptualization (tasawwur jazm) which is the purpose of definition. Rather, it is for diversification (tanwi'). And (or) which is for diversification enters into the descriptive definition (rasm) but not into the true definition (hadd), because if it enters into the true definition, the differentia (fasl) would be coextensive with the essence and more specific than it, since the differentia in the true definition is certainly coextensive with the essence. So if another differentia is mentioned that replaces it, the essence would exist with it, so the essence would be more general, but the assumption is that it is coextensive with it. From it: His saying: (and the judge by it) i.e., by the judgment (hukm), not by the mentioned meaning, as is apparent, but in the sense of the predicate (al-mahkum bihi). Thus there is a use (istikhdam), and it is valid that the pronoun refer to the thing (al-amr); i.e., the judge by the thing affirmed for something else, which is the predicate. Returning.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of 'hukm' (judgment) in kalam. It begins by identifying the three components of a judgment: the subject (mahkum 'alayh), the predicate (mahkum bihi), and the affirmation (ithbat) itself. The author then addresses a potential problem in the definition 'affirmation of something for something or negation of it from it': the pronoun in 'negation of it' might be understood to refer back to the thing previously affirmed, which would exclude cases of negation ab initio (without prior affirmation). The answer is that the pronoun refers to 'the thing' without the qualification of affirmation, so it includes all negations. This is distinguished from the Arabic construction 'I have a dirham and its half' where the pronoun cannot refer back to the same dirham. Then the author explains that the word 'or' (aw) in the definition is not for doubt (shakk), because doubt is incompatible with the certain conceptualization (tasawwur jazm) that definitions aim for. Instead, 'aw' is for diversification (tanwi'), which is permissible in a descriptive definition (rasm) but not in a true definition (hadd). The reason given is that in a hadd, the differentia (fasl) is coextensive with the essence; if another differentia could replace it, the essence would be more general, violating coextensiveness. Finally, the author clarifies the phrase 'al-hakim bihi' (the judge by it), explaining that it refers to the predicate (mahkum bihi) and not the act of judging.

Key Terms
hukm
mahkum 'alayh
mahkum bihi
ithbat
nafy

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of hukm only applies to negations that follow a prior affirmation.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that the pronoun in 'negation of it' refers to the thing without the condition of affirmation, so initial negations are included.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the dirham analogy in Al-Sawi's discussion?
A) To show that pronouns can refer to different things in different contexts.
B) To illustrate that the pronoun in 'negation of it' must refer to a different thing than the one affirmed.
C) To prove that 'and its half' is a valid construction in Arabic.
D) To argue that the definition of hukm is flawed.

Key Takeaway
The definition of hukm covers all affirmations and negations because the pronoun in 'negation of it' refers to the thing in an absolute sense, not limited by prior affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a definition in Islamic texts, are you patient enough to resolve pronoun ambiguities, or do you jump to conclusions? How might Al-Sawi's careful analysis inspire you to be more precise in your own understanding of Aqeedah? What practical step can you take this week to verify a meaning you previously assumed?
?
Al-Sawi shows that the definition of hukm covers both initial affirmations and initial negations. In your life, do you sometimes restrict God's commands to only certain situations, thinking they don't apply to you because you haven't explicitly practiced them? How can you embrace the absolute applicability of divine rulings?
?
Consider the analogy of 'a dirham and its half'—the pronoun forced a different referent. Are there areas where you mistakenly assume that a pronoun or phrase refers back to the same previous context, leading to a misunderstanding of a Quranic verse or hadith? How can you develop the habit of checking such references more carefully?

The Meaning of 'Aw' in Definitions and Clarifying the Judge
Al-Sawi explains the function of 'aw' and clarifies a phrase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'aw' (or) in the definition of hukm indicates diversification (tanwi') rather than doubt, and how this relates to the distinction between descriptive and true definitions. You will also learn the correct interpretation of 'al-hakim bihi'.

Core Idea
'Or' in a definition never means doubt—it signals a choice between equally valid ways to conceptualize.

Al-Sawi now addresses the word 'aw' in the definition. Some might think it introduces doubt, but he explains that doubt is incompatible with the certain conceptualization that definitions aim for. Instead, 'aw' indicates diversification (tanwi'), which is allowed in descriptive definitions (rasm) but not in true definitions (hadd). He then clarifies a related phrase about 'the judge'.

Guided Path
1
Understand why 'aw' cannot indicate doubt
Al-Sawi states that doubt (shakk) cannot coexist with the certain conceptualization (tasawwur jazm) that definitions produce, so 'aw' must have another meaning.
This is a logical point: definitions are meant to give certainty.
2
Distinguish between rasm and hadd
Al-Sawi says 'aw' for diversification (tanwi') is permissible in rasm but not in hadd. Learn why: in a hadd, the differentia (fasl) must be coextensive with the essence, so alternative differentiae would imply the essence is more general.
This is a technical distinction in logic.
3
Clarify the phrase 'al-hakim bihi'
Al-Sawi interprets 'the judge by it' as meaning the predicate (al-mahkum bihi), not the act of judging. He notes a rhetorical device (istikhdam) where the same word is used in two senses.
This resolves a potential ambiguity in the text.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Term 'Fahm' and the First Division
Clarifies pronunciation and introduces the first division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct pronunciation and meaning of 'fahm' and see how the commentator transitions to the first major division of the book: Ilahiyyat.

Core Idea
Correct pronunciation unlocks precise meaning.

Before diving into divine matters, the commentator clarifies a key term. The word 'fahm' (perception) must be pronounced correctly to distinguish it from a similar word meaning 'understanding.' This lexical precision sets the stage for the study of Ilahiyyat, or divine matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice the diacritical marks and the short statement about the three divisions.
The Arabic is short but packed with information.
2
Focus on the lexical point
Identify the correct vowelization of 'fahm' and why it matters. The commentary explains that 'fahm' with fatha means perception, while with kasra it means understanding.
This distinction is important for accurate reading of Islamic texts.
3
Understand the exhortation
The commentator urges you to understand 'these three divisions' after their mention. This refers to the upcoming division of the book into Ilahiyyat, and likely two more.
The three divisions are a structural guide for the entire work.
4
Identify the first division
The passage ends by naming 'The First Section: Ilahiyyat.' Recognize that this is the beginning of the theological discussion.
Ilahiyyat covers topics about Allah, His attributes, and acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بفتح الهمزة جمع فهم) ، وهو الإدراك ؛ أي: العلم والمعرفة، فإنَّ من أعطي لذة العلوم والمعارف ؛ فقد أعطي خيري الدنيا والآخرة.
فافهم هذه الأقسام الثلاثة بعد ذكرها وعدها .
قوله : (بفتح الهمزة احترز به عن كسرها، إذ معناه : التَّفهم، وليس مراداً هنا .
القسم الأول
الإلهيات
With the fatha (vowel) on the hamza, [جمع فهم] which is perception; i.e., knowledge and cognition. For whoever is given the pleasure of sciences and cognitions has been given the goods of this world and the Hereafter. So understand these three divisions after their mention and counting. His statement: (with the fatha on the hamza) is to guard against its being with kasra, for its meaning [with kasra] is 'understanding' (tafahhum), and that is not intended here. The First Section: Ilahiyyat (Divine Matters).
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage combines a lexical clarification and a structural outline. First, the commentator explains the correct pronunciation of the word 'فهم' (fahm), noting that it has a fatha on the hamza to distinguish it from the form with kasra (فِهْم, fihm), which means 'understanding' (tafahhum) and is not intended here. He states that 'فهم' means perception ('الإدراك'), i.e., knowledge and cognition ('العلم والمعرفة'), and that whoever is granted the delight of such knowledge has received the best of both this world and the next. He then exhorts the reader to understand 'these three divisions' (الأقسام الثلاثة), which have presumably been enumerated earlier. After this, he begins the first division: 'Ilahiyyat' (Divine Matters). The passage thus serves as a transition from a general lexical point to the main subject matter of the work.

Key Terms
فهم
الإدراك
الإلهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'فهم' is pronounced 'فِهْم' (with kasra) and means perception.
Correction: The correct pronunciation is 'فَهْم' (with fatha), meaning perception. The form with kasra means 'understanding' and is not intended here.

Checkpoint

A) فَهْم (fahm)
B) فِهْم (fihm)
C) فُهْم (fuhm)
D) فَهِم (fahim)

Key Takeaway
The term 'fahm' (perception) must be pronounced correctly, and it leads into the first major section of the book: Ilahiyyat.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Definition of Taklif and Its Scope
Definition of taklif and its scope over different types of acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of taklif (legal obligation) and which acts fall under it.

Core Idea
Taklif is imposing or demanding difficulty — but not all demands are impositions.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational definition of taklif as understood by the Ash'ari tradition. The passage presents two competing definitions and clarifies which acts are covered, setting the stage for who is obligated.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully. Notice the two definitions: 'ilzam ma fihi kulfa' and 'talab ma fihi kulfa'.
The first is considered sound.
2
Compare the two definitions
Identify the difference between 'imposing' and 'demanding'. Which acts are included under each? The text says mandub and makruh are excluded on the first but included on the second.
3
Understand the consensus on mubah
The passage states that mubah is never considered taklif by consensus. Reflect on why.
Because it involves no difficulty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف التكليف
حاشية الصاوي على شرح الخريدة البهية
على المكلَّف من الثَّقلين الإنس والجن، والتكليف : إلزام ما فيه كُلفة ، وقيل : طلب ما فيه كلفة فلا تكليف بالمندوب والمكروه على الأول الصحيح، بخلاف الثاني، ولا تكليف بالمباح اتفاقاً .
والمكلّف : البالغ العاقل
قوله : (من التَّقلين): سمُّوا بذلك لكونهم مثقلون بالتكاليف، أو مثقلون الأرض، فهو اسم مفعول، أو اسم فاعل.
قوله : (الإنس والجن أي خاصة وأمَّا الملائكة؛ فليسوا مكلَّفين بالمعرفة، إذ هي ضروريَّة في حقهم ؛ كالنَّفس .
قوله : إلزام ما فيه كلفة :أي فعلاً كالواجب، أو تركاً ؛ كالحرام. قوله : (طلب ما فيه كلفة أي فعلاً أو تركاً جازماً أو لا
قوله : (فلا تكليف بالمندوب والمكروه أي : وإن كانا مطلوبين .
قوله : (على الأوّل الصَّحيح) : أي : وعليه فالصَّبيُّ غير مكلف. قوله : (بخلاف الثَّاني : أي : وهو طلب ما فيه كلفة، فالمندوب والمكروه مكلف بهما، وعليه فالصَّبيُّ مكلّف، وقوله في تعريف المكلف البالغ العاقل : إمَّا على القول الأوّل، أو تعريف للمكلَّف الكامل.
قوله : (والمكلف : البالغ العاقل): هذا تعريف للمكلف من الإنس، وأمَّا الجن؛ فهم مكلَّفون من حين الخلقة .
قوله : (البالغ) : أي : وأمَّا الصَّبيُّ : فليس مكلَّفاً .
Definition of taklif (legal obligation). Hashiya of al-Sawi on the commentary of al-Kharidat al-Bahiyya. Upon the mukallaf from the thaqalayn (the two weighty ones) – humans and jinn. And taklif is: imposing what involves difficulty (ilzam ma fihi kulfa), and it is said: demanding what involves difficulty (talab ma fihi kulfa). So there is no taklif concerning the mandub (recommended) and the makruh (disliked) according to the first, which is sound; contrary to the second. And there is no taklif concerning the mubah (permissible) by consensus. And the mukallaf is: the adult, sane person. His saying: '(from the thaqalayn)' – they are so named because they are burdened with obligations, or because they burden the earth; it is either a passive participle or an active participle. His saying: '(humans and jinn)' meaning specifically; as for the angels, they are not obligated to know [Allah], for it is necessary for them like breathing. His saying: 'imposing what involves difficulty' meaning an act like the obligatory, or an omission like the forbidden. His saying: '(demanding what involves difficulty)' meaning an act or omission, either decisively or not. His saying: '(so there is no taklif concerning the mandub and makruh)' meaning even though they are demanded. His saying: '(according to the first, which is sound)' meaning: and upon it, the child is not obligated. His saying: '(contrary to the second)' meaning: it is demanding what involves difficulty, so the mandub and makruh are obligated by them, and upon it, the child is obligated. And his saying in the definition of the mukallaf 'the adult, sane' is either according to the first opinion, or a definition of the complete mukallaf. His saying: '(and the mukallaf: the adult, sane)' – this is a definition of the mukallaf from among humans; as for the jinn, they are obligated from the moment of creation. His saying: '(the adult)' meaning: as for the child, he is not obligated.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines taklif (legal obligation) and identifies the mukallaf (the person subject to obligation). It is a combination of the original matn and al-Sawi's gloss. The main text states that taklif applies to the thaqalayn (humans and jinn) and gives two definitions: (1) ilzam ma fihi kulfa – imposing what involves difficulty, and (2) talab ma fihi kulfa – demanding what involves difficulty. According to the first definition, mandub (recommended) and makruh (disliked) are not considered taklif because they are not imposed (only obligatory and forbidden are imposed). According to the second definition, they are included because they are demanded. The first is considered sound. There is no taklif concerning mubah (permissible) by unanimous agreement. The mukallaf is defined as the adult (baligh) and sane (aqil). The commentary then explains: the term thaqalayn refers to their being burdened with obligations or burdening the earth. Angels are not obligated to know Allah because such knowledge is necessary for them, like breathing. The two definitions are further elucidated: ilzam involves an act like wajib or an omission like haram; talab involves an act or omission either decisively or non-decisively. The implication for children (sabi) is that according to the first definition, a child is not obligated, but according to the second, a child is obligated. The final definition of mukallaf as baligh and aqil applies to humans; jinn are obligated from the moment of their creation. Thus, the passage clarifies the boundaries of obligation and who bears it.

Correction: The passage clarifies that only wajib and haram are taklif under the sound definition; mandub and makruh are not considered taklif.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Taklif is defined as imposing difficulty, and only obligatory and forbidden acts fall under it by the sound definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson's distinction between imposed taklif and voluntary demand change your motivation to pursue such acts out of love rather than burden?
?
Think of a haram act you struggle with. Do you truly feel that Allah's prohibition is an imposition (ilzam) meant to protect you, or do you resent it as unnecessary difficulty? How does understanding taklif as 'imposing what involves difficulty' reshape your heart towards obedience?
?
If taklif by the sound definition only covers wajib and haram, why do you often feel guilty about missing a mandub or committing a makruh? Is that guilt from your own perfectionism or from a genuine desire to draw closer to Allah? How can you align your sense of accountability with what is actually imposed upon you?

Conditions of the Mukallaf: Who is Obligated?
Conditions for being a mukallaf and classification of beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for being a mukallaf (obligated person) and which beings are included or excluded.

Core Idea
Only adult sane humans (and all jinn) are obligated; angels and children are not.

Having defined taklif itself, we now turn to who is subject to it. The passage specifies that the mukallaf must be adult and sane, and clarifies that humans and jinn are obligated while angels are not. It also addresses the status of children.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on conditions
Identify the two explicit conditions: baligh (adult) and aqil (sane).
2
Explore the thaqalayn
Understand why humans and jinn are called 'thaqalayn' and why angels are excluded from obligation to know Allah.
Angels have necessary knowledge, so they are not commanded to acquire it.
3
Consider the case of children
The passage presents two views: children are not obligated according to the sound definition (ilzam), but are obligated according to the second definition (talab).
