Jawhar and 'Arad: Definitions and Their Rational Import
Author defines substance and accident and explains perception of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad) as used in kalam, how accidents are perceived, and why divine attributes are not called accidents.

Core Idea
Everything other than God is either a self-subsisting substance or an accident that depends on it.

In this passage, the author lays the conceptual groundwork for the proof of the world's temporal origination. He defines the two primary categories of created existence: substance and accident. He also explains how accidents are known to us—some through the senses, others through the intellect—and why the eternal attributes of God cannot be classified as accidents. This foundation is essential for the argument that the world is originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of jawhar and 'arad
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the precise wording: substance 'stands by itself', accident 'stands by another'.
2
Understand the claim of temporal origination without doubt
The author asserts that for anyone who reflects, the temporal origination of everything other than God is indubitable. This sets up the argument against the philosophers who claim the world is eternal.
3
Explore the classification of accidents by perception
The passage lists six types: five by the external senses and one (like power, will, knowledge) by the intellect. Recognize that all accidents are existent and potentially visible, though some are veiled.
4
Note the distinction between temporal accidents and eternal divine attributes
Accidents are called 'arad because they occur to a substance and are temporal. God's attributes are eternal and cannot be temporal, so they are not called accidents.
5
Reflect on the subtle criticism of those who deny the world's origination
The final phrase 'for anyone who reflects' is a pointed remark that those who claim the world is eternal have not truly reflected on the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ فسره بقوله : (أي ما ) أي : الشَّيءُ الذي هو (سوى الله العلي العالما) - نعت الله تعالى على القطع، فهو منصوب على المدح، وألفه للإطلاق - من الجواهر والأعراض، والجوهر: ما قام بنفسه والعَرَض : ما قام بغيره من الجواهر كالألوان (من غير شكٍّ) متعلّق بقوله : (حادثُ) أي : موجود بعد عدم، وهو خبر (أنَّ) أي : إنَّ حدوثه غير مشكوك فيه لمن تأمل، قوله : (والأعراض) : اعلم أنَّ بعضها يدرك بالذوق؛ كالحلاوة والملوحة والمرارة، وبعضها يدرك بالسَّمع ؛ كالأصوات، وبعضها بالبصر؛ كالألوان، وبعضها بالشَّم ؛ كالروائح، وبعضها باللمس؛ كالحرارة والبرودة، والنُّعومة والخشونة. وأمَّا مثل القدرة والإرادة والعلم الحادثة : فإنَّما تدرك بالعقل، وكذا بقيَّة المعاني، وهذه الأعراض كلُّها موجودة يصح رؤيتها، وبعضها يرى بالفعل ؛ كالألوان والأجسام، وبعضها لم ير بالفعل؛ لوجود مانع وحجاب خلقه الله تعالى من رؤيتها بالفعل لا اطلاع لنا على حقيقة ذلك الحجاب، وذلك كبعض صفات المعاني القائمة بنا والرَّوائح والأصوات ونحو ذلك . قوله : (والعرض : ما قام بغيره : سمّي عرضاً ؛ لأنَّه يعرض لما قام به ويطرأ عليه، ومن ثمَّ لا يقال في صفات الله تعالى : أعراض؛ لأنَّها أزليَّة يستحيل عليها الطُّرقُ . وقوله : (من) الجواهر) بيان لغيره. قوله : (لمن تأمل) : فيه تعريض بمن يقول : إنَّ العالم قديم؛ فإنَّه لم يتأمل .
Then he explained it by his saying: (i.e., what) i.e., the thing which is (other than God, the Most High, the All-Knowing) - an attribute of God, may He be exalted, in the definitive sense, thus it is in the accusative of praise, and its alif is for absolute [form] - of substances and accidents. The substance (jawhar) is what subsists by itself, and the accident ('arad) is what subsists by other than it from among the substances like colors. (Without any doubt) is attached to his saying: (originated) i.e., existing after non-existence, and it is the predicate of (that) i.e., that its origination is not doubted by anyone who reflects. His saying: (And the accidents): Know that some of them are perceived by taste, like sweetness, saltiness, and bitterness; some are perceived by hearing, like sounds; some by sight, like colors; some by smell, like odors; some by touch, like heat and cold, softness and roughness. As for things like power, will, and originated knowledge, they are perceived by intellect, and likewise the rest of the meanings. And all these accidents are existing, and it is permissible to see them; some are seen actually, like colors and bodies; some have not been seen actually due to the existence of a barrier and veil created by God, may He be exalted, that prevents their actual seeing, and we have no knowledge of the reality of that veil. That is like some of the attributes of meanings subsisting in us, and odors, sounds, and the like. His saying: (And the accident is what subsists by other than it): It is called 'arad because it occurs to (ya'rid) what it subsists in and happens to it; hence it is not said of God's attributes that they are accidents, because they are eternal and it is impossible for them to be temporal. And his saying: (from the substances) is an explanation of 'other than it'. His saying: (for anyone who reflects): Therein is an allusion to those who say the world is eternal, for he did not reflect.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms 'substance' (jawhar) and 'accident' ('arad) used in the proof of the world's temporal origination. The author first explains that the phrase 'what is other than God' refers to substances and accidents. He then gives the classic definitions: a substance subsists by itself, an accident subsists in a substance (e.g., colors). He asserts that the world's temporal origination is beyond doubt for anyone who reflects. Next, he discusses the perception of accidents: some are perceived by the five senses (taste, hearing, sight, smell, touch), while others like power, will, and originated knowledge are perceived by the intellect. He notes that all accidents exist and can be seen in principle, but some are not actually seen due to a divine veil, the nature of which is unknown to us. Finally, he explains why God's attributes are not called accidents: accidents are temporal (they 'occur' to substances), whereas God's attributes are eternal and cannot be temporal. The passage ends with a subtle criticism of those who claim the world is eternal, implying they have not reflected properly.

Everything besides God is either a substance (self-subsisting) or an accident (dependent on a substance), and all such entities are temporally originated—a truth that should be evident to anyone who reflects.

Clarifying the Meaning of Doubt in the Context of Aqeedah
Two marginal notes clarifying the term 'shakk' in this aqeedah context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'shakk' in this aqeedah text refers to any uncertainty in belief, including assumption and illusion, not just literal equal hesitation.

Core Idea
In aqeedah, any uncertainty—even a leaning opinion—is as harmful as pure doubt.

This short marginal note does something crucial: it tells us not to read 'shakk' narrowly. In everyday language, 'doubt' means hesitation between two equal options. But when it comes to matters of faith, the author uses it to cover all forms of uncertainty, including strong inclinations (ẓann) and weak ones (wahm). Understanding this saves us from missing the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the original note
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the gist.
2
Identify the two parts
The first part defines the literal meaning of shakk. The second part explains the intended meaning in context.
3
Understand the inclusion of ẓann and wahm
The note says they harm the creed just like shakk. Why? Because any uncertainty in creedal matters is detrimental, no matter how slight.
This is the key insight of the passage.
4
Apply the lesson
When you encounter 'shakk' in aqeedah texts, remember it includes all forms of uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وحقيقة الشَّكِّ) : أي : أصل معناه . قوله : (ومراده به هنا) : أي بقرينة المقام؛ لأنَّ الظَّنَّ والوهم يضرّان في العقيدة؛ كالشَّكِّ .
His saying: 'And the reality of doubt' means its original meaning. His saying: 'And his intent here' means by contextual evidence, because assumption (ẓann) and illusion (wahm) harm the creed just as doubt does.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage consists of two marginal notes clarifying the term 'shakk' (doubt). The first note states that the 'reality of doubt' refers to its original linguistic meaning, which is hesitation between two equal possibilities. The second note specifies the author's intended meaning in this context: due to the context, the word 'doubt' is used broadly to include assumption (ẓann, a stronger inclination) and illusion (wahm, a weaker inclination). All of these, when they concern creedal matters, are harmful to one's faith, just as pure doubt is. The point is that any degree of uncertainty in matters of belief is detrimental.

Temporal Origination: Definition and Refutation of the Philosophers
Definition of ḥaduth, refutation of philosophers, and assertion of exclusive opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of temporal origination (ḥaduth), the philosophers' erroneous view, and why there is no middle ground between temporal origination and eternity.

Core Idea
True ḥaduth means existence after non-existence; anything else is disbelief.

Al-Sawi begins by clarifying the technical meaning of ḥaduth (temporal origination) as the opposite of qidam (eternity). He immediately warns against the philosophers' redefinition of the term, which leads to denying God's exclusive eternity. This passage establishes the boundaries of correct belief on creation's beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ḥaduth
Read the first sentence where Al-Sawi defines ḥaduth as 'existence after non-existence'. Notice the direct statement.
This is the core definition that governs the whole discussion.
2
Identify the philosophers' error
Find where Al-Sawi mentions the philosophers' claim. They use ḥaduth in a different sense (need of another), not actual origination. Note the ruling of disbelief.
The consensus on disbelief is a critical point.
3
Understand the opposite of ḥaduth
Read the explanation of 'opposite'. Al-Sawi clarifies that it means counterpart, not logical opposite, and identifies qidam as the unique attribute of Allah.
Qidam belongs to Allah alone; everything else is ḥādith.
4
See the rejection of a middle ground
Examine the statement 'no middle ground between ḥaduth and qidam'. Al-Sawi denies any intermediate category, contrary to philosophers' claims.
This closes any loophole for compromise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدوثه وُجوده بعد العَدَم) يعني : أن حدوث العالم عبارة عن وجوده بعد عدمه، خلافاً للفلاسفة ؛ فإنَّهم ذهبوا إلى قدمه، ومع ذلك أطلقوا القول بحدوث ما سوى الله تعالى لكن بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الغير، لا بمعنى سبق العدم عليه، ومعتقد ذلك كافر بإجماع المسلمين . (وضدُّه) أي ضد الحدوث أي مقابله؛ يعني: عدم أوَّليَّة الوجود (هو المسمى بالقِدَم ولا يكون إلا الله وحده كما سيأتي، ولا واسطة بين الحدوث والقدم. قوله : (خلافاً للفلاسفة :أي: فإنَّهم ذهبوا إلى أنَّ قدمه بالتَّبع لقدمه تعالى بطريق العلَّة، ويسمونه أيضاً : قدماً زمانيّاً، وأمَّا قدمه تعالى؛ فهو قدم ذاتي، وتقدَّم إيضاحه (۱) . قوله : (لكن بمعنى الاحتياج إلى الغير) : أي : إنَّ قدم هذا العالم مستند إلى قدمه تعالى؛ أي: فقدمه تعالى أوجب قدم هذا العالم هكذا زعموا قبَّحهم الله تعالى . قوله : (أي مقابله) : أشار بذلك إلى أنَّه ليس المراد بالضّد حقيقته، بل المراد به مطلق المقابل، فتقابل القدم والحدوث من مقابلة الشَّيء والمساوي لنقيضه ؛ لأنَّ نقيض الحدوث لا حدوث، ولا حدوث مساوٍ للقدم . قوله : (ولا واسطة بين الحدوث والقدم) : أي : خلافاً للفلاسفة، وتقدَّم
(Its temporal origination (ḥaduth) is its existence after nonexistence) — meaning: that the temporal origination of the world is that it exists after its nonexistence, contrary to the philosophers (falāsifa); for they held that it is eternal (qadīm), yet they still asserted the temporal origination of all besides God, but in the sense of need of another, not in the sense of preceding nonexistence. And whoever believes that is a disbeliever (kāfir) by consensus of the Muslims. (And its opposite) — i.e., the opposite of temporal origination, i.e., its counterpart — meaning: the privation of primacy of existence. It is what is called eternity (qidam), and it is only for God alone as will come, and there is no middle ground between temporal origination and eternity. His saying: (contrary to the philosophers): i.e., for they held that its eternity is dependent on His eternity by way of causality, and they also call it temporal eternity (qidam zamānī); as for His eternity, it is essential eternity (qidam dhātī), and its clarification preceded. His saying: (but in the sense of need of another): i.e., the eternity of this world is based on His eternity; i.e., His eternity necessitated the eternity of this world — thus they claimed, may God deform them. His saying: (i.e., its counterpart): he indicated by that that what is meant by 'opposite' is not its true meaning, but rather absolute counterpart. So the opposition of eternity and temporal origination is of the type of opposition between a thing and what is equivalent to its contradictory; because the contradictory of temporal origination is 'not temporal origination', and 'not temporal origination' is equivalent to eternity. His saying: (and there is no middle ground between temporal origination and eternity): i.e., contrary to the philosophers, and it preceded.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term ḥaduth (temporal origination) and clarifies its opposite, qidam (eternity). The author starts by quoting and explaining a line from the matn: 'its temporal origination is its existence after nonexistence'. He stresses that ḥaduth refers to existence preceded by nonexistence. This is in opposition to the philosophers (falāsifa) who claim the world is eternal (qadīm) but then speak of 'ḥaduth' for other-than-God in a different sense: meaning dependency on another, not actual origination after nonexistence. The author declares that holding the world to be eternal (in the sense of no beginning) is disbelief (kufr) by consensus. Next, he discusses the opposite (ḍidd) of ḥaduth. He clarifies that 'opposite' here means counterpart (muqābil), not logical opposite. The counterpart of ḥaduth is the privation of primacy of existence, which is called qidam, and it belongs exclusively to God. He then elaborates on the philosophers' claim: they say the world's eternity is derived from God's eternity by way of causality, calling it 'temporal eternity' (qidam zamānī), while God's eternity is essential (qidam dhātī). The author refutes this by noting that it is a false claim (may God deform them). Finally, he explains the relationship between ḥaduth and qidam: they are not true opposites (like hot and cold) but rather like a thing and what is equal to its contradictory. The contradictory of ḥaduth is 'not ḥaduth', and that is equivalent to qidam. Therefore, there is no middle ground between them, contrary to what the philosophers might imply.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' view that the world is eternal (qadīm) yet still called 'ḥādith' in a secondary sense is acceptable.
Correction: True ḥaduth means existence after non-existence; using the term for mere dependency distorts its meaning and constitutes disbelief.

Checkpoint

A) He is a misguided innovator
B) He is a disbeliever (kāfir) by consensus
C) He is excused due to interpretation
D) He is a Muslim but mistaken

Key Takeaway
Temporal origination (ḥaduth) is strictly existence after non-existence, and any other interpretation is a deviation leading to disbelief.

?
The passage declares that believing the philosophers' definition is disbelief. Have you ever convinced yourself that something created can exist without a beginning? Why does your heart resist acknowledging that everything besides Allah is truly ḥādith (temporally originated)?

Linguistic and Theological Distinction of Sifa and Wasf
Sifa and wasf distinct definitions linguistically and theologically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between 'sifa' and 'wasf' according to linguists and theologians, and the broad theological definition of 'sifa'.

Core Idea
'Sifa' and 'wasf' are synonyms in language but distinct in theology.

This passage clarifies two key terms in Islamic theology: 'sifa' and 'wasf'. While they are used interchangeably among linguists and grammarians, theologians give 'sifa' a precise technical meaning that encompasses much more than essential attributes. Understanding this distinction is crucial for following later discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic definition
Begin by reading how linguists and grammarians define 'sifa' and 'wasf' as synonymous. Note the morphological derivation from 'wasafa'.
The term 'na't' is used in grammar for epithet.
2
Examine the theological definition
Next, read the mutakallimun's definition: 'sifa' is anything predicated of something, whether identical to essence, subsisting in it, or external. Understand that this includes existence, attributes of meanings, and attributive attributes.
This definition is deliberately broad.
3
Consider the implications
Ponder why the author adds 'even according to the negation of states and privations'. This hints at a theological debate about the reality of attributes.
The commentary invites deep reflection.
4
Apply to divine attributes
Reflect on the statement that God's attributes are infinite. This connects the definition to the topic of divine perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And know that 'al-sifa' and 'al-wasf' have one meaning according to the linguists and grammarians, and that is 'al-na't' (the epithet); because it is a verbal noun of 'wasafa yasifu sifatan', so its origin is 'wasf' with a kasra on the waw, then the kasra was shifted to the sad, then the waw which is the fa of the word was deleted, and a ta marbuta was substituted for it. So this definition includes existence, attributes of meanings, and attributive attributes even according to the negation of states and privations, ponder. Commentary
This passage explains the definitions of 'sifa' and 'wasf'. Linguistically and grammatically, they are synonymous, both meaning 'epithet' or 'attribute', with a specific morphological derivation. Theologically, 'sifa' is defined as anything predicated of something, whether identical to its essence, subsisting in it, or external. This broad definition includes the attribute of existence. The commentator then notes the partitive 'min' in 'min wajibat' and states that God's attributes are infinite, specifically existential attributes, though the text is cut off.

What would change in your day if you treated each moment as a direct encounter with the One whose very Being is an attribute to be contemplated?

Definitions of Sifah and Wasf in Language and Kalam
Al-Sawi defines sifah and wasf linguistically and theologically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct definitions of sifah (attribute) as used by linguists and theologians, and how the theological definition is broader.

Core Idea
In theology, sifah includes anything predicated of a thing, even its very essence.

The terms sifah and wasf are often confused. In this passage, al-Sawi clarifies their linguistic and technical meanings. He first explains how they are used by grammarians, then presents the broader theological definition. This distinction is foundational for understanding discussions about God's attributes in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the key terms and their derivations.
Pay attention to the morphological derivation of sifah from wasf.
2
Compare the linguistic and theological definitions
Identify how linguists define sifah (as synonymous with na't) versus how theologians define it (as anything predicated).
Theological definition includes even the essence itself.
3
Reflect on the implications of the broad definition
Consider why theologians would adopt such a broad definition. How does it affect discussions of divine attributes?
It allows existence to be considered an attribute, and accommodates different theological views on states (ahwal) and privations (sulub).

ب) صفة (الوجود) و يصح أن يراد أيضاً بالوصف الصفة، والباء للتصوير والتفسير ؛ أي بأنَّ الصِّفة المفسّرة بالوجود (من واجبات : الواحد المعبود أي بعض الصفات الواجبة له تعالى، إذ الواجبات له تعالى كثيرة لا تنحصر فيما ذكر هنا ؛ لأنَّ صفاته تعالى الكماليَّة لا تتناهی وهو الإخبار عن قيام الصفة بالموصوف، فهو صفة للواصف؛ لأَنَّه وكلامه . فيه . خبره قوله : (بالوجود) : أي : الذاتي ؛ أي : إنَّه وجد لذاته، ولا مدخل لغيره قوله : ( ويصح أن يراد أيضاً بالوصف الصفة) : أي : فالمراد المعنى الأسمى. واعلم : أنَّ الصِّفة والوصف بمعنى واحد عند اللُّغويّين والنُّحاة وهو النَّعت ؛ لأنَّها مصدر وصف يصف صفة، فأصلها وصف بكسر الواو، ونقلت الكسرة إلى الصَّاد ، ثم حذفت الواو وهي فاء الكلمة، وعوّض عنها هاء التَّأنيث ، وأما عند المتكلمين ؛ فالصفة ما يحكم به على الشَّيء، سواء كان عين حقيقته ، أو قائماً بها أو خارجاً عنها، فدخل في هذا التعريف الوجود وصفات المعاني والمعنوية ولو على القول بنفي الأحوال والسُّلوب، تأمل.
(b) the attribute (of existence). It is also possible that what is meant by 'al-wasf' is 'al-sifah' (the attribute). The ba is for depiction and explanation: i.e., by the attribute interpreted as existence (from the necessary [attributes] of the One worshipped) i.e., some of the attributes necessary for Him, Exalted, for the necessary [attributes] for Him, Exalted, are many, not limited to what is mentioned here, because His perfect attributes, Exalted, are without end. And it is the informing of the subsistence of the attribute in the described, so it is an attribute of the describer; because... his speech... in it... his report. His statement: (by existence) i.e., essential; i.e., it exists in itself, no other has entry into it. His statement: (and it is also valid that what is meant by 'al-wasf' is 'al-sifah') i.e., the intended meaning is the more general sense. Know that 'al-sifah' and 'al-wasf' have one meaning according to linguists and grammarians, and it is 'al-na't' (the epithet); because it is the verbal noun of 'wasafa yasifu sifah', so its original is 'wasf' with a kasra on the waw, then the kasra was moved to the sad, then the waw, which is the first radical, was deleted, and the ta marbuta was substituted for it. As for according to the theologians (al-mutakallimun): 'al-sifah' is what is predicated of a thing, whether it be the very essence of the thing, or subsisting in it, or external to it. Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'sifah' and 'wasf' and clarifies their usage. The author first comments on the phrase 'bi-sifati al-wujud' (by the attribute of existence), noting that 'wasf' can also mean 'sifah' and that the letter ba is for depiction and explanation. He then explains that the attributes necessary for God are many and infinite. The text then digresses into a linguistic and technical definition of 'sifah'. According to linguists and grammarians, 'sifah' and 'wasf' are synonymous, both meaning 'na't' (epithet or description). The author provides the morphological derivation: the original form 'wasf' (with kasra on waw), then the kasra moves to the sad, the waw is deleted, and the ta marbuta is added. In contrast, according to the theologians (al-mutakallimun), 'sifah' is defined as anything that is predicated of a thing, whether it is identical with the essence, subsisting in it, or external to it. This broad definition includes existence, the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani, i.e., life, power, knowledge, etc.), and the attributes of meaning-relations (al-ma'nawiyya, such as being living, powerful, knowing), even if one denies the reality of modes (ahwal) and privations (sulub). The passage ends with 'tamal' (reflect), urging careful consideration of this technical definition.

Definition of the Essential Attribute
Definition of the essential attribute and its naming.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the essential attribute and why it is called 'essential'.

Core Idea
The essential attribute is inseparable from the essence itself.

The author begins by explaining the term 'essential attribute' (الصفة النفسية). This attribute is so called because it relates to the essence (الذات) directly. Understanding this foundational concept is crucial for later discussions on divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic definition of the essential attribute and its translation. Note that it is defined as that which the essence cannot be conceived without.
2
Identify the key elements
Notice that it is a positive attribute (ثبوتية) and that the description indicates only the essence itself, not an additional meaning.
3
Reflect on the name
Consider why it is called 'essential' (نفسية) and how it relates to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة النفسية معناها والخلاف فيها (وذي) أي : وهذه الصفة؛ أي صفة الوجود، (تسمى صفةً نفسيَّة) نسبة إلى النَّفْس؛ أي: الذات. والصَّفة النَّفسيَّة : هي التي لا تُعقل الذات بدونها، وهي صفة ثبوتية يدلُّ الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها. قوله: (وذي تسمّى صفة نفسيَّة: اعلم أنَّ الصِّفات من حيث هي منقسمة إلى أربعة أقسام لا زائد عليها نفسيَّة، وسلبية، ومعان، ومعنوية، ووجه ذلك : أنَّ الصِّفة إما أن يكون مدلولها عدماً أو لا الأوَّل: السَّلبيَّة، والثَّاني : إمَّا موجودة أو لا، الأوّل: المعاني، والثَّاني: إما أن يدلَّ الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها أو لا، الأوّل: النَّفسيَّة، والثَّاني : المعنوية. قوله : (وهي صفة ثبوتيَّة . . . إلخ) : هذا التّعريف للشَّيخ سعد الدين التفتازاني، وقوله: (صفة) كالجنس، يدخل فيه سائر الصفات، وقوله: (ثبوتيَّة) نسبة للثُبوت؛ لكونها ثابتة في الذهن، فخرج بذلك الصفات السَّلبيَّة. قوله : (يدلُّ الوصف بها أي بالمشتقّ منها لا بها نفسها؛ لعدم صحة ذلك، فنقول : الله موجود، ولا يصح أن نقول: الله وجود. هي على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها. ويقال أيضاً: الحال الواجبة للذات ما دامت الذات معلَّلة غير بعلة، وذلك كالوجود والتَّحيز للجرم، وكون الجوهر جوهراً، والشّيء شيئاً، فهذا تعريف للنّفسيَّة مطلقاً، قديمة كانت أو حادثة. قوله : ( على نفس الذَّات :أي: لا على معنى زائد عليها، وخرج به المعاني ؛ نحو القدرة والإرادة ؛ فإنَّ الوصف بها يدلُّ على معنى زائد على الذات. وقوله : (دون معنى زائد عليها خرج به المعنوية، وفي الحقيقة خرج بقوله: (على نفس الذات المعاني والمعنوية؛ لأَنَّ كلّاً منهما لا يدلُّ الوصف به على نفس الذَّات ، ولا دلالة لهما عليها، وإنما يدلان على معنى زائد عليها، إلَّا أنَّ هذا المعنى الزَّائد في المعاني وجودي، وفي المعنوية ثبوتي إذا علمت ذلك فقوله : (دون معنی زائد عليها مستدرك لا حاجة إليه إلَّا أن يقال : أتى به للإيضاح . قوله : (ويقال أيضاً) : هذا التّعريف هو المشهور بين المتأخرين ؛ كالسنوسي وغيره. قوله : (هي الحال الواجبة للذَّات) : أي : الثَّابتة لها خرج السَّلبيَّة والمعاني . قوله : (ما دامت الذَّات أي مدَّة دوامها فما مصدرية ظرفية، وهذا الدوام واجب بالنّسبة للقديم جائز بالنسبة للحادث.
The essential attribute, its meaning and the disagreement concerning it. (And this) meaning: and this attribute; i.e., the attribute of existence, (is called an essential attribute) in relation to the self (nafs); i.e., the essence (dhat). And the essential attribute is that the essence cannot be conceived without it, and it is a positive attribute (sifa thubutiyya) whose description indicates the essence itself without an additional meaning over it. His saying: (And this is called an essential attribute): Know that attributes, as such, are divided into four divisions with nothing beyond them: essential (nafsiyya), privative (salbiyya), meanings (ma'ani), and relational (ma'nawiyya). The explanation of that: The attribute either its significate (madlul) is non-existence or not; the first: privative; the second: either existent or not; the first: meanings; the second: either the description by it indicates the essence itself without an additional meaning over it or not; the first: essential; the second: relational. His saying: (And it is a positive attribute... etc.): This definition is by Shaykh Sa'd al-Din al-Taftazani. His saying: (attribute) is like a genus, encompassing all attributes; his saying: (positive) is a relation to positivity (thubut) because it is established in the mind; thus privative attributes are excluded. His saying: (the description by it) i.e., by its derived form, not by itself, because that is not valid; so we say: God is existent, and it is not valid to say: God is existence. It indicates the essence itself without an additional meaning over it. It is also said: It is the necessary state (hal wajiba) of the essence as long as the essence is conditioned without a cause, such as existence, spatiality for a body, being a substance for a substance, and being a thing for a thing. This is a definition of the essential attribute absolutely, whether eternal or originated. His saying: (upon the essence itself) i.e., not upon an additional meaning over it; thus meanings (ma'ani) are excluded, such as power and will, for the description by them indicates an additional meaning over the essence. His saying: (without an additional meaning over it) excludes relational (ma'nawiyya). In reality, his saying: (upon the essence itself) excludes both meanings and relational, because each of them does not indicate the essence itself, nor do they have any indication of it; rather they indicate an additional meaning over it, except that this additional meaning in meanings is existential (wujudi) and in relational is established (thubuti). If you know that, then his saying: (without an additional meaning over it) is redundant, no need for it, unless it is said: it was brought for clarification. His saying: (It is also said): This definition is the well-known one among later scholars, such as al-Sanusi and others. His saying: (It is the necessary state of the essence) i.e., the established for it; privative and meanings are excluded. His saying: (as long as the essence) i.e., as long as it endures, 'ma' is a circumstantial verbal noun. This enduring is necessary for the eternal and possible for the originated.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the essential attribute (الصفة النفسية). It begins by identifying the attribute of existence as the essential attribute, so named because it relates to the essence (الذات) itself. The definition states that the essential attribute is that without which the essence cannot be conceived; it is a positive attribute (ثبوتية) because it signifies something established in the mind, and its description indicates the essence alone without any extra meaning. The commentary then presents a classification of all attributes into four categories: essential (نفسية), privative (سلبية), meanings (معاني), and relational (معنوية). The logic of division is: if the attribute's significate is non-existence, it is privative; if existent, it falls either under meanings (if it denotes an existent additional meaning) or under the remaining two. Among existent attributes, if the description indicates only the essence, it is essential; if it indicates an additional meaning, it is relational. The passage then cites two specific definitions: one by al-Taftazani, who defines the essential attribute as a positive attribute whose description indicates the essence without an extra meaning, and another by later scholars like al-Sanusi, who define it as the necessary state of the essence as long as the essence is considered without a cause. Examples given are existence, spatiality for a body, being a substance, and being a thing. The commentary explains that the phrase 'without an additional meaning' excludes the meanings (e.g., power and will, which indicate extra meanings) and also excludes relational attributes. In fact, the phrase 'upon the essence itself' alone already excludes both meanings and relational attributes, so the additional phrase is for clarification. Finally, the passage notes that the enduring of the essence is necessary for the eternal (God) but possible for originated beings.

صفة ثبوتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that an essential attribute adds an extra quality to the essence.
Correction: The definition explicitly states that the description indicates only the essence itself, without any additional meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the description of an essential attribute indicate?
A) An additional meaning over the essence
B) The essence itself without an additional meaning
C) The non-existence of the essence
D) A privative state

Key Takeaway
The essential attribute is a positive attribute that is inseparable from the essence and points to nothing but the essence itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah exists,' do you truly believe that His existence is inseparable from His essence, or do you secretly imagine it as a quality He could lose? Let this understanding root your certainty in His eternal reality.
?
If the essential attribute like existence points directly to Allah's essence without adding anything extra, how does that humble your attempt to fully describe Him? Will you let this silence be a form of worship?
?
This lesson teaches that the essence cannot be conceived without its essential attribute. How does this reshape your awareness of Allah's constant presence? Commit to feeling His existence in a new way today.

Logic and Technical Terms

Classification of Attributes into Four Types
Classification of attributes into four types with logical division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how attributes are classified into four categories: essential, privative, meanings, and relational.

Core Idea
Attributes are divided by whether they signify existence or non-existence and by what they indicate.

The author provides a logical division of all attributes into four exhaustive types. This classification is fundamental for organizing the study of divine attributes in kalam. Understanding this division will help you place any attribute into its proper category.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic text that divides attributes into four types. Follow the logical steps.
2
Trace the division logic
Start from the first split: does the attribute signify non-existence? If yes, it is privative. If no, then it signifies existence. Then ask: is it existent or not? If existent, it is meanings. Then among those that signify existence, does the description indicate only the essence? If yes, essential; if no, relational.
This is a decision tree.
3
Identify examples
Think of examples for each type: privative (e.g., not being composed), meanings (e.g., power), essential (e.g., existence), relational (e.g., being eternal).
The passage does not give examples here, but you can infer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصفة النفسية معناها والخلاف فيها (وذي) أي : وهذه الصفة؛ أي صفة الوجود، (تسمى صفةً نفسيَّة) نسبة إلى النَّفْس؛ أي: الذات. والصَّفة النَّفسيَّة : هي التي لا تُعقل الذات بدونها، وهي صفة ثبوتية يدلُّ الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها. قوله: (وذي تسمّى صفة نفسيَّة: اعلم أنَّ الصِّفات من حيث هي منقسمة إلى أربعة أقسام لا زائد عليها نفسيَّة، وسلبية، ومعان، ومعنوية، ووجه ذلك : أنَّ الصِّفة إما أن يكون مدلولها عدماً أو لا الأوَّل: السَّلبيَّة، والثَّاني : إمَّا موجودة أو لا، الأوّل: المعاني، والثَّاني: إما أن يدلَّ الوصف بها على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها أو لا، الأوّل: النَّفسيَّة، والثَّاني : المعنوية. قوله : (وهي صفة ثبوتيَّة . . . إلخ) : هذا التّعريف للشَّيخ سعد الدين التفتازاني، وقوله: (صفة) كالجنس، يدخل فيه سائر الصفات، وقوله: (ثبوتيَّة) نسبة للثُبوت؛ لكونها ثابتة في الذهن، فخرج بذلك الصفات السَّلبيَّة. قوله : (يدلُّ الوصف بها أي بالمشتقّ منها لا بها نفسها؛ لعدم صحة ذلك، فنقول : الله موجود، ولا يصح أن نقول: الله وجود. هي على نفس الذات دون معنى زائد عليها. ويقال أيضاً: الحال الواجبة للذات ما دامت الذات معلَّلة غير بعلة، وذلك كالوجود والتَّحيز للجرم، وكون الجوهر جوهراً، والشّيء شيئاً، فهذا تعريف للنّفسيَّة مطلقاً، قديمة كانت أو حادثة. قوله : ( على نفس الذَّات :أي: لا على معنى زائد عليها، وخرج به المعاني ؛ نحو القدرة والإرادة ؛ فإنَّ الوصف بها يدلُّ على معنى زائد على الذات. وقوله : (دون معنى زائد عليها خرج به المعنوية، وفي الحقيقة خرج بقوله: (على نفس الذات المعاني والمعنوية؛ لأَنَّ كلّاً منهما لا يدلُّ الوصف به على نفس الذَّات ، ولا دلالة لهما عليها، وإنما يدلان على معنى زائد عليها، إلَّا أنَّ هذا المعنى الزَّائد في المعاني وجودي، وفي المعنوية ثبوتي إذا علمت ذلك فقوله : (دون معنی زائد عليها مستدرك لا حاجة إليه إلَّا أن يقال : أتى به للإيضاح . قوله : (ويقال أيضاً) : هذا التّعريف هو المشهور بين المتأخرين ؛ كالسنوسي وغيره. قوله : (هي الحال الواجبة للذَّات) : أي : الثَّابتة لها خرج السَّلبيَّة والمعاني . قوله : (ما دامت الذَّات أي مدَّة دوامها فما مصدرية ظرفية، وهذا الدوام واجب بالنّسبة للقديم جائز بالنسبة للحادث.

Two Definitions of the Essential Attribute
Two definitions of the essential attribute and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of the essential attribute and the significance of the phrase 'without an additional meaning'.

Core Idea
Two definitions clarify the essential attribute, with the second being more precise.

The author presents two definitions of the essential attribute: one by al-Taftazani and a later one by al-Sanusi and others. He then explains each part and discusses a redundancy in the phrasing. This lesson will help you grasp the nuances of the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the two definitions
Read the Arabic text that gives Taftazani's definition and the alternative definition ('It is the necessary state...').
2
Analyze Taftazani's definition
Examine each phrase: 'attribute' as genus, 'positive' excludes privative, 'indicates the essence itself' excludes meanings and relational.
3
Understand the redundancy
The author notes that the phrase 'without an additional meaning' is redundant because 'indicates the essence itself' already excludes extra meaning. But it is included for clarification.
4
Contrast the second definition
The second definition defines it as 'the necessary state of the essence as long as the essence is considered without a cause'. Connect this to examples like existence for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Existent', do you truly feel that His existence is not separate from His essence, or do you sometimes imagine existence as an extra quality added to Him? How can this awareness deepen your tawakkul?
?
The essential attribute applies to all things: a stone is a stone, a human is human. Do you ever pause to see the sheer 'thingness' of creation as a sign of Allah's oneness and self-sufficiency? What would change if you did?
?
In your daily life, when you describe yourself or others, do you focus on what is essential (like being a human) or on accidental qualities? How might shifting to essential descriptions bring you closer to understanding Allah's attributes?

The Three Modes of Existence for Conceptual Considerations
Commentator defines three modes for conceptual considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that conceptual considerations (iʿtibārāt) have only three modes of existence: in minds, in words, and in souls.

Core Idea
Conceptual thoughts exist in minds, words, and souls—not in external reality.

This passage introduces the concept of modes of existence (thubūtāt) for things that do not have external reality. The commentator explains that such conceptual considerations have exactly three modes of existence, distinguishing them from real entities that also exist externally.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the three modes
Find each of the three modes mentioned: establishment in minds, in words, and in souls. Notice how the commentator defines them.
3
Understand why conceptual matters lack external existence
Ask yourself why conceptual considerations do not have external establishment. The passage implies they are mental constructs, not physical realities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن لم يكن لها ثبوت خارجاً أي فيكون لها ثلاث ثبوتات فقط : ثبوت في الأذهان، وثبوت في الألفاظ، وثبوت في النفوس، بخلاف المعاني وكل موجود؛ فله أربع ثبوتات بزيادة الثبوت في الأعيان، وأما السَّليَّة : فلها ثبوتان ؛ ثبوت في الألفاظ، وثبوت في النفوس. قوله: (أي: يلاحظ الماهيَّة بدون الوجود أي كملاحظة ماهية القول في الذهن مع عدم وجوده في الخارج. قوله : (ثم تليها في الذِّكر): أي لا في الواقع ونفس الأمر، إذ لا ترتيب بين صفات الله تعالى في نفس الأمر، إذ الترتيب يقتضي حدوث
His saying: (And even if they have no external establishment) meaning: so they have only three establishments: establishment in minds, establishment in words, and establishment in souls. Unlike the meanings (al-maʿānī) and every existing thing; they have four establishments with the addition of establishment in external entities. As for the privative (al-salbiyya), it has two establishments: establishment in words and establishment in souls. His saying: (i.e., the intellect observes the quiddity without existence) meaning: like observing the quiddity of the statement in the mind while it does not exist externally. His saying: (Then it follows in mention) meaning: not in reality and actual fact, for there is no order among God's attributes in actual fact, because order entails temporality.

Commentary
This passage explains the different modes of existence (thubūtāt) for various types of attributes. The commentator starts by explaining the phrase 'وإن لم يكن لها ثبوت خارجاً' (even if they have no external establishment) referring to the considerations (iʿtibārāt). He says that such things have only three establishments: establishment in minds (al-adhhān), establishment in words (al-alfāẓ), and establishment in souls (al-nufūs). Observing Quiddity and Order Among Divine Attributes
Commentator explains mental observation and order among divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of observing quiddity without existence and why there is no order among God's attributes in reality.

Core Idea
The mind can conceive a thing without its external existence; God's attributes have no real order.

This lesson covers two clarifications from the commentator. First, he explains that the intellect can observe the quiddity (māhiyya) of something without its existence, like a mental concept with no external reality. Second, he clarifies that when attributes are mentioned in sequence, the order is only linguistic, not real, because order would imply temporality in the divine essence.

3

The Three Names of the Proof of Mutual Hindrance
Al-Sawi defines the three names of the proof and their conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three names of the proof of mutual hindrance and the logical conditions under which each name is used.

Core Idea
One proof, three names depending on whether the gods agree or disagree.

In this short but important passage, Al-Sawi clarifies the terminology of a central kalām argument. The proof of mutual hindrance is known by different names depending on the logical scenario being examined. Understanding these names helps you see the unity behind the argument and avoids confusion when you encounter them in later texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and the literal English translation. Note the three distinct names mentioned.
The passage is only one sentence, but it packs a lot of meaning.
2
Identify the three names and their conditions
Look for the three names: برهان التمانع, برهان التطارد, برهان التوارد. Notice that the first is the general name, the second is for when the gods disagree, and the third is for when they agree.
The word 'فرض' means 'assumption' – these are hypothetical scenarios.
3
Understand the logical significance
Why does the proof have different names? Because the proof works by showing that either disagreement or agreement leads to contradiction. The names reflect the specific logical flaw being exposed.
4
Connect to the broader proof
Remember that all three names refer to the same underlying argument. This prevents the common mistake of thinking they are separate proofs.
The proof is a cornerstone of Islamic theology for establishing the oneness of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (برهان التَّمانع) : أي : ويقال له: برهان التطارد، وهذا في فرض اختلافهما، ويقال له : برهان التوارد في فرض اتفاقهما .
His statement: (the proof of mutual hindrance): that is, and it is called: the proof of mutual repulsion, and this is on the assumption of their disagreement, and it is called: the proof of mutual concurrence on the assumption of their agreement.
Source pages p. 207

Commentary
This passage is a brief definition of a famous kalam proof. The author notes that the proof has three names depending on the scenario. When two supposed gods are assumed to disagree, it is called 'the proof of mutual repulsion' (burhān al-taṭārud). When they are assumed to agree, it is called 'the proof of mutual concurrence' (burhān al-tawārud). The most common name is 'the proof of mutual hindrance' (burhān al-tamānuʿ), which covers both possibilities. The author is clarifying that these names are not arbitrary but reflect the logical situation being considered.

Key Terms
برهان التمانع
برهان التطارد
برهان التوارد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some students think that برهان التمانع, برهان التطارد, and برهان التوارد are three different proofs.
Correction: They are three names for the same proof, used under different logical assumptions. The proof remains one unified argument.

Checkpoint
What is the name of the proof used when the two supposed gods are assumed to disagree?
A) برهان التمانع
B) برهان التطارد
C) برهان التوارد
D) برهان التضاد

Key Takeaway
The proof of mutual hindrance is referred to by different names based on whether the gods are assumed to disagree or agree, but it remains a single logical argument for the oneness of God.

Kasb and Iktisab: Human Acquisition vs. Divine Creation
Defines kasb and iktisab as human acquisition distinct from divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between divine creation (ijad/ikhtira') and human acquisition (kasb/iktisab), and why human actions are attributed to us only as acquisition.

Core Idea
Humans acquire actions; God alone creates them.

One of the most subtle topics in Islamic theology is the relationship between divine power and human action. The Ash'ari school solves this by distinguishing between creation, which belongs only to God, and acquisition, which is the human role. In this passage, the author establishes this distinction clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text once slowly. Then read the English translation. Notice the contrast between 'إيجاد واختراع' and 'كسب واكتساب'.
The passage is concise but dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where the passage defines divine creation (attached to eternal power) and where it defines human acquisition (attached to temporal power). Note the key terms: القدرة القديمة and القدرة الحادثة.
These two categories are the heart of the distinction.
3
Understand the key distinction
The passage says human actions are attributed to us only as kasb, not as creation. Why? Because creation is exclusively God's act of bringing something from non-existence. Human power only 'accompanies' the action through choice and intention.
This preserves both God's sole creative power and human moral responsibility.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: Why does this matter? The answer is that it solves the problem of predestination versus free will without compromising divine omnipotence.
This distinction is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : النسبة إلينا، ومخاطبتنا بتحصيله من حيث إنَّه كسب أو اكتساب، لا من حيث إنه إيجاد واختراع . وتوضيح ذلك : أنَّ قدرته تعالى أبرزت الأشياء على طبق إرادته، من العدم إلى الوجود، وهذا الإبراز هو المسمَّى بالإيجاد والاختراع، المراد بتعلُّق القدرة القديمة، وأما قدرتنا فقد تعلقت ببعض الأفعال الاختيارية؛ أي التي لنا فيها الاختيار والميل والقصد من غير إيجاد واختراع، وهذا التعلق على طبق إرادتنا هو المسمَّى بالكسب والاكتساب .
We said: the attribution is to us, and our being addressed to acquire it insofar as it is kasb or iktisab, not insofar as it is creation and invention. And clarification of that: that His power brought forth things according to His will from non-existence to existence, and this bringing forth is what is called creation and invention, intended by the attachment of the eternal power. As for our power, it has attached to some voluntary actions — i.e., those in which we have choice, inclination, and intention without creation and invention. And this attachment in accordance with our will is what is called kasb and iktisab.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage defines the key Ash'ari terms kasb and iktisab, which denote human acquisition of actions as opposed to divine creation. It begins by stating the central claim: actions are attributed to humans only as acquisition, not as creation. The clarification explains why: God's power creates existence from nothing, which is called ijad/ikhtira'. Human power attaches to voluntary actions but only as acquisition — we have choice and intention, but the actual bringing into existence is God's act. Thus kasb/iktisab signifies the human contribution of intention and effort without any creative power.

Key Terms
الكسب
الاكتساب
الإيجاد
الاختراع
القدرة القديمة
القدرة الحادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Humans create their own actions independently.
Correction: Humans only acquire actions; God creates them. The passage states human power attaches 'without creation and invention'.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relationship between human power and voluntary actions?
A) Human power creates the action.
B) Human power acquires the action without creating it.
C) Human power has no connection to action.
D) Human power shares creation with God.

Key Takeaway
God alone creates; humans only acquire their actions through choice and intention without any creative power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a good deed, do you thank Allah sincerely, or do you feel a sense of your own independent ability? How does the truth that only God creates change your gratitude?
?
Reflect on a time you fell into sin. Did you blame yourself fully, or did you feel that God ‘created’ the sin and you were powerless? With the understanding of kasb, how should you account for your choice and intention while knowing Allah’s power?
?
If God alone creates, and you merely acquire through your choice, what does that mean for your daily reliance on Him? Will you start each action by consciously acknowledging that your power is a gift, not an independent force?

Milla, Din, and Sharia: Identical in Essence
Al-Sawi defines milla, dīn, and sharīʿa and states the ruling on disbelief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between milla, dīn, and sharīʿa and how they relate to the ruling on disbelief in Islam.

Core Idea
Milla, din, and sharia are three perspectives on one reality: Islamic law.

In this passage, al-Sawi clarifies three key terms that appear frequently in Islamic theological discourse: milla, dīn, and sharīʿa. Although often used interchangeably, each highlights a different aspect of divine legislation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping why certain statements are considered disbelief (kufr) according to the consensus of the Muslim community.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Note the opening statement about disbelief and the definition of the three terms.
2
Identify the ruling on disbelief
The passage begins by stating that attributing a statement to disbelief makes the speaker a disbeliever. Understand the basis: affirming a partner or incapacity to Allah.
This ruling is according to the people of the religion (Islam).
3
Understand the three terms and their perspectives
Milla, dīn, and sharīʿa are said to be identical in essence but differ in aspect. Examine each perspective: milla (dictated for transmission), dīn (adhered to in worship), sharīʿa (legislated by the Lawgiver).
They all refer to the same legal rulings.
4
Reflect on the phrase 'apparent attribution'
The passage ends by clarifying that the attribution (of kufr to a statement) is apparent and needs no interpretation. Consider why this clarity is important in theological judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحمل ظاهر على معنى : فقَوْلُه (كُفْرُ) ، فيكون القائل به كافراً؛ لأنَّه أثبت الشَّريك والعجز الله تعالى عن ذلك (عند) (أهل الملة) جميع أي : ملة الإسلام. والملة والدين والشَّريعة : عبارة عن الأحكام الشرعية، فهي متّحدة بالذات لكنَّها مختلفة بالاعتبار ؛ لأنَّ الأحكام الشَّرعيَّة من حيث إِنَّهَا تُملى لِتُنقَلَ مِلَّة، ومن حيث إنّها يُتَدَيَّن بها – أي : يُتعبد بها - دِينُ، – – ومن حيث إنها شرعت - أي : بينها الشارع - شريعة؛ أي : مشروعة . قوله فالحمل ظاهر ) أي : الإخبار عنه ظاهر واضح لا يحتاج لتأويل .
So the attribution is apparent in meaning: his statement 'disbelief', so the one who says it is a disbeliever, because he affirmed partnership and incapacity to Allah Almighty – about that – according to the people of the religion, all of them, i.e. the religion of Islam. And milla, dīn, and sharīʿa are expressions for the legal rulings; they are identical in essence but different in aspect, because the legal rulings – in that they are dictated to be transmitted – are a milla, and in that they are adhered to religiously – i.e., worshipped with – they are a dīn, and in that they are legislated – i.e., the Lawgiver clarified them – they are a sharīʿa; i.e., legislated. His statement: 'so the attribution is apparent' – i.e., the reporting about it is apparent and clear, not needing interpretation.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and clarification of key terms and a ruling on disbelief. The author begins by explaining that when a statement is attributed to disbelief (kufr), the speaker is a disbeliever because he has affirmed a partner or incapacity to Allah. The phrase 'according to the people of the religion' refers to the Islamic community. Then he defines three terms: milla, dīn, and sharīʿa. They all refer to the same entity – the legal rulings – but are distinguished by perspective: milla from the angle of being dictated for transmission, dīn from the angle of being practiced religiously (i.e., worship), and sharīʿa from the angle of being legislated by the Lawgiver. Finally, a gloss on the initial phrase 'so the attribution is apparent' clarifies that the reporting (or predicate) is clear and needs no interpretation.

Misconception: One might think milla, dīn, and sharīʿa are entirely separate concepts.
Correction: The passage shows they are identical in essence and differ only in aspect.

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between milla, dīn, and sharīʿa according to this passage?
A) They are completely different concepts.
B) They are identical in essence but differ in aspect.
C) They are unrelated to legal rulings.
D) They are synonymous without any distinction.

Key Takeaway
The three terms milla, dīn, and sharīʿa refer to the same divine law but emphasize transmission, worship, and legislation respectively.

?

?

?

Enumeration of the Six Contingent Aspects Specified by Will
Al-Sawi enumerates the six categories that divine will specifies, citing a poem and noting an omission in the sharh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six categories of contingent things that divine will specifies, and you will learn about a discrepancy in the commentary regarding the inclusion of 'attribute'.

Core Idea
Divine will specifies six categories: existence, non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and measures – but the sharh omitted 'attributes'.

In this passage, al-Sawi clarifies the precise list of six categories that are subject to divine will. He presents a poetic enumeration and points out that the original commentary (sharh) omitted one of these categories – 'attributes' (الصفة). Understanding this list helps grasp the scope of God's creative act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the poetic line.
2
Identify the six categories
The passage mentions that there are six categories of contingent things. Find them in the poem and note that the poem lists seven items – we will discuss this discrepancy.
3
Understand the commentary's omission
Notice that al-Sawi says the commentator omitted the sixth category, which is 'attribute'. Reflect on why this might have happened.
4
Check the footnote
The footnote traces the poetic line to a specific author. Consider the reliability of the attribution.
This shows how kalam texts preserve scholarly chains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله تخصص :(الممكن خرج به ما عداها من الصفات.
قوله : (من وجود أو عدم بيان لبعض ما يجوز عليه قصد به تعداد الممكنات المتقابلات، وهي ستّة جمعها بعضهم بقوله (۱): من الرجز] الممكنات المتقابلات وجودنـا والـعـدم الـصـفـات أزمنة أمــكــنــة جــهــات كذا المقادير روى
وقد أسقط الشارح سادسها وهو الصفة.
(۱) عزاها العلامة العارف بالله السيد محمد الهاشمي في مفتاح الجنة (ص (۱۰۸) إلى الإمام أبي عبد الله محمد بن قاسم القيسي المشهور بالقصار الفاسي المتوفى سنة (١٠١٢هـ).
His saying: 'specifies' – (the possible) is exclusive of what is other than these attributes. His saying: 'from existence or non-existence' is an explanation of some of what is admissible for it, intended to enumerate the corresponding possible things. They are six, compiled by some in his saying (in rajaz meter): 'The corresponding possibles: our existence, non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, likewise measures, he narrated.' And the commentator omitted the sixth, which is the attribute. (1) The learned gnostic Sayyid Muhammad al-Hashimi attributed it in Miftah al-Jannah (p. 108) to Imam Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Qasim al-Qaysi known as al-Qassar al-Fasi, died 1012 AH.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a textual note on the enumeration of the six aspects of contingent things that the divine will specifies. The commentary first explains that the phrase 'specifies the possible' excludes other attributes. Then it clarifies that the phrase 'from existence or non-existence' is an illustration of some of the admissible possibilities, intended to enumerate the corresponding possible things. These are six categories, and a poet has compiled them in rajaz meter: existence, non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and also measures – though the line 'كذا المقادير' may be a variant. The passage then notes that the commentator (the author of the Sharh, i.e., al-Kharidat's commentary) omitted the sixth category, which is 'attribute' (الصفة). A footnote attributes this poetic line to Muhammad ibn Qasim al-Qaysi al-Fasi.

Emphasis in 'Hatmman' and 'Dawaman'
Explanation of emphatic terms in kalam phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the terms 'hatman' and 'dawaman' function to emphasize the necessary and perpetual nature of divine attributes in kalam discourse.

Core Idea
In kalam, 'hatman' stresses obligation; 'dawaman' adds perpetual emphasis.

The commentator takes a moment to unpack a concise phrase from the matn, explaining the specific role of each emphatic term. This clarification prevents misunderstanding about the redundancy of 'hatman' and 'dawaman' when describing wajib attributes. Let's see how each term contributes to the overall meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation once. Notice how brief it is. The commentator is clarifying two key terms.
2
Identify what 'hatman' emphasizes
The passage says 'hatman' emphasizes 'the meaning of obligation' (wujub). Understand that 'hatman' makes the obligation explicit and emphatic.
3
Understand 'dawaman' as extra emphasis
The word 'dawaman' adds another layer, emphasizing perpetuity. It is not redundant; it reinforces that the obligation is ongoing.
4
Connect to the broader context
Reflect on why such emphasis matters: in discussing Allah's attributes, precision and emphasis prevent misunderstanding about the eternal nature of His qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهذا من زيادة التأكيد) أي: قوله: (دواماً)، فحتماً توكيد لمعنى الوجوب، و(دواماً) زيادة تأكيد.
His saying: 'And this is from the increase of emphasis' means his saying: 'dawaman' (perpetually). So 'hatman' (necessarily) is an emphasis for the meaning of obligation, and 'dawaman' is an extra emphasis.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This short passage is a direct explanation of the previous phrase. The commentator clarifies that when the author said 'this is from the increase of emphasis', he is referring to the word 'dawaman' (perpetually). The word 'hatman' (necessarily) serves as an emphasis for the meaning of wajib (obligatory), and 'dawaman' adds another layer of emphasis, reinforcing the perpetual nature of the relation.

Key Terms

حتماً
دواماً

Checkpoint

A) The meaning of perpetuity
B) The meaning of obligation (wujub)
C) The attribute itself
D) The increase of emphasis

Key Takeaway
In kalam, 'hatman' emphasizes the obligatory nature, while 'dawaman' adds emphasis on perpetuity, together reinforcing the eternal and necessary quality of divine attributes.

Defining 'Opposite' as Linguistic Incompatibility
Al-Sawi defines 'ḍidd' as linguistic opposite, meaning absolute incompatibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'ḍidd' in this context means absolute incompatibility, including both existential and non-existential opposites, and why it is used in discussing divine attributes.

Core Idea
In kalam, 'opposite' means any incompatibility, not just technical opposites like black and white.

Al-Sawi begins by clarifying the sense in which 'opposite' (ḍidd) is used when discussing what is impossible for Allah. He distinguishes between the linguistic opposite, which is any incompatibility, and the technical opposite that will be discussed later. This distinction is crucial because the attributes of Allah are beyond simple binary opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of linguistic opposite
Read the Arabic sentence defining 'ḍidd' as absolute incompatibility. Notice the phrase 'mā yunāfī' (whatever is incompatible).
The term 'linguistic opposite' here is broad.
2
Contrast with technical opposite
Al-Sawi contrasts 'ḍidd luġhawī' with 'ḍidd iṣṭilāḥī'. The technical opposite is a narrower category. Understand why the broader sense is needed for divine attributes.
The example of black and white is a technical opposite.
3
Apply to divine transcendence
Reflect on the phrase 'wa yastaḥīlu ʿalayhi taʿālā kullu mā yunāfī mā taqaddama min aṣ-ṣifāt'. This means anything incompatible with Allah's necessary attributes is impossible for Him.
This includes both existential and non-existential incompatibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بالضّدّ هنا الضّدُّ اللُّغوي، وهو: مطلق المنافي، سواء كان وجوديّاً أو عدميّاً، فكأنَّه قال : ويستحيل عليه تعالى كل ما ينافي ما تقدَّم من الصفات لا الضد الاصطلاحي على ما سيأتي .
أنواع المنافاة عند المناطقة:
وأنواع المنافاة عند المناطقة أربعة : تنافي النقيضين، وتنافي الضّدَّين، وتنافي العَدَم والمَلكة ، وتنافي المتضايفين .
قوله : (كلُّ ما ينافي ... إلخ ) : أي : سواء كان ضدّاً حقيقة، أو نقيضاً
أو مساوياً للنقيض أو أخص منه .
قوله : (وأنواع المنافاة عند المناطقة ) : أي : وأما عند الأصوليين ؛ فهما اثنان فقط تنافي النَّقيضين وتنافي الضّدَّين، ويجعلون العدم والملكة داخلين
في النقيضين، والمتضايفين داخلين في الضّدّين.
قوله : (أربعة) وجه الحصر فيها أنَّ المتقابلين إما أن يكونا وجوديين أو وجوديّاً وعدميّاً، فإن كانا وجوديين؛ فلا يخلو إما أن يتوقف تعقُل أحدهما على تعقُل الآخر أو لا .
الأوَّل: المتضايفان كالأبوّة ،والبنوّة والثَّاني : المتضادان، كالبياض والسَّواد، وإن كان أحدهما وجوديّاً والآخر عدميّاً، فإن اعتبر في العدمي محله قابلاً للوجودي؛ كالبصر والعمى بالنسبة لزيد، لا بالنسبة للحائط ؛ فعدم وملكة، وإن لم يعتبر ذلك فتقابل النقيضين؛ كسواد ولا كون سواد.
And what is meant by “opposite” here is the linguistic opposite, which is absolute incompatibility, whether existential or non-existential. So it is as if he said: And it is impossible for Him, exalted, everything that is incompatible with what preceded from the attributes, not the technical opposite, as will come. The types of opposition according to the logicians: And the types of opposition according to the logicians are four: opposition of contradictories, opposition of opposites, opposition of privation and possession, and opposition of relatives. His statement: “(everything that is incompatible … etc.)” i.e. whether it is truly an opposite, or a contradictory, or equal to a contradictory, or more specific than it. His statement: “(and the types of opposition according to the logicians)” i.e. As for the jurists, there are only two: opposition of contradictories and opposition of opposites; they make privation and possession included within contradictories, and relatives included within opposites. His statement: “(four)”—the reason for the enumeration is that the two opposed things are either both existential or existential and non-existential. If they are both existential, then it is either the case that the conception of one depends on the conception of the other or not. The first: the two relatives, like fatherhood and sonship. The second: the two opposites, like whiteness and blackness. And if one is existential and the other non-existential, then if a locus capable of the existential is considered for the non-existential—like sight and blindness in relation to Zayd, not in relation to a wall—then it is privation and possession; and if that is not considered, then it is contradiction of contradictories, like blackness and non-blackness.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage provides a definition and classification. It first defines what is meant by 'ḍidd' (opposite) in the context of the impossible attributes of Allah. It is the linguistic opposite, meaning absolute incompatibility, whether the incompatible thing is existential (like white) or non-existential (like blindness). So the intended meaning is that anything incompatible with the mandatory attributes is impossible for Allah, not just the technical opposite (like the relation between white and black). The passage then enumerates the four types of opposition (tanāfuʿ) according to the logicians: (1) contradiction of contradictories (like existence and non-existence), (2) opposites proper (like white and black), (3) privation and possession (like sight and blindness in a subject capable of sight), and (4) relatives (like fatherhood and sonship, where each requires the other for conception). The commentary adds two notes: first, that the phrase 'everything that is incompatible' includes any type of incompatibility, whether it is truly an opposite, a contradictory, or something equal or more specific. Second, it contrasts the logicians' fourfold division with the jurists' (uṣūliyyūn) twofold division, where only contradictories and opposites are recognized, with privation and possession subsumed under contradictories and relatives under opposites. Finally, the passage explains the logical basis for the fourfold enumeration: considering whether the two opposed items are existential or not, and if existential, whether they are interdependent in conception (relatives) or not (opposites); if one is existential and one non-existential, it is privation if the non-existential is a lack in a suitable subject, otherwise it is contradiction. Examples clarify each case.

Key Terms
الضد اللغوي
الضد الاصطلاحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'linguistic opposite' (al-ḍidd al-luġhawī) mean in this passage?
A) The opposite that is only existential
B) Absolute incompatibility, whether existential or non-existential
C) The technical opposite like black and white
D) A synonym for contradictory

Key Takeaway
In this context, 'opposite' means any incompatibility, not just technical opposites, so that all attributes incompatible with Allah's perfection are ruled out.

?
The linguistic opposite includes non-existential things like blindness. Are there spiritual 'blindnesses' in your life—things you fail to see or acknowledge—that contradict the attribute of Allah as All-Seeing? How will you seek to remove that blindness?

The Four Types of Opposition in Logic
Al-Sawi enumerates four types of opposition according to logicians and explains their rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four types of opposition (tanāfuʿ) according to logicians and understand their logical basis, as well as the jurists' alternative classification.

Core Idea
Logicians classify opposition into four types; jurists simplify to two.

After defining 'opposite' broadly, Al-Sawi presents the technical classification of opposition as used by logicians. He enumerates four types: contradictories, opposites, privation and possession, and relatives. He then explains the logical reasoning behind this fourfold division and notes that jurists compress them into just two categories. This classification is foundational for understanding how different kinds of incompatibility are distinguished in kalam and logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of the four types
Read the passage listing the four types: tanāfuʿ an-naqīḍayn, aḍ-ḍiddayn, al-ʿadam wa al-milkah, and al-mutaḍāyifayn.
Memorize these names.
2
Understand the logical basis
Read the explanation of why there are exactly four: consider whether the two opposed items are existential or not, and whether they are interdependent in conception.
This reasoning shows the completeness of the classification.
3
Contrast with the jurists' division
Al-Sawi notes that jurists only recognize two types (contradictories and opposites), subsuming the other two under them. Think about why jurists might prefer a simpler scheme.
This highlights the different concerns of logic vs. jurisprudence.
4
Reflect on examples
Study the examples given (whiteness/blackness, fatherhood/sonship, sight/blindness, blackness/non-blackness). See how each fits its category.
The examples clarify the abstract definitions.

So the intended meaning is that anything incompatible with the mandatory attributes is impossible for Allah, not just the technical opposite (like the relation between white and black). The passage then enumerates the four types of opposition (tanāfuʿ) according to the logicians: (1) contradiction of contradictories (like existence and non-existence), (2) opposites proper (like white and black), (3) privation and possession (like sight and blindness in a subject capable of sight), and (4) relatives (like fatherhood and sonship, where each requires the other for conception). The commentary adds two notes: first, that the phrase 'everything that is incompatible' includes any type of incompatibility, whether it is truly an opposite, a contradictory, or something equal or more specific. Second, it contrasts the logicians' fourfold division with the jurists' (uṣūliyyūn) twofold division, where only contradictories and opposites are recognized, with privation and possession subsumed under contradictories and relatives under opposites. Finally, the passage explains the logical basis for the fourfold enumeration: considering whether the two opposed items are existential or not, and if existential, whether they are interdependent in conception (relatives) or not (opposites); if one is existential and one non-existential, it is privation if the non-existential is a lack in a suitable subject, otherwise it is contradiction. Examples clarify each case.

Key Terms
تنافي النقيضين
تنافي الضدين
تنافي العدم والملكة
تنافي المتضايفين
الأصوليين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the logicians' four types and the jurists' two are completely different, but they are just different levels of generality.
Correction: The jurists' two types subsume the logicians' four: privation/possession is a kind of contradiction, and relatives are a kind of opposite.

Checkpoint
According to the logicians, how many types of opposition are there?
A) Two
B) Four
C) Three
D) Six

Key Takeaway
Logicians classify opposition into four based on existential status and conceptual interdependence; jurists simplify to two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a seeming contradiction in religious teachings, do you tend to oversimplify it into a 'right vs wrong' binary? How might distinguishing between actual contradictories, opposites, and relatives help you approach such issues with more depth and humility before Allah?
?
The jurists chose pragmatism over precision by reducing four types to two. In your own spiritual life, where do you prioritize practicality over nuance? Is there an area where you need to be more like a logician—seeking careful distinction—to avoid error in your beliefs?
?
This lesson shows that even among scholars, classifications vary based on purpose. How does this affect your trust in scholarly differences? Will you now be more patient with those who categorize differently, knowing they may be operating under a different framework, not necessarily wrong?
