The Four Types of Opposition in Logic
Al-Sawi enumerates and defines the four types of opposition in logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of opposition in logic as defined by logicians, including their distinction from the jurists' classification and the rationale behind the enumeration.

Core Idea
Opposition in logic is a fourfold classification based on existence and conceptual dependence.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of opposition used in logic. The logicians distinguish four types: contradictories, contraries, privation/possession, and correlatives. The passage also compares this with the simpler twofold division used by jurists, and addresses a subtle objection to the classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types
Read the first sentence to list the four types of opposition. Note how each is named in Arabic.
Also notice the footnote contrasting with jurists.
2
Understand the rationale
Study the explanation following 'وجه الحصر' to see why there are exactly four types, based on existence and conceptual dependence.
3
Define each type
Go through the definitions of contradictories, contraries, privation/possession, and correlatives given later in the passage.
Pay attention to examples like black/white and sight/blindness.
4
Consider the objection
Read the objection and response at the end to see a potential challenge to the fourfold division and how it is handled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنواع المنافاة عند المناطقة أربعة : تنافي النقيضين، وتنافي الضّدَّين، وتنافي العَدَم والمَلكة ، وتنافي المتضايفين . قوله : (وأنواع المنافاة عند المناطقة ) : أي : وأما عند الأصوليين ؛ فهما اثنان فقط تنافي النَّقيضين وتنافي الضّدَّين، ويجعلون العدم والملكة داخلين في النقيضين، والمتضايفين داخلين في الضّدَّين. قوله : (أربعة) وجه الحصر فيها أنَّ المتقابلين إمَّا أن يكونا وجوديين أو وجوديّاً وعدميّاً، فإن كانا وجوديين؛ فلا يخلو إمَّا أن يتوقف تعقُل أحدهما على تعقُل الآخر أو لا . الأوَّل: المتضايفان كالأبوَّة والبنوَّة والثَّاني : المتضادان، كالبياض والسَّواد، وإن كان أحدهما وجوديّاً والآخر عدميّاً، فإن اعتبر في العدمي محله قابلاً للوجودي؛ كالبصر والعمى بالنسبة لزيد، لا بالنسبة للحائط ؛ فعدم وملكة، وإن لم يعتبر ذلك فتقابل النقيضين؛ كسواد ولا كون سواد. أما النقيضان : فهما إيجاب الشَّيء وسلبه، نحو: (زيد، لا زيد) و زید قائم، زيد ليس بقائم. وأمَّا الضّدَّان فهما المعنيان الوجوديَّان اللذان بينهما غاية الخلاف، ولا يتوقف تعقُل أحدهما على تعقل الآخر؛ كالبياض والسواد، واحترزنا بـ غاية الخلاف من نحو : البياض مع الحركة . وأمَّا العَدَم والمَلكة : فهما وجود الشَّيء وعدمه عما من شأنه أن يتصف به؛ كالبصر والعمى والعلم والجهل البسيط، فالبصر وجودي، وهو الملكة، والعمى عدمي، إذ العمى عدم شأنه البصر، وكذا العلم والجهل . واعترض الحصر بأن العدمي قد يقابل بالعدميّ؛ كالعمى، ولا عمى فهو أعم من أن يكون باعتبار الاتصاف بالبصر، أو باعتبار عدم القابلية، وعلى هذا فتزيد الأقسام على الأربعة المذكورة، ولكنَّ المنقول عن المناطقة هذه الأربعة، والإشكال لا يدفع النقل. قوله : (فهما إيجاب الشَّيء وسلبه) أي: ويكون في المفردات؛ كالمثال الأول والمركبات كالثَّاني. قوله: (من نحو: البياض مع الحركة :أي: فليس بينهما غاية الخلاف، إذ قد يرتفعان بأن يكون ساكناً أسود، وقد يجتمعان بأن يكون أبيض . قوله : (وأما العدم والملكة . . . إلخ) : اعلم: أن الملكة عبارة عن : . الأمر
And the types of opposition according to the logicians are four: opposition of contradictories, opposition of contraries, opposition of privation and possession, and opposition of correlatives. His saying: (And the types of opposition according to the logicians): i.e., but according to the jurists, they are only two: opposition of contradictories and opposition of contraries, and they include privation and possession within contradictories, and correlatives within contraries. His saying: (Four): the reason for the enumeration is that the two opposites are either both existent or one existent and one non-existent. If both are existent, then either the conceptualization of one depends on the conceptualization of the other or not. The first: correlatives like fatherhood and sonship; the second: contraries like whiteness and blackness. If one is existent and the other non-existent, then if the non-existent is considered in a substrate capable of the existent—like sight and blindness with respect to Zayd, not the wall—then it is privation and possession. And if that is not considered, then it is opposition of contradictories, like blackness and non-blackness. As for contradictories, they are the affirmation of a thing and its negation, e.g., (Zayd, not Zayd) and Zayd is standing, Zayd is not standing. As for contraries, they are two existent meanings between which there is extreme opposition, and the conceptualization of one does not depend on the conceptualization of the other—like whiteness and blackness. And we restricted by 'extreme opposition' to exclude things like whiteness and motion. As for privation and possession, they are the existence of a thing and its non-existence in that which is capable of being characterized by it—like sight and blindness, and knowledge and simple ignorance. For sight is existent, and it is possession, and blindness is non-existent, since blindness is the non-existence of what is capable of sight. Likewise knowledge and ignorance. And the enumeration was objected to on the grounds that a non-existent may be opposite to a non-existent—like blindness and no blindness—it is more general, whether considered in terms of being characterized by sight or in terms of lack of capacity. On this basis, the categories would increase beyond the four mentioned. But what is transmitted from the logicians is these four, and the problem does not refute the transmission. His saying: (They are the affirmation of a thing and its negation): i.e., and it occurs in single terms, like the first example, and in compounds, like the second. His saying: (From examples like whiteness with motion): i.e., there is no extreme opposition between them, for they may both be absent in something black and still, and they may both be present in something white and moving. His saying: (And as for privation and possession...etc.): Know that possession is an expression for... the matter.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification and definition of the four types of opposition (منافاة) according to the logicians. The author begins by stating that the types are four: opposition of contradictories (نقيضان), contraries (ضدان), privation and possession (عدم وملكة), and correlatives (متضايفان). He then notes that jurists reduce these to two, subsuming privation/possession under contradictories and correlatives under contraries. Next, he explains the rationale for the fourfold division: opposites are either both existent or one existent and one non-existent. If both existent, they are either correlatives (where one concept depends on the other, like father and son) or contraries (no mutual conceptual dependence, like black and white). If one is existent and the other non-existent, the opposition is either privation/possession (if the non-existent is considered in a substrate capable of the existent, e.g., blindness in an eye) or contradiction (if no such capacity is considered, e.g., 'black' and 'non-black'). He then defines each type in detail: contradictories are affirmation and negation; contraries are two existent extremes with no mutual dependence; privation/possession is the existence and absence of a property in something capable of it. Finally, an objection is raised: a non-existent can be opposed to another non-existent (e.g., blindness vs. no blindness), which would add more categories, but the author responds that the transmitted classification from the logicians is accepted despite the logical issue. The remaining lines are additional commentary on specific phrases, clarifying that contradictories can be in single terms or compounds, and that 'extreme opposition' excludes cases like whiteness and motion.

Correction: The passage clarifies that while logicians use four types, jurists use only two, and the logicians' division is based on specific criteria.

Al-Sawi defines al-naqidan and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of contradictories (al-naqidan) and how they appear in both simple and compound expressions.

Core Idea
Contradictories are the affirmation and negation of the same thing.

In this passage, al-Sawi clarifies one of the four types of opposition: contradictories. He defines them precisely and illustrates their occurrence in both single terms and full propositions. Understanding this distinction is essential for logical analysis in kalam.

The commentary clarifies that the first example 'Zayd' and 'not Zayd' shows contradiction in simple terms, while the second 'Zayd is standing' and 'Zayd is not standing' shows it in compound propositions. The explanation follows the author's order: first the definition, then the specification of its scope.

Definition of Contraries (al-Didan)
Al-Sawi defines al-didan and distinguishes them from mere difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of contraries (al-didan) in Islamic theology and why certain pairs like whiteness and motion are not considered contraries.

Core Idea
Contraries are two existent meanings in utmost opposition that can never meet in the same subject.

This lesson introduces the technical term 'al-didan' (contraries) as used in classical kalam. Al-Sawi provides a precise definition that distinguishes true contraries from mere differences. By examining the examples of whiteness/blackness and whiteness/motion, you will grasp the key condition of mutual exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the structure: definition, condition, example, exclusion.
2
Identify the definition's components
Break down the definition: two existential meanings + utmost opposition + no dependence in understanding.
These three conditions are all necessary.
3
Understand the exclusion
See why whiteness and motion are excluded: they can coexist or both be absent, lacking utmost opposition.
This is the key differentiator.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for discussions about divine attributes in kalam.
True contraries cannot be present together in a subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الضّدَّان فهما المعنيان الوجوديَّان اللذان بينهما غاية الخلاف، ولا يتوقف تعقُل أحدهما على تعقل الآخر؛ كالبياض والسواد، واحترزنا بـ غاية الخلاف من نحو : البياض مع الحركة .
قوله: (من نحو: البياض مع الحركة) :أي: فليس بينهما غاية الخلاف، إذ قد يرتفعان بأن يكون ساكناً أسود، وقد يجتمعان بأن يكون أبيض
And as for the two contraries: they are the two existential meanings between which there is utmost opposition, and understanding one does not depend on understanding the other; like whiteness and blackness. And we qualified with 'utmost opposition' to exclude such as whiteness and motion. His saying: (from such as whiteness and motion) means: there is not utmost opposition between them, for they may both be absent (as in a motionless black object) and they may coexist (as in a white moving object).
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines al-didan (contraries). The base text describes them as two existent meanings that have utmost opposition and do not depend on each other conceptually, using whiteness and blackness as examples. The qualification 'utmost opposition' excludes cases like whiteness and motion, which can coexist or both be absent. The commentary explains that whiteness and motion are not contrary because a black stationary object lacks both, and a white moving object has both. Thus, the definition is precise.

Defining Privation and Possession
Al-Sawi defines privation and possession with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of privation (al-‘adam) and possession (al-malaka), and why privation requires a subject capable of possessing the quality.

Core Idea
Privation is the absence of a quality where it belongs.

The author begins by defining a key category of opposites: privation and possession. Unlike simple absence, privation is the lack of a quality in something that is naturally meant to have it. This distinction is crucial for understanding how theologians classify attributes of Allah and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully.
Read the Arabic definition and its translation. Notice the key phrase 'camma man yattasifu bihi' meaning 'from that which is capable of being described by it'.
2
Identify the examples.
Sight and blindness, knowledge and ignorance. See that one is existential (possession) and the other is its absence (privation).
Privation is not just any absence.
3
Ask: Why is the condition important?
Consider why the definition specifies that the absence must be in a subject that normally has that quality. This distinguishes privation from mere non-existence.
A stone lacks sight, but that is not privation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا العَدَم والمَلكة : فهما وجود الشَّيء وعدمه عما من يتصف به؛ كالبصر والعمى والعلم والجهل البسيط، فالبصرُ البصر وجودي، وهو الملكة، والعمى عدمي، إذ العمى عدم شأنه البصر، وكذا العلم والجهل .
واعترض الحصر بأن العدمي قد يقابل بالعدميّ؛ كالعمى، ولا عمى فهو أعم من أن يكون باعتبار الاتصاف بالبصر، أو باعتبار عدم القابلية، وعلى هذا فتزيد الأقسام على الأربعة المذكورة، ولكنَّ المنقول عن المناطقة هذه الأربعة، والإشكال لا يدفع الأنقال.
قوله : (وأما العدم والملكة . . . إلخ) : اعلم: أن الملكة عبارة عن الأمر الوجودي القائم بالشَّيء ; كالبصر ; فإنَّه أمر وجودي قائم بالعين، والعدم عبارة عن انتفاء تلك الملكة على المحلّ، الذي شأنه أن يتّصف بتلك الملكة وقت انتفائها، فقول الشَّرح : (عمَّا من شأنه أن يتصف به أي : عن المحل الذي شأنه أن يتصف به وقت النَّفي والتمثيل لمقابلة العدم للملكة، بمقابلة العمى ،للبصر، بناء على مذهب الحكماء، وعند المتكلمين: العمى وصف وجودي قائم بالعين ; كالبصر، وحينئذ فالتقابل بينهما من تقابل الضدين .
And as for privation and possession: they are the existence of a thing and its absence in that which is capable of being described by it; like sight and blindness, and knowledge and simple ignorance. Sight is existential and is possession, and blindness is non-existential, for blindness is the absence of what would be sight, and similarly knowledge and ignorance. And the enumeration was objected to on the grounds that a non-existential may be opposed by a non-existential, like blindness and not-blindness; so it is broader: either with respect to being described by sight, or with respect to lack of capacity. On this, the categories increase beyond the four mentioned. But what is transmitted from the logicians is these four, and the objection does not repudiate what is transmitted.
His saying: (As for privation and possession ... etc.): Know that possession is the existential attribute subsisting in the thing, like sight, for it is an existential attribute subsisting in the eye. Privation is the absence of that possession in the subject which is capable of being described by that possession at the time of its absence. Thus the words of the commentary: 'from that which is capable of being described by it' means: from the subject that is capable of being described by it at the time of negation. And the example of the opposition of privation to possession is the opposition of blindness to sight, based on the doctrine of the philosophers. According to the mutakallimun, blindness is an existential attribute subsisting in the eye, like sight; in that case their opposition is the opposition of contraries.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines al-‘adam wa al-malaka (privation and possession) and addresses an objection. The base text defines it as the existence and absence of a quality in a subject naturally capable of it, giving examples: sight (possession) vs blindness (privation), knowledge vs simple ignorance. Then an objection is raised: the enumeration of four opposites is challenged because a privation (like blindness) can be opposed by another privation (like not-blindness) in different senses (lack of capacity vs absence in a capable subject), which would yield more types. The response is that what is transmitted from logicians is only these four, and the objection does not refute the transmitted classification. The hashya then explains possession as an existential attribute, and privation as its absence in a capable subject. It clarifies that the example of blindness vs sight is based on the philosophers’ view that blindness is a privation; according to the mutakallimun, blindness is an existential attribute, making the opposition one of contraries, not privation/possession. This shows a key doctrinal difference between philosophers and theologians.

Key Terms
العدم والملكة
الملكة
العدم
البصر
العمى
الجهل البسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Privation is simply the non-existence of something, regardless of the subject's capacity.
Correction: Privation (al-‘adam) is specifically the absence of a quality in a subject that is naturally fit to have it. For example, a stone lacks sight, but that is not privation because a stone is not capable of sight. Blindness in a human is privation because humans are capable of sight.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of privation (al-‘adam) according to the definition?
A) The non-existence of a stone.
B) Blindness in a human.
C) The absence of wings in a human.
D) The absence of sight in a statue.

Key Takeaway
Privation is the absence of a quality in a subject that is naturally capable of possessing it, making it distinct from mere non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life have you assumed you possess knowledge when, in truth, you are simply ignorant (the absence of knowledge)? How does this lesson's definition of simple ignorance challenge you to seek the truth before it's too late?
?
Consider your spiritual blindness—are there truths about your own soul that you have avoided acknowledging? What would it mean for you to let that privation be filled by sight from Allah?
?
You have no excuse to remain ignorant: privation is not a thing you possess; it is a lack. What step will you take today to replace one area of simple ignorance with knowledge?

Objection and Doctrinal Difference on Blindness
Al-Sawi addresses an objection and explains the nature of blindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the enumeration of four opposites and the doctrinal difference between philosophers and mutakallimun regarding the nature of blindness.

Core Idea
Blindness: privation or existential attribute? Philosophers and theologians disagree.

The text now addresses an objection to the classification of opposites. Some argued that privation can be opposed by another privation, which would increase the number of categories. The author then comments on the nature of blindness, revealing a key difference between the philosophers (hukama') and the theologians (mutakallimun).

Guided Path
1
Read the objection.
The objection states that a privation (like blindness) can be opposed by another privation (like not-blindness), which would add more types beyond the four opposites.
The objector seems to be a logician.
2
Consider the response.
The response is that the transmitted classification from logicians is only these four, and the objection doesn't refute the transmitted classification.
Transmission is authoritative.
3
Examine the hashya's explanation.
The hashya explains that possession is an existential attribute, and privation is its absence in a capable subject. The example of blindness opposing sight is based on the philosophers' view that blindness is a privation.
4
Understand the doctrinal difference.
According to the mutakallimun, blindness is an existential attribute like sight, so their opposition is that of contraries, not privation/possession.
This affects how categories are applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا العَدَم والمَلكة : فهما وجود الشَّيء وعدمه عما من يتصف به؛ كالبصر والعمى والعلم والجهل البسيط، فالبصرُ البصر وجودي، وهو الملكة، والعمى عدمي، إذ العمى عدم شأنه البصر، وكذا العلم والجهل .
واعترض الحصر بأن العدمي قد يقابل بالعدميّ؛ كالعمى، ولا عمى فهو أعم من أن يكون باعتبار الاتصاف بالبصر، أو باعتبار عدم القابلية، وعلى هذا فتزيد الأقسام على الأربعة المذكورة، ولكنَّ المنقول عن المناطقة هذه الأربعة، والإشكال لا يدفع الأنقال.
قوله : (وأما العدم والملكة . . . إلخ) : اعلم: أن الملكة عبارة عن الأمر الوجودي القائم بالشَّيء ; كالبصر ; فإنَّه أمر وجودي قائم بالعين، والعدم عبارة عن انتفاء تلك الملكة على المحلّ، الذي شأنه أن يتّصف بتلك الملكة وقت انتفائها، فقول الشَّرح : (عمَّا من شأنه أن يتصف به أي : عن المحل الذي شأنه أن يتصف به وقت النَّفي والتمثيل لمقابلة العدم للملكة، بمقابلة العمى ،للبصر، بناء على مذهب الحكماء، وعند المتكلمين: العمى وصف وجودي قائم بالعين ; كالبصر، وحينئذ فالتقابل بينهما من تقابل الضدين .
And as for privation and possession: they are the existence of a thing and its absence in that which is capable of being described by it; like sight and blindness, and knowledge and simple ignorance. Sight is existential and is possession, and blindness is non-existential, for blindness is the absence of what would be sight, and similarly knowledge and ignorance. And the enumeration was objected to on the grounds that a non-existential may be opposed by a non-existential, like blindness and not-blindness; so it is broader: either with respect to being described by sight, or with respect to lack of capacity. On this, the categories increase beyond the four mentioned. But what is transmitted from the logicians is these four, and the objection does not repudiate what is transmitted.
His saying: (As for privation and possession ... etc.): Know that possession is the existential attribute subsisting in the thing, like sight, for it is an existential attribute subsisting in the eye. Privation is the absence of that possession in the subject which is capable of being described by that possession at the time of its absence. Thus the words of the commentary: 'from that which is capable of being described by it' means: from the subject that is capable of being described by it at the time of negation. And the example of the opposition of privation to possession is the opposition of blindness to sight, based on the doctrine of the philosophers. According to the mutakallimun, blindness is an existential attribute subsisting in the eye, like sight; in that case their opposition is the opposition of contraries.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines al-‘adam wa al-malaka (privation and possession) and addresses an objection. The base text defines it as the existence and absence of a quality in a subject naturally capable of it, giving examples: sight (possession) vs blindness (privation), knowledge vs simple ignorance. Then an objection is raised: the enumeration of four opposites is challenged because a privation (like blindness) can be opposed by another privation (like not-blindness) in different senses (lack of capacity vs absence in a capable subject), which would yield more types. The response is that what is transmitted from logicians is only these four, and the objection does not refute the transmitted classification. The hashya then explains possession as an existential attribute, and privation as its absence in a capable subject. It clarifies that the example of blindness vs sight is based on the philosophers’ view that blindness is a privation; according to the mutakallimun, blindness is an existential attribute, making the opposition one of contraries, not privation/possession. This shows a key doctrinal difference between philosophers and theologians.

Key Terms
الحكماء
المتكلمين
الضدان
العمى
البصر
الاعتراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scholars agree that blindness is a privation (absence of sight).
Correction: The mutakallimun (theologians) hold that blindness is an existential attribute (a positive quality), not a privation, so the opposition between sight and blindness is that of contraries, not privation/possession.

Checkpoint
According to the mutakallimun, how is blindness classified?
A) As a privation opposite to sight.
B) As an existential attribute opposite to sight.
C) As a non-existential attribute.
D) As a contradictory of sight.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers see blindness as privation, while the mutakallimun see it as an existential attribute, affecting how the opposition to sight is classified.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you ever categorised someone or something based on a framework without considering the deeper reality, as the two schools differ here? Write down one way you can apply the mutakallimun's approach of seeing even absences as existent qualities in your own spiritual life.
?
The passage shows an objection that is acknowledged but not allowed to overturn the transmitted knowledge. In your own learning, how do you handle objections that challenge established teachings? Do you dismiss them easily, or do you hold them in tension? This lesson suggests that the transmitted classification stands despite the objection. What is one area of your faith where you need to trust the transmitted knowledge even when intellectual objections arise?
?
Both the philosophers and mutakallimun are discussing the nature of blindness. But note: blindness is a real condition. How does the way you 'classify' someone's afflictions affect how you relate to them? Do you see a person's blindness as a lack (privation) or as an attribute that shapes their existence? Think of someone you know with a disability. How might your theological view change your compassion?

Definition of Correlatives
Al-Sawi defines correlatives and clarifies 'existential'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of correlatives and why 'existential' in this context means not a privation, not external existence.

Core Idea
Correlatives exist only in the mind, not externally.

This lesson explores the foundational definition of al-mutadayifan (correlatives) as used in kalam. The author clarifies that 'existential' here refers to a meaning that is not a privation, unlike entities that exist in the external world. We will see how fatherhood and sonship exemplify this mutual dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the first sentence in Arabic: 'وأمَّا المتضايفان فهما الأمران الوجوديَّان...' and its translation. Notice the three elements: they are existential, there is utmost opposition, and mutual dependence in understanding.
Focus on the phrase 'غاية الخلاف' – it means extreme difference, not compatibility.
2
Understand the clarification of 'existential'
The text says 'المراد بالوجودي في المتضايفين ما ليس معناه عدم كذا' – existential here means the meaning is not an absence. This is a technical usage. Then it says fatherhood has no external existence – it is purely mental.
This guards against a common misunderstanding that correlatives are real entities.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider fatherhood and sonship. You cannot understand one without the other, and they are not physical objects. This helps solidify the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا المتضايفان فهما الأمران الوجوديَّان اللذان بينهما غاية الخلاف، ويتوقف تعقل أحدهما على تعقل الآخر؛ كالأُبوة والبنوة. والمراد بالوجودي في المتضايفين ما ليس معناه عدم كذا، لا الموجود في الخارج عن الذهن، إذ الأبوَّة مثلاً لا وجود لها في الخارج عن الذهن.
قوله: (لا وجود لها في الخارج عن الذهن) : أي: خلافاً للفلاسفة القائلين : بأنَّ الأمور النِّسيَّة كالإضافات وغيرها أعراض موجودة.
قوله : (كالبياض والحركة) أي وكلُّ متخالفين في الحقيقة يمكن اجتماعهما ; كالقدرة والعلم مثلاً .
قوله : (بأنَّ المحلَّ لو قبل المثلين . . . إلخ ) : حاصله قياس استثنائي
And as for the two correlatives: they are the two existential matters between which there is utmost opposition, and understanding one depends on understanding the other; like fatherhood and sonship. What is meant by 'existential' in correlatives is that whose meaning is not 'absence of such-and-such', not that which exists externally in the mind, for fatherhood, for example, has no existence external to the mind.
His saying: (has no existence external to the mind) means: contrary to the philosophers who say that relative matters, like relations and others, are existing accidents.
His saying: (like whiteness and motion) means: and every two things differing in reality that can coexist, like power and knowledge, for example.
His saying: (that if the subject were to receive two similars ... etc.): its summary is a hypothetical syllogism.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines al-mutadayifan (correlatives). The base text states they are two existential matters with utmost opposition and mutual dependence in understanding, like fatherhood and sonship. It clarifies that 'existential' here means not being a privation, not that they exist externally—since fatherhood exists only in the mind. The hashya first refutes the philosophers who claim relations are real accidents. Then it explains that the example 'like whiteness and motion' refers to any two things that can coexist (like power and knowledge). Finally, it begins to explain a proof about similars (two identical attributes) but cuts off. The commentary follows the author's order: definition, clarification, refutation of philosophers, then an additional example, and the start of a logical proof.

Key Terms

الأبوة
البنوة
الوجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correlatives exist as real accidents in the external world.
Correction: The text explicitly says fatherhood has no existence external to the mind, so correlatives are mental relations.

Checkpoint
What does 'existential' (الوجودي) mean in the definition of correlatives?
A) Existing externally in the mind.
B) Not being a privation.
C) Being an accident in the world.
D) Being a substance.

Key Takeaway
Correlatives are mutually dependent mental concepts, not external realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If relationships like fatherhood and sonship are only mental constructs, does that make the responsibilities tied to them less real? How does this affect the way you treat your family members today?
?
The passage says understanding one correlative depends on understanding the other. In your relationship with Allah, do you see that knowing yourself as a servant depends on knowing Him as Lord? What step will you take this week to deepen that mutual understanding?
?
Correlatives exist only in the mind, yet they shape your daily life. Are you more attached to the mental labels you carry (son, daughter, spouse) than to the actual consciousness of those relations? How can you shift from automatic role-playing to mindful awareness of your relationships?

Refutation of the Philosophers on Relations
Al-Sawi refutes the philosophers on the existence of relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam position that relative matters (like relations) are not real accidents existing in the external world, contrary to the philosophers.

Core Idea
Relations are mental, not real accidents.

Building on the definition, Al-Sawi now directly addresses the philosophers who claim that relative matters such as relations are real accidents. This lesson unpacks that refutation and explains why the kalam school maintains that relations are purely mental constructs.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the hashya: 'قوله: (لا وجود لها في الخارج عن الذهن) : أي: خلافاً للفلاسفة...' and its translation. Identify the philosophers' claim and the counterclaim.
The philosophers say relations (al-umūr al-nisbiyya) are existing accidents.
2
Understand the kalam position
The text says relations like fatherhood have no external existence – they only exist in the mind. This is a key difference from the philosophers.
The term 'al-umūr al-nisbiyya' refers to relative matters, including relations.
3
Consider why this matters
If relations were real accidents, they would be part of the created world. This has implications for how we understand attributes of Allah and other theological issues.

Example and Proof of Similars
Al-Sawi gives an example and begins a proof about similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the example of whiteness and motion as diverse things that can coexist, and the beginning of a proof that two similar attributes cannot coexist in the same subject.

Core Idea
Attributes that differ can coexist; identical ones cannot.

This lesson covers two distinct parts: first, an additional example to show that correlatives (like whiteness and motion) are not the only pair; second, the start of a logical proof about two similar attributes. The proof is incomplete here but introduces an important kalam argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the example passage
Read 'قوله : (كالبياض والحركة) أي وكلُّ متخالفين في الحقيقة يمكن اجتماعهما ; كالقدرة والعلم مثلاً .' Understand that whiteness and motion are different in reality but can coexist in a body.
This example illustrates that correlatives are not the only pair; any two differing things that can coexist are similar in that respect.
2
Read the proof beginning
Read 'قوله : (بأنَّ المحلَّ لو قبل المثلين . . . إلخ ) : حاصله قياس استثنائي'. Recognize that it is a hypothetical syllogism, though the full argument is not given here.
The proof is about a substrate (maḥall) receiving two similars (mithlayn). This likely argues that two identical attributes cannot coexist in one subject.
3
Connect to the lesson
This proof is related to the earlier refutation: if relations were real accidents, they might conflict with this principle. But the text cuts off; we only have the summary.

?

Definition of Correlatives in Kalam
Al-Sawi defines correlatives with two conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of correlatives (al-mutadayifan) as used by Islamic theologians, including their two key characteristics.

Core Idea
Correlatives exist in pairs that cannot be understood without each other.

In this passage, al-Sawi provides the technical definition of correlatives, a key term in Islamic theological discourse. He explains that they are two existential matters that are maximally opposed and whose conceptualization depends on each other. Let's examine this definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition of al-mutadayifan. Notice the two conditions: being existential, being maximally opposed, and mutual dependency in conceptualization.
2
Understand the example
Al-Sawi gives the example of paternity and sonship. Reflect on how these two are opposite yet dependent on each other for meaning.
3
Grasp the significance
Why does this definition matter? It establishes a foundational concept for many kalam arguments about attributes and relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا المتضايفان فهما الأمران الوجوديَّان اللذان بينهما غاية الخلاف، ويتوقف تعقل أحدهما على تعقل الآخر؛ كالأُبوة والبنوة. والمراد بالوجودي في المتضايفين ما ليس معناه عدم كذا، لا الموجود في الخارج عن الذهن، إذ الأبوَّة مثلاً لا وجود لها في الخارج عن الذهن. قوله: (لا وجود لها في الخارج عن الذهن) : أي : خلافاً للفلاسفة القائلين : بأنَّ الأمور النِّسيَّة كالإضافات وغيرها أعراض موجودة.
As for correlatives (al-mutadayifan), they are the two existential matters between which there is utmost opposition, and the conceptualization of one depends on the conceptualization of the other, like paternity and sonship. And what is meant by 'existential' in correlatives is that whose meaning is not 'non-existence of something', not that which exists externally in the mind, for paternity, for example, has no existence outside the mind. His statement: 'No existence outside the mind' means contrary to the philosophers who say that relational matters (al-umur al-nisbiyya), like relations (al-idafat) and others, are existing accidents.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation. First, al-Sawi defines al-mutadayifan (correlatives) as two existential (wujudiyyan) matters that are maximally opposed and whose conceptualization depends on each other, like paternity and sonship. Then he explains the intended meaning of 'wujudiy' in this context: it is not that they exist externally, but rather that their meaning is not 'non-existence of something'. For example, paternity has no existence outside the mind. Then he adds a gloss on the phrase 'no existence outside the mind', saying this is contrary to the philosophers who claim that relational matters (al-umur al-nisbiyya) like relations and others are existing accidents.

Key Terms
المتضايفان (al-mutadayifan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Existence and non-existence

D) Being accidents and substances

Key Takeaway
Correlatives are opposite pairs whose meanings rely on each other.

The Meaning of 'Wujudiy' in Correlatives
Al-Sawi clarifies the term 'existential' and refutes philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why 'wujudiy' in the definition of correlatives does not mean existence in external reality, and how this differs from the philosophers' view.

Core Idea
'Wujudiy' here means not being a negation, not existing externally.

Having defined correlatives, al-Sawi now clarifies a potential confusion about the term 'wujudiy' (existential). He explains that it does not refer to external existence, but rather to the fact that correlatives are affirmations of positive meanings. He then contrasts this with the philosophers who consider relational matters as existing accidents.

Guided Path
1
Clarify 'wujudiy'
Read al-Sawi's clarification that 'wujudiy' in this context means 'what is not the meaning of non-existence of something'. Understand that it is about logical positivity.
2
Reject external existence
Notice that he explicitly denies that correlatives like paternity have external existence. They are concepts, not external things.
3
The philosophers' view
Read the gloss about the philosophers' claim that relational matters are existing accidents. Understand the disagreement.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Why does this distinction matter for kalam? It preserves that attributes like relations are not independent entities.

Definition of Wali and Reason for the Name
Al-Sawi defines the wali linguistically and terminologically and explains the name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and terminological definition of wali (saint) in Islamic theology and the reason why such individuals are called 'awliya'.

Core Idea
A wali is defined by fulfilling rights and knowing God, not merely by friendship.

This passage from Al-Sawi's commentary establishes the foundational definition of wali, a term central to discussions of sainthood and miracles in Islam. Before exploring the extraordinary acts (karamat) of the awliya, Al-Sawi clarifies who qualifies as a wali and why they are so named. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once through to get the flow. Notice the concise style of definition and the two points: the definition itself and the etymology.
2
Understand the definition
Focus on the definition of wali: the one who fulfills the rights of God and people, knows God and His attributes, is diligent in obedience, avoids sin, and turns away from excessive pleasures. Note the phrase 'حسب الإمكان' (according to possibility).
This definition combines external action and inner knowledge.
3
Examine the etymology
The word 'wali' is derived from either the wali undertaking God's service (تولى خدمة الله) or God undertaking the wali's affairs (تولى الله أمره). Both highlight a special relationship of devotion and divine care.
The dual derivation shows the mutual connection: the wali serves God, and God takes care of the wali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جمع وليّ، وهو القائم بحقوق الله تعالى وحقوق العباد حسب الإمكان، وهو معنى قول من قال : هو العارف بالله تعالى وصفاته حسب الإمكان المواظب على الطاعات، المُجتَنِب للمخالفات، المُعرِض عن الانهماك في اللذات والشهوات. ويجب اعتقاد كراماتهم والكرامة أمر خارق للعادة يظهر على الميزان الشّرع. قوله: (جمع ولي ) : سمّي بذلك ؛ لأنَّه تولَّى خدمة الله، أو لأنَّ الله تولى أمره، فلم يكله لغيره طرفة عين.
Plural of wali, and he is the one who upholds the rights of God Most High and the rights of servants according to possibility, and that is the meaning of the statement of one who said: he is the knower of God Most High and His attributes according to possibility, diligent in acts of obedience, avoiding acts of disobedience, turning away from indulgence in pleasures and desires. And belief in their karamat is obligatory, and the karamah is an extraordinary matter that appears according to the scale of the Law. His statement: (Plural of wali): he is called that because he undertakes the service of God, or because God undertakes his affair, so He does not entrust him to other than Him for the blink of an eye.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage provides a lexical and terminological definition of wali (saint). It begins by stating that 'awliya' is the plural of wali, and then defines the wali as one who fulfills the rights of God and the rights of people to the best of his ability. This is further explained by another definition: the wali is one who knows God and His attributes, perseveres in acts of worship, avoids sins, and turns away from excessive indulgence in worldly pleasures. The author then asserts that belief in the karamat (extraordinary acts) of awliya is obligatory, and gives a preliminary definition of karamah as an extraordinary matter that appears in accordance with the Sharia. Finally, the etymology of the word 'wali' is given: it is derived either from the wali's undertaking of God's service (tawalla) or from God undertaking the wali's affairs (tawalla), meaning that God takes care of him completely. This dual derivation highlights the relationship of mutual devotion and divine protection.

Key Terms
ولي
أولياء
كرامة
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think wali means only a 'friend' in a general sense
Correction: The definition here is specific: one who fulfills rights and has knowledge of God.

Checkpoint

B) Because he undertakes God's service or God undertakes his affairs.
C) Because he performs miracles.
D) Because he is a prophet.

Key Takeaway
The term 'wali' combines active devotion (fulfilling rights) and divine guardianship (God taking charge), forming the basis for the concept of sainthood in Islam.

Deriving Existence from Exception and Self-Sufficiency
Answer to objection on existence derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection about deriving existence from the Shahada's exception clause is resolved by distinguishing between absolute existence and necessary existence.

Core Idea
What is taken from 'exception' is absolute existence; from 'self-sufficiency' is necessary existence.

The author presents an objection regarding the derivation of existence from the Shahada formula. Some might think that the exception clause ('illa Allah') yields existence in an absolute sense, but the answer clarifies that it is necessary existence that is derived from self-sufficiency. This distinction is crucial for understanding the precise attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The passage begins with an answer to an implicit objection. Notice how the answer immediately distinguishes between two types of existence.
The objection is not explicitly stated; it is inferred from the answer.
2
Identify the two terms
The answer mentions 'absolute existence' (mujarrad al-wujūd) from the exception, and 'necessary existence' (wujūb al-wujūd) from self-sufficiency. Understand the difference.
3
Interpret the phrase 'yūjibu lahu al-wujūd'
The author clarifies that this phrase means 'necessitates necessary existence', not just any existence. This is the key correction.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أجيب : بأنَّ المأخوذ من الاستثناء مطلق الوجود، والمأخوذ من الاستغناء، وجوب الوجود، فقوله : (يوجب له الوجود) أي: وجوب الوجود.
قوله: (وقيامه بنفسه : إن قلت : إنَّ القيام بالنفس هو الاستغناء فيلزم عليه اتحاد الموجب والموجب، فكأنَّه قال : الاستغناء أوجب الاستغناء . أجيب بأنَّ القيام بالنَّفس استغناء خاص وهو الاستغناء عن المحل : والمخصص، والاستغناء الموجب الذي هو أحد جزأي مدلول الكلمة المشرفة ،عام وإثبات العام يستلزم إثبات الخاص.
It was answered: That which is taken from the exception is absolute existence, and that which is taken from self-sufficiency is necessary existence, so his statement: (necessitates existence for Him) means necessary existence. His statement: (and His self-subsistence): If you say: Self-subsistence is self-sufficiency, so it follows that the necessitator and the necessitated are the same, as if he said: self-sufficiency necessitates self-sufficiency. It was answered: Self-subsistence is a specific self-sufficiency, namely independence from place and specifier, and the self-sufficiency that necessitates is general, which is one of the two parts of the meaning of the noble word, and affirming the general necessitates affirming the specific.
Source pages p. 396

Commentary
This passage contains two objections and their answers. The first objection (implied by 'أجيب') is about how existence is derived: the answer explains that 'الإستثناء' gives absolute existence, while 'الاستغناء' gives necessary existence, so 'يوجب له الوجود' means necessary existence. The second objection is explicit: if self-subsistence is self-sufficiency, then the statement 'self-subsistence necessitates self-subsistence' would be circular. The answer distinguishes: self-subsistence is a specific self-sufficiency (independence from place and specifier), while the self-sufficiency mentioned in the Shahada is general; affirming the general does not cause circularity, and the specific follows from the general.

Key Terms
الاستثناء
الاستغناء
مطلق الوجود
وجوب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The exception clause in the Shahada directly proves necessary existence.
Correction: The exception clause yields absolute existence, not necessary existence; necessary existence is derived from the attribute of self-sufficiency (al-istighnā') indicated by the word 'Allāh'.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does the exception ('istithnā') give?
A) Necessary existence
B) Absolute existence
C) Self-sufficiency
D) Self-subsistence

Key Takeaway
The exception in the Shahada provides absolute existence, while necessary existence is taken from self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Lā ilāha illā Allāh', do you merely affirm that God exists, or do you profoundly recognize that He is necessarily existent and self-sufficient? How does this distinction shape the way you depend on Him each day?
?
What would change in your life if you truly internalized that God's existence is not just absolute but necessary—meaning He cannot not exist, and you cannot escape His reality? Are you living as if His existence is optional or compulsory?
?
The exception in the Shahada gives absolute existence, but does your heart settle for merely acknowledging God's existence without embracing His self-sufficiency and necessary nature? What step will you take today to shift from passive belief to active reliance on the Necessary Being?

Resolving the Circularity of Self-Subsistence and Self-Sufficiency
Objection and answer on self-subsistence circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs) leads to circularity is resolved by distinguishing specific self-sufficiency from general self-sufficiency.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence is a specific self-sufficiency, so there is no circularity.

A second objection arises: if self-subsistence is equated to self-sufficiency, then the statement 'self-subsistence necessitates self-subsistence' would be circular. The author answers by clarifying that self-subsistence is a particular kind of self-sufficiency—independence from place and specifier—while the self-sufficiency that necessitates is general. Affirming the general indeed implies the specific, so no logical problem occurs.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
The passage presents an explicit objection and its answer. Identify the objection's claim of circularity.
2
Distinguish specific and general self-sufficiency
The answer introduces a distinction: self-subsistence is a specific self-sufficiency (independence from place/specifier), while the self-sufficiency from the Shahada is general.
This is the core of the resolution.
3
Understand the logical relationship
Affirming the general self-sufficiency logically entails the specific self-subsistence, so no circularity occurs; it is a valid inference.
This connects to the learning objective.

Junayd's Aphorisms on Sincerity and Inner Worship
Four aphorisms of Junayd al-Baghdadi about spiritual sincerity and inner awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four key teachings of Junayd al-Baghdadi on sincerity, hidden worship, and the value of time.

Core Idea
True worship includes invisible acts of the heart that even angels cannot record.

Junayd al-Baghdadi, a master of Islamic spirituality, offers four profound sayings that cut through outward appearances. He warns of the immense loss from even a moment of heedlessness, highlights divine generosity, reveals the hidden dimension of worship known only to God, and urges us to guard our fleeting hours. These aphorisms redirect our focus from external deeds to the inner reality of faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the four sayings
Read the Arabic and translation without analysis. Let the words settle.
2
Reflect on the first saying
Notice the contrast between a thousand years of devotion and a moment of turning away. What does that teach about vigilance?
3
Reflect on the second saying
How does a single atom of divine generosity bridge the gap between sinner and righteous? What does that say about God's mercy?
4
Reflect on the third saying
Identify the hidden acts the recording angels do not see. Why are these considered superior?
5
Reflect on the fourth saying
Consider the urgency of guarding time and performing litanies. How does this connect to the first saying?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن كلامه أيضاً : (لو أقبل صادق على الله ألف ألف سنة ثمَّ أعرض لحظةً ؛ كان ما فاته أكثر ممَّا ناله) (۱) .
ومن كلامه أيضاً : (إن بدت ذرّةٌ من عين الكرم والجود؛ ألحقت المسيء المحسن، وبقيت أعمالهم فضلاً لهم .
ومن كلامه أيضاً : (من الأعمال ما لا يطلع عليه الحفظة، وهو ذكر الله بالقلب، وما طويت عليه الضَّمائر من الهيبة والتعظيم الله، واعتماد الخوف، وإجلال أوامره ونواهيه)(۲).
ومن كلامه أيضاً : احفظوا ساعاتكم؛ فإنَّها زائلة غير راجعة، وصلوا أورادكم تجدوا نفعها في دار الإقامة، ولا يشغلكم عن الله قليل الله قليل الدُّنيا ؛ فإنَّ قليلها يشغل عن كثير الآخرة) (۳) .
And among his sayings also: 'If a sincere one turns to God for a thousand thousand years and then turns away for a moment, what he missed is more than what he attained.' (1)
And among his sayings also: 'If an atom from the eye of generosity and munificence appears, it joins the evildoer to the good-doer, and their works remain as a favor for them.'
And among his sayings also: 'Among works are those which the recording angels do not become aware of, and that is the remembrance of God in the heart, and what the hearts conceal of awe and glorification of God, and reliance on fear, and reverence for His commands and prohibitions.' (2)
And among his sayings also: 'Guard your hours, for they are fleeting not returning, and perform your litanies, you will find their benefit in the abode of permanence, and let not the little of this world distract you from God, for its little distracts from the much of the Hereafter.' (3)
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage presents four separate sayings attributed to Junayd al-Baghdadi. The first saying teaches that even a brief moment of turning away from God after long devotion outweighs all previous good in terms of loss, emphasizing the immense danger of heedlessness. The second saying illustrates divine generosity: a single atom of God's generous regard can elevate a sinner to the rank of a good-doer, making their works a gratuitous favor. The third saying distinguishes between outward acts and inward states: the recording angels cannot perceive the remembrance of God in the heart or the inner feelings of awe, fear, and reverence for God's commands—these are hidden acts of worship. The fourth saying exhorts the reader to guard time carefully because it is irreplaceable, to perform regular litanies (awrad) which will benefit in the afterlife, and not to let worldly distractions occupy one's attention away from God, since even a small preoccupation with this world can hinder the pursuit of the greater reward of the Hereafter.

Key Terms
صادق
ذكر الله
الحفظة
أوراد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only outward deeds matter to God.
Correction: Junayd teaches that inward states like heart-remembrance and awe of God are beyond angelic perception and are of great value.

Checkpoint
According to the third saying, what kind of work do the recording angels not perceive?
A) Charitable donations given in secret.
B) The remembrance of God in the heart and inner awe of God.
C) Night prayers performed alone.
D) Fasting on a cloudy day.

Key Takeaway
The essence of worship lies in the unseen heart, and every moment must be guarded for the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have prayed, fasted, and given charity—but has your heart ever truly felt awe of God that no one else knows? The third aphorism says such hidden states are beyond the angels' record. When you are alone, what is the state of your heart regarding God's commands and prohibitions? Will you dare to examine that inner reality today?
?
Imagine you have worshipped God for a thousand years, then turned away for a single moment. The loss, says the first aphorism, is greater than all you gained. How many moments today have you been heedless? Identify one specific hour you let slip. What will you do to 'guard that hour' tomorrow?
?
You have likely felt despair over your sins, thinking your deeds are ruined. But the second aphorism reveals that a single atom of God's generosity can join you to the good-doers. Do you truly believe that? If yes, how will that change the way you view your shortcomings and your hope in God's mercy starting now?

The Four Types of Inner Thoughts
Sanusi defines the four types of inner thoughts and distinguishes divine from angelic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of inner thoughts (khawatir) and how they differ in terms of sin versus obedience, and specificity versus generality.

Core Idea
Your inner thoughts have four sources: self, devil, mercy, and angels.

In Islamic spirituality, not every passing thought is the same. Scholars have classified internal promptings into four categories based on their origin and effect. This passage from Sanusi's commentary gives precise definitions for each type, helping you discern whether a thought leads to sin, obedience, and whether it is specific or general.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to get an overview of the four types.
Notice the two-part structure: first the rabbani/malaki distinction, then the four definitions.
2
Understand the rabbani vs malaki distinction
The divine thought alerts to good without urging, while the angelic thought urges to obedience. This sets the stage for the four types.
3
Focus on the four definitions
Identify each of the four types: nafsani (specific sin), shaytani (non-specific sin), rahmani (specific obedience), malaki (non-specific obedience).
The pattern uses two parameters: outcome (sin vs obedience) and specificity.
4
Reflect on the practical application
Consider how this classification helps you recognize the source of your inner promptings and respond appropriately.
This is foundational for self-awareness and spiritual discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفرق بين الخاطر الرباني والخاطر الملكي: أن الأول ما فيه تنبيه على الخير من غير حث، ولا يؤدي إلى حيرة. والثاني: ما فيه حث على الطاعة. الخاطر النفساني: ما يلزم معصية بعينها. والشيطاني: ما يلزم معصية لا بعينها. والرحماني: ما يلزم طاعة بعينها. والملكي: ما يلزم طاعة لا بعينها.
And as for the difference between the divine thought (al-khatir al-rabbani) and the angelic thought (al-khatir al-malaki): the first is that which contains an alert to good without urging, and does not lead to confusion. The second: that which contains urging to obedience. The lower-self thought (al-khatir al-nafsani): that which necessitates a specific sin. The satanic thought (al-khatir al-shaytani): that which necessitates a sin without being specific. The merciful thought (al-khatir al-rahmani): that which necessitates a specific obedience. The angelic thought (al-khatir al-malaki): that which necessitates an obedience without being specific.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage defines four types of inner thoughts (khawatir) and also distinguishes between the divine and angelic ones. The author begins by explaining the difference between the divine thought (rabbani) and the angelic thought (malaki): the former alerts to good without urging and does not lead to confusion, while the latter contains urging to obedience. Then he provides definitions for the four main categories: nafsani (derived from the lower self) necessitates a specific sin; shaytani (satanic) necessitates a sin without it being specific; rahmani (merciful/divine) necessitates a specific obedience; malaki (angelic) necessitates an obedience without being specific. The key distinction is that nafsani and shaytani are related to sin, while rahmani and malaki are related to obedience. The specification (bi-aynihā) indicates a direct command for a particular action, while without specification it is a general inclination. This classification is important in Islamic spirituality for recognizing the source of inner promptings.

Key Terms
الخاطر
النفساني
الشيطاني
الرحماني
الملكي
الرباني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nafsani and shaytani thoughts are the same.
Correction: Nafsani necessitates a specific sin (e.g., lying to a particular person), while shaytani necessitates a sin without specifying the act (e.g., a general inclination to sin).

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the nafsani thought from the shaytani thought?
A) Nafsani necessitates a sin without specificity; shaytani with specificity.
B) Nafsani necessitates a specific sin; shaytani without specificity.
C) Nafsani leads to good; shaytani to evil.
D) They are the same.

Key Takeaway
The four types of inner thoughts are distinguished by their outcome (sin or obedience) and specificity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which type of thought do you find yourself most often acting upon—specific sins, vague temptations, clear commands, or gentle reminders? Are you aware of the difference between an angelic urge and a divine alert?
?
Reflect on a recent moment when you felt a sudden inclination toward disobedience. Was it driven by a specific desire (nafsani) or a vague temptation (shaytani)? How can you better identify the source next time?
?
The divine thought comes without urging and without confusion. Have you ever ignored a clear inner call to good because it lacked a sense of urgency? Will you commit to acting on such alerts immediately, trusting their source?

The Supplication for Protection from Worldly Obstacles
Al-Sawi explains the supplication and defines worldly obstacles with proof texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the supplication 'O Lord, do not cut me off from You' and the obstacles that worldly attachments pose to one's relationship with Allah.

Core Idea
Worldly attachments are obstacles that cut the heart off from Allah.

In this passage, al-Sawi unpacks a powerful supplication that asks Allah not to be cut off from Him. He explains that the 'obstacle' (qati') refers to any trial that diverts the heart from worship, specifying love of wealth, children, status, and desires. He then supports this with three Quranic verses that warn against being distracted from remembrance of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the supplication and its context
Begin by reading the Arabic supplication and its translation. Notice the state of abasement (biddhill) and the promise that Allah is with the broken-hearted.
The supplication is a plea, not just a ritual phrase.
2
Understand the meaning of 'biddhill'
Al-Sawi explains that 'biddhill' (with abasement) describes the state of the supplicant. The easier interpretation is that it is a circumstantial accusative: you should say the supplication while in a state of humility.
This underscores the importance of sincerity.
3
Identify the definition of 'qati'' (obstacle)
The obstacle is defined as every fitnah (trial) that occupies the heart from worship. Al-Sawi specifies these trials: love of wealth, children, status, and desires.
Worldly attachments are not inherently evil but can become obstacles.
4
Reflect on the three Quranic proofs
Read the three verses (64:15, 3:14, 63:9) that al-Sawi cites. Each emphasizes that wealth, children, and desires are tests and should not divert one from Allah.
The verses serve as authoritative evidence for the claim.

('With abasement'), then indeed God Most High is with those whose hearts are broken: 'O Lord, do not cut me off from You by any obstacle' from every trial that occupies the heart from worship, from love of wealth, children, status, and desires. 'Your wealth and your children are only a trial' (1), 'Adorned for people is the love of desires, from women and children...' the verse (2), 'O you who have believed, let not your wealth and your children divert you from the remembrance of Allah. His statement: ('biddhill'): The commentator made it related to an omitted [word] as an adjective of an omitted verbal noun that is an absolute object of ('qul'), and the ba is for accompaniment, and there is awkwardness in it, and the easier [interpretation] is to make the prepositional phrase related to an omitted [word] as a circumstantial accusative of the subject of ('qul'), and the estimation: Say: 'O Lord, do not cut me off...' etc., while you are in a state of being clothed in abasement.
And the genitive [phrase].
His statement: ('Then indeed God Most High is with those whose hearts are broken'): It is a cause for what came before it, and in it there is a quotation from the sacred hadith: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken because of Me.'
His statement: ('from every trial'): It is an explanation of the obstacle.
And his statement: ('from') love of wealth... etc.) It is an explanation of the trial.
(4)

(2) Surah Al Imran: 14.
(3) Surah al-Munafiqun: 9.
(4) Reported by Abu Nu'aym in 'Hilyat al-Awliya'' (4/31).
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on a line of the original poem or its gloss (sharh). The commentator, al-Sawi, begins by explaining the word 'biddhill' (with abasement) as it appears in the supplication: 'O Lord, do not cut me off from You by any obstacle.' He discusses the grammatical construction, noting that the sharh (commentary) treats the prepositional phrase as related to an omitted adjective, but he prefers to treat it as a circumstantial accusative (hal) of the subject of 'qul' (say). The meaning is that the supplicant should utter this plea while in a state of abasement.

Then al-Sawi explains that the phrase 'ind al-munakisirati qulubuhum' (with those whose hearts are broken) is a cause for the supplication and quotes a sacred hadith confirming that God is with such people.

Next, he clarifies the term 'qati'' (obstacle) as any 'fitnah' (trial) that occupies the heart from worship, and he specifies that such trials include love of wealth, children, status, and desires. He provides three Qur'anic verses as evidence: Surah al-Taghabun (64:15), Surah Al Imran (3:14), and Surah al-Munafiqun (63:9). Each verse highlights how worldly attachments can divert one from God. The footnotes give the exact surah and verse numbers.

Thus, the passage defines the first category of obstacles (qawati') as worldly attachments and establishes scriptural proof that they are indeed trials that cut one off from God.

قاطع (qati')
فتنة (fitnah)
المنكسرة قلوبهم (al-munakisirat qulubuhum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the supplication is merely a ritual phrase.
Correction: It is a plea from a heart conscious of its lowliness and the danger of worldly trials.

Key Takeaway
The supplication asks for protection from obstacles that cut the heart off from Allah; these obstacles are worldly attachments, as proven by the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Allah promises to be with those whose hearts are broken because of Him. Are you willing to let go of the pride that prevents you from being one of them? What would that cost you?

Higher Obstacles: From Pride to Mercenary Worship
Al-Sawi lists additional obstacles and explains the correct intention in worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why worshiping for reward or spiritual openings can be a subtle obstacle, and that the correct intention of the awliya' is to worship Allah for His own essence.

Core Idea
Worship Allah for His essence, not for gain.

Al-Sawi continues listing obstacles that cut one off from God. Beyond obvious vices like pride and hypocrisy, he identifies a more subtle barrier: worshiping for reward or spiritual openings. True worship, he explains, is for God's essence alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of obstacles
Read the first part listing kibr, hiqd, riya', and 'ujb. Notice these are internal vices that can cut one off from God.
These are the 'lower' obstacles compared to the subtle one that follows.
2
Understand the subtle obstacle
Read the part about worship for reward or spiritual opening (fath). Note that even seeking spiritual stations can be an obstacle if it becomes the motive.
3
Learn the correct intention
Read the explanation that the awliya' worship Allah for His essence. The commentary includes the hadith Qudsi: 'If I had not created Paradise or Hell, would I not deserve to be worshipped?'
This is the core teaching of the passage.
4
Grasp the servant's position
Understand that the servant has no right over God; openings are bounty, deprivations are justice. God is not obliged to grant divine gnoses.
5
Clarify the term 'bad servants'
Note that being called a 'bad servant' does not mean sin but a lower spiritual rank. It is a decline from high stations.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن القواطع : الكِبْرُ والحقد والرِّياء والعُجب، ومنها: العبادة لأجل حصول ثواب، أو حصول فَتْح لدنّي ليكون م أولياء الله، وإنَّما شأنهم أن يعبدوا الله تعالى لذاته وامتثالاً لأمره ونهيه، ثم إن حصل لهم فتح فذلك من فضله وإن حجبوا فذلك من عدله، إذ ليس للعبد على مولاه حقٌّ ، وإِنَّما الحقُّ له تعالى على العبد، فالعبد مطلوب بأن يخلّص نفسه من الرُّعونات النَّفسيَّة، وليس على الله تعالى أن يهبه المعارف القدسيّة، والذي يعبده لذلك معدود عندهم من عبيد السُّوء
قوله: (ومنها : العبادة ... إلخ) أي من جملة القواطع عن الله تعالى .
قوله : (وإنَّما شأنهم أن يعبدوا الله تعالى لذاته) : أي : لكونه مستحقاً وأهلاً للعبادة ورد في مناجاة داود : «يا داود؛ إن لم أخلق جنَّة ولا ناراً، أفلا أستحقُ أن أعبد ؟ » (۱) .
قوله : (إذ ليس للعبد على مولاه حقٌّ أي وأما قوله تعالى: كتب رَبُّكُمْ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ الرَّحْمَةُ ) (۲) ؛ فمعناه على سبيل التَّفضُّل والإحسان .
قوله : (من عبيد السُّوء): ليس المراد أنَّ ذلك حرام يعاقب عليه، بل المراد أنَّ ذلك انحطاط عن المراتب العليَّة .
(۱) ينظر قوت القلوب (٩٢/٢ .
(۲) سورة الأنعام: (٥٤).
And among the obstacles are: pride, rancor, hypocrisy, and self-conceit. And among them is: worship for the sake of attaining reward, or attaining a spiritual opening (fath) so that he may be among the friends of Allah. But their proper way is that they worship Allah Most High for His own essence and in obedience to His command and prohibition. Then if an opening comes to them, that is from His bounty; and if they are withheld, that is from His justice. For the servant has no right over his Master; rather, the right belongs to Him Most High over the servant. So the servant is required to purify himself from psychological follies, and it is not incumbent upon Allah Most High to bestow upon him divine gnoses. And whoever worships Him for that reason is counted among them as a bad servant.
His statement: ('And among them is worship... etc.') i.e., it is among the obstacles that cut off from Allah Most High.
His statement: ('But their proper way is that they worship Allah Most High for His own essence') i.e., because He is worthy and deserving of worship. It is related in the intimate conversation of David: 'O David, if I had not created Paradise or Hell, would I not deserve to be worshiped?' (1).
His statement: ('For the servant has no right over his Master') i.e., as for the saying of the Most High: 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself' (2), its meaning is by way of favor and benevolence.
His statement: ('bad servants'): It does not mean that this is forbidden and punishable; rather, it means that it is a decline from the high stations.
(1) See Qut al-Qulub (2/92).
(2) Surah al-An'am: 54.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is an additional enumeration of obstacles (qawati') that cut one off from God. The commentator lists internal vices: pride (kibr), rancor (hiqd), hypocrisy (riya'), and self-conceit ('ujb). Then he discusses a more subtle obstacle: worshiping for the sake of obtaining reward or a spiritual opening (fath dunyawi) in order to become among the friends (awliya') of God. He explains that the true state of the awliya' is to worship God for His own essence (li-dhatihi) and out of obedience to His commands, not for any worldly or otherworldly gain. If an opening occurs, it is from God's favor; if they are deprived, it is from His justice, because the servant has no claim upon God. The servant's only duty is to purify himself from base traits. God is not obliged to grant esoteric knowledge (ma'arif qudsiyya).

Then al-Sawi clarifies the quoted phrases. For 'worship for His own essence', he explains it means worship because God is intrinsically deserving. He supports this with the narration from David's munajat (intimate conversation) where God says: even if He had not created Paradise or Hell, He still deserves worship. Regarding the statement that the servant has no right over his Master, al-Sawi addresses a potential objection from the verse 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself' (Qur'an 6:54), explaining that this mercy is a favor (tafaddul) and not an obligation. Finally, he clarifies that calling such worshipers 'bad servants' (abid al-su') does not mean their worship is sinful or punishable, but that it is a lower spiritual station, beneath the high rank of sincere worship.

Thus, this passage moves from external worldly attachments to internal vices and then to the subtlest obstacle: a mercenary attitude in worship.

كبْر (kibr)
حقد (hiqd)
رياء (riya')
عُجب ('ujb)
عبادة لأجل ثواب (ibada li-ajl thawab)
فتح (fath)
معارف قدسية (ma'arif qudsiyya)
عبيد السوء (abid al-su')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Worshiping for Paradise or to avoid Hell is forbidden or always wrong.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that worshiping for reward is not sinful; it is merely a lower station. True seekers aim to worship Allah for His essence, not for gain.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, why is worshiping to obtain a divine opening (fath) considered an obstacle?
A) It is sinful and forbidden
B) It indicates that the worshiper is a bad person
C) The true way is to worship God for His own essence, not for gain
D) It shows ingratitude for God's mercy

Key Takeaway
Worship Allah for His essence, and accept any opening or withholding as His bounty or justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform your daily prayers, do you ever catch yourself hoping for a reward or fearing punishment? How can you train your heart to worship Allah purely for His essence, even if no reward came?
?
If Allah withheld all spiritual feelings, openings, and signs of acceptance from your worship, would you continue with the same devotion? Why or why not?
?
Are you willing to be among the 'bad servants' in the eyes of the lofty seekers—those who worship for lower motives—or do you truly desire to worship for Allah alone? What is one concrete change you can make today to elevate your intention?
